

2014 WL 4407961 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAUL RUBEN OCHOA-BENITEZ A.K.A. SAUL OCHOA A.K.A. SAUL R. OCHOA
File: AXXX XX8 147 - New York, New York
August 14, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Julie A. Goldberg, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s June 26, 2013, decision which denied his motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgment and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of her own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C).
 
The respondent was pro se before the Immigration Court. He received personal service of the Notice to Appear on February 15, 2006, which bears his signature (Exh. 1). A hearing notice dated March 16, 2006, which scheduled a hearing for June 1, 2006, was mailed to him (Exh. 2). Following a hearing conducted in absentia on June 1, 2006, at which the respondent failed to appear, and the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) presented evidence, an Immigration Judge found him subject to removal as charged, determined that he had abandoned any pending applications and any applications that he was then eligible to file, and ordered him removed from the United States. See generally section 240 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a.
 
The respondent went to the Immigration Court 2 days later to check on the status of his case, was detained by DHS-ICE when he went to the ICE office, and was removed to Mexico. He reentered the United States in 2007 without authorization. On May 24, 2013, almost 7 years after he had been ordered removed in absentia and had been removed to Mexico, the respondent, through counsel, filed a motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. He claimed that his daughter had suffered a cut lip the day before the June 1, 2006, hearing, that he told his counsel that he would be unable to appear at his hearing the next day, and that counsel informed him that he would file a motion to reopen and that his failure to appear would be excused if he provided his daughter’s medical records. After he was removed to Mexico, the respondent said he contacted counsel who informed him that the motion to reopen had been denied. The respondent claimed that his alleged former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to appear on his behalf on June 1, 2006, and for failing to file a motion to reopen, and that he was unable to file a bar complaint against him because he has been disbarred.
 
*2 The DHS filed a response in opposition to the motion to reopen, noting that the respondent was never formally represented by an attorney because the record of proceeding did not contain a Notice of Entry of Appearance.
 
On June 26, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen. We affirm the denial of the motion. The respondent is precluded from claiming that ineffective assistance of counsel excused his failure to appear because he was not represented by an attorney before the Immigration Court. Although the DHS pointed out that he was not represented by an attorney in its response to the motion, he continues to allege on appeal ineffective assistance of counsel and does not respond to the record not reflecting that he was represented by an attorney in 2006.
 
The respondent has not established that his absence at the scheduled hearing was the result of lack of notice because he concedes that he received the notice for the June 1, 2006, hearing. Therefore, he has not established that he merits reopening under the standards to reopen in absentia removal orders. In addition, he has not indicated what steps, if any, he initiated to resolve his unlawful immigration status after he reentered the United States without authorization in 2007, and why he waited until 2013 to file a motion to reopen.
 
To the extent that the respondent argues on appeal that he is eligible for prosecutorial discretion, we note that only the DHS, and not the Board, has the jurisdiction to consider such a request. Although he requests reopening to apply for voluntary departure, the Immigration Judge’s in absentia removal order reflects that he waived any form of relief for which he was eligible in 2006 because of his failure to appear.
 
Because we have decided the appeal on the preceding basis, it is not necessary to address his remaining arguments. Accordingly the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 4407961 (BIA)
End of Document

2014 WL 4407757 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALAN CANTU-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX9 579 - Harlingen, TX
August 11, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kim Alabasi, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Daniel Gilbert
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s March 6, 2014, decision denying his motion to reopen removal proceedings which were conducted in absentia on May 29, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal and, in the alternative, asks that we deem the respondent’s appeal to be withdrawn.1 Assuming for the purpose of appeal, that the respondent’s appeal was not withdrawn, we will nonetheless dismiss the appeal.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision with the following notations. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
A Notice to Appear (NTA) was personally served on the respondent on January 20, 2012 (Exh. 1). The respondent’s former counsel submitted a Notice of Entry of Appearance (Form EOIR-28) for the respondent on January 27, 2012 (I.J. at 1). A notice of hearing for May 29, 2012, was mailed to the respondent’s attorney at the address provided on the Form EOIR-28. On May 29, 2012, an attorney from the same law firm as the respondent’s attorney appeared, but the respondent did not (I.J. at 1-2). The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia. On February 12, 2014, the respondent filed a motion to rescind his in absentia removal order on the basis that he lacked notice and received ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge denied the motion and the respondent appealed.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent was afforded proper notice of his hearing on May 29, 2012, through his counsel of record (I.J. at 2). See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (recognizing that notice to the attorney of record constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5 (notice of hearing sent to an alien’s attorney of record is sufficient notice).
 
*2 We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “““exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order. See Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996). However, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent cannot seek rescission of the in absentia order based on “exceptional circumstances,” because his motion to reopen was filed more than 180 days after the in absentia order of removal was entered (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
Alternatively, we also agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established that his failure to appear at removal proceedings was due to ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 4). The respondent claims that either his attorney or a paralegal informed him that his hearing was not scheduled and he did not need to attend his hearing (Resp. Motion at 2; Resp. Br. at 4; Resp. Br. at Affidavit). While ineffective assistance of counsel may constitute an exceptional circumstance justifying the rescission of an in absentia removal order, the respondent must demonstrate that he complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent did not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra (I.J. at 2; Resp. Motion). The Immigration Judge properly denied the motion to reopen based on the lack of evidence.
 
On appeal, the respondent cites to a Ninth Circuit case, Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934 (9th Cir. 2003), for the proposition that strict adherence to Matter of Lozada, supra, is not required (Resp. Br. at 4). However, the Ninth Circuit has also held that the types of cases in which strict adherence to Matter of Lozada, supra, is not required are in those instances “when the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.” See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002); see also Tamang v. Holder, 598 F.3d 1083, 1090 (9th Cir. 2010) (holding that strict compliance with Lozada was necessary because the ineffectiveness of the former counsel was not “plain on its face”). The record here does not show a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance. This case would benefit from strict compliance with Matter of Lozada, and we find no basis to disturb the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Rodriguez-Manzano v. Holder, 666 F.3d 948, 953 (5th Cir. 2012) (rejecting arguments for a flexible application of Lozada).
 
*3 Lastly, the Immigration Judge concluded, and we agree, that sua sponte reopening is not warranted in the respondent’s case. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s motion for a stay of removal was denied by this Board on March 20, 2014, and the respondent was physically removed on March 26, 2014. The DHS argues that that the respondent’s departure constitutes withdrawal of appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.4; Long v. Gonzales, 420 F.3d 516, 520 (5th Cir. 2005). As we decide this case on other grounds, we will not address this argument further.

2014 WL 4259397 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANTONIO MUNOZ-SALINAS
File: AXXX XX7 743 - Portland, OR
July 31, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Manuel Rios, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sarah C. Lara
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s March 18, 2014, denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen (I.J. at 1-4). The Immigration Judge correctly concluded that the respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely, as it was filed in 2014, over 12 years after he was ordered removed in 2001.1 See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (“A motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal”); see also Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 679 (9th Cir. 2011).
 
Further, the respondent has not shown that he is entitled to equitable tolling because there is no evidence that he was prevented from timely filing his motion by “deception, fraud, or error.” See Avagyan v. Holder, supra; Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003). On appeal, the respondent asserts that his removal order was based upon a miscarriage of justice, because the Immigration Judge did not advise him of his right to apply for pre-conclusion voluntary departure, and also improperly sustained the charge of removability under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I).2 See Resp. Br. at 6-18. We note, however, that these are not allegations of “deception, fraud, or error” that prevented the respondent from filing a timely motion to reopen. Rather, these are attacks on the underlying removal order, which has no bearing as to whether the respondent warrants equitable tolling for the significant time that passed before he filed his motion to reopen.
 
Further, the respondent has not shown that he acted with “due” or “““reasonable” diligence in discovering any “deception, fraud, or error” that prevented him from timely filing his motion to reopen. See Avagyan v. Holder, supra, at 679 (aliens relying on equitable tolling must demonstrate, inter alia, that they exercised due diligence during the period sought to be tolled); Iturribarria v. INS, supra, at 897. Nor has he presented any explanation as to why he waited until 2014 to file his motion to reopen, or otherwise claimed ignorance of law and that such ignorance was caused by circumstances beyond his control. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001) (in order for equitable tolling to apply, respondent needs to show that his ignorance was caused by circumstances beyond his control, and which go beyond a claim of excusable neglect).
 
*2 Moreover, we adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not demonstrate that his underlying removal proceedings were fundamentally unfair or otherwise resulted in a gross miscarriage of justice, such that granting the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen would be warranted (I.J. at 2-4). See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
In particular, the respondent does not dispute on appeal that he was represented by counsel at the time he was asked by the Immigration Judge during his removal proceedings whether he would be filing any application for relief, and that counsel answered in the negative (I.J. at 3). The cases relied upon the respondent to support his contention that not being specifically advised that he could seek voluntary departure deprived him of a full and fair hearing are factually distinguishable from the matter at hand. See Resp. Br. at 8. Unlike in those cases, the respondent was both represented by counsel and was given the opportunity to apply for relief from removal, but chose not to seek any relief; nor was he discouraged by the Immigration Judge from seeking any particular form of relief. The respondent has raised no allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Further, the respondent has not demonstrated that any failure by the Immigration Judge to advise him that he could apply for pre-conclusion voluntary departure prejudiced him, as he has not presented any evidence with his motion to reopen demonstrating that he would have warranted such relief as a matter of discretion. See Matter of Arguelles, 22 I&N Dec. 811, 817 (BIA 1999) (“discretion remains a required element of [pre-conclusion] voluntary departure”); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i); see also Munoz v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 950, 954 (9th Cir. 2003) (no due process right lies in discretionary relief from removal).
 
Finally, insofar as the respondent seeks reopening to contest his removability under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act, as having been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude, we note that he had the opportunity, but waived his right to appeal the removal order. See United States v. Melendez-Castro, 671 F.3d 950, 954 (9th Cir. 2012) (an alien is barred from collaterally attacking his underlying removal order if he validly waived his right to appeal that order). Further, the respondent does not dispute that, through counsel, he had conceded the second charge of removability under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act. Additionally, the respondent has presented no new and previously unavailable evidence demonstrating that he was not removable under the crime involving moral turpitude charge or showing that the outcome of his proceedings would have been different had the moral turpitude charge not have been sustained. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1992).
 
*3 The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent had waived his right to appeal the order of removal entered against him.

2

The respondent conceded the second charge of removability under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act (I.J. at 1).

2014 WL 4259175 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WENDY KARINA ABULARACH-GALVEZ
File: AXXX XX5 248 - Omaha, NE
July 29, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Asma Bano Din, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anna L. Speas
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s order dated December 31, 2013, denying her motion to reopen her proceedings. The request for a waiver of the appeal fee is granted. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the determination of credibility. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues de novo, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was ordered removed in absentia when she failed to appear for her removal hearing. She filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Court arguing that she did not receive notice of her hearing. The record reflects that the hearing notice was mailed to the respondent at the address she provided and was returned as undeliverable after her hearing. The respondent contends that she provided notice of her change of address, but does not state with whom she filed such a change and the record does not include a change of address. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. On appeal, the respondent states only her “attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel and this ineffective assistance resulted in Motion to Reopen being denied.” (Notice of Appeal, Form EOIR-26).
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). The respondent does not contest the Immigration Judge’s finding that she failed to update the Immigration Court with a new address (I.J. Dec. at 3). See section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(B) (eliminating the notice requirement where the alien fails to provide contact information); see also Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674-76 (BIA 2008) (stating that an alien must comply with the statutory responsibility to provide current address information). The respondent’s assertion of ineffective assistance of counsel is not supported by any facts nor has the respondent complied with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1998). We therefore find no reason to disturb the decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen the in absentia hearing. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 4259175 (BIA)
End of Document

2014 WL 4259178 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DANIEL UCHENNA ONYEMA
File: AXXX XX8 168 - Denver, Co
July 29, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Imdad Seehar, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
P. Michael Truman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)] - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)] - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)] - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)] - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)] - Conditional resident status terminated
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s April 16, 2013, decision denying his motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. During the pendency of his appeal, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge which will be considered by the Board as a motion to remand. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) (2014). The appeal will be sustained, the proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
The record reflects that through prior counsel the respondent conceded removability under section 237(a)(1)(D)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i), but denied removability under both charges lodged under section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Act. However, during his proceedings on October 26, 2012, the respondent, through the same counsel, conceded all factual allegations and charges in the Notice to Appear in exchange for pre-conclusion voluntary departure based on a deal with the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) (I.J. at 3). The respondent was granted 120 days voluntary departure and, as required, waived appeal (I.J. at 3). Subsequently, on February 19, 2013, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge alleging ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 4). The DHS opposed the motion.
 
On April 16, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion (I.J. at 5-6). Specifically, the Immigration Judge found that, while the respondent had complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), he had not shown sufficient due diligence in seeking redress for purposes of equitably tolling the motion’s deadline which had long since passed (I.J. at 5-6). See Riley v. INS, 310 F.3d 1253 (10th Cir. 2002). In this regard, the Immigration Judge noted that, while the evidence showed the respondent knew of his former counsels’ actions upon which he based his claim of ineffectiveness as early as the day after the October 26, 2012, hearing, he had not sought new counsel until almost 4 months later (I.J. at 5). Further, the Immigration Judge found that, even if the respondent had used due diligence, he had not established prejudice because he did not dispute the concessions and admissions he made through prior counsel (I.J. at 6). See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent appealed that decision.
 
*2 Given that there is no dispute the respondent complied with the technical requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, we find that the Immigration Judge erred in finding the respondent did not exercise due diligence in seeking redress for his claims of ineffectiveness and in finding the respondent did not establish prejudice (I.J. at 5-6). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (de novo review). The record reflects that the respondent contacted the Immigration Judge the day after his proceedings on October 26, 2012, notifying her of his grievances against his former counsel. In his letter, the respondent indicated to the Immigration Judge that there was some coercion in his acceptance of the deal with DHS to concede all charges, waive his rights to file for relief and to appeal, and to accept pre-conclusion voluntary departure. Although the respondent expressed his unwillingness to make the deal and possible “threats” of arrest communicated to him by his prior counsel, the Immigration Judge did not take any action at that time.
 
Further, contrary to the Immigration Judge’s decision, the evidence submitted by the respondent with his motion reflects that he sought new counsel as early as November 1, 2012, when he signed a contract with another law firm (I.J. at 5). According to this evidence, the new firm was notified of the respondent’s claims but did not take any action and the contract was terminated by the respondent in January 2013 when he sought current counsel. The respondent’s motion was filed less than a month later. Based on his efforts to obtain new counsel within a few days of his hearing, we cannot agree that the respondent did not exercise due diligence (I.J. at 5-6). In addition, the fact that the respondent was not permitted to apply for any relief from removal, such as obtaining a de novo review of his Form I-751, by virtue of his acceptance of pre-conclusion voluntary departure establishes the requisite prejudice for his ineffective assistance of counsel claim (I.J. at 6). See Matter of Assaad, supra. Based on the circumstances of this case, in particular the Immigration Judge’s decision not to act upon the respondent’s October 27, 2012, letter; the respondent’s actions in seeking new counsel within a few days of his October 26, 2012, hearing; and, the inability of the respondent to file for any type of relief from removal, we find that reopening is warranted.1
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be sustained, the proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
*3 Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Board Member Roger A. Pauley respectfully dissents without opinion.
 
Footnotes

1

Because the appeal is otherwise sustained, we find the respondent’s motion to remand is moot.

2014 WL 4259159 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KAMIL CWALINSKI
File: AXXX XX1 455 - Batavia, NY
July 25, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George A. Terezakis, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony (as defined in section 101(a)(43)(F))
 
237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony (as defined in section 101(a)(43)(U))
 
237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony (as defined in section 101(a)(43)(G))
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s March 5, 2014, decision ordering him removed from the United States. The appeal will be dismissed. We also deny the respondent’s request for a three Board Member review of his case, as this case does not fall under any of the categories warranting such review. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(6).
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including credibility determinations, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Poland and a lawful permanent resident of the United States (I.J. at 2-3; Exh. 1). On November 7, 2013, the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) issued a Notice to Appear (Form I-862) (“NTA”) alleging that the respondent was convicted of the offense of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree, to wit: oxycodone, in violation of New York State Penal Law (“NYPL”) § 220.03 on August 21, 2013 (I.J. at 2-3; Exhs. 1, 2C). The NTA also alleges that the respondent was convicted of the offense of attempted robbery in the second degree in violation of NYPL § 110-160.10(2)(b) on September 13, 2012, and was sentenced to a term of imprisonment of at least one year (I.J. at 3; Exhs. 1, 2D).
 
The NTA charges the respondent with removability pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony within the meaning of sections 101(a)(43)(F), (G), and (U) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1101(a)(43)(F), (G), and (U). Under sections 101(a)(43)(F) and (G) of the Act, respectively, a “““crime of violence” and “theft offense” “for which the term of imprisonment [is] at least one year” are aggravated felonies. Section 101(a)(43)(U) of the Act provides that an “attempt or conspiracy to commit an offense described in this paragraph” is an aggravated felony. The NTA also charges the respondent with removability under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation.
 
*2 The respondent denied the allegations pertaining to his criminal history and denied all of the charges of removability (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 14; Exh. 1). The Immigration Judge sustained the allegations and found the respondent removable on all charges (I.J. at 4-6). The Immigration Judge also denied the respondent’s request for a continuance (I.J. at 7-8; Tr. at 33-34). The respondent did not file any applications for relief (I.J. at 7-8; Tr. at 32).
 
The respondent does not directly challenge his removability as an aggravated felon on appeal. Instead, he argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for a continuance to seek post-conviction relief in State criminal court for his attempted robbery conviction (Resp. Br. at 7-14). The respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel and due process violations in that he allegedly was not informed of the possible immigration consequences of his conviction, citing Padilla v. Kentucky, 599 U.S. 356 (2010), and People v. Peque, 22 N.Y.3d 168 (2013) (Resp. Br. at 3, 7-9, 12). Although he conceded that his conviction was not on direct appeal at the time of his hearing, he also sought a continuance to potentially file late notice of direct appeal (I.J. at 7; Tr. at 24-26, 34).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to continue (I.J. at 7-8). An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. The decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the Immigration Judge’s discretion and will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing. Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987). The alien must show that actual prejudice materially affecting the outcome of the case resulted from the denial of the continuance. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). The pursuit of post-conviction relief is a collateral attack, and as such, does not impair the validity of his conviction for immigration purposes. See Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The respondent has not demonstrated that he suffered prejudice as a result of the denial of the continuance. He has not presented evidence on appeal that his conviction has been vacated or materially modified in any way by the appropriate court. The respondent’s speculation that his conviction may be invalid does not change the finality of the conviction for immigration purposes, unless and until it has been overturned by a criminal court. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996).
 
*3 The respondent’s reliance on Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009), and Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009), is misplaced.1 Those cases address the factors an Immigration Judge should consider in deciding whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings to await the adjudication of pending employment-based or family-based visa petitions or labor certifications, not whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings to seek post-conviction relief (Resp. Br. at 9-10, 12). The Immigration Judge properly concluded that the respondent did not establish good cause for a continuance.
 
In light of our disposition, and the Immigration Judge’s unchallenged determination that the respondent is removable on three aggravated felony charges, we need not reach the respondent’s argument on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that he was convicted of a controlled substance offense pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act. Lastly, we deny the respondent’s request for us to defer adjudication of his appeal for another 60 or 90 days to afford him additional opportunity to seek post-conviction relief (Resp. Br. at 4-5, 12, 13-14).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent actually cited Rajah v. Mukasey, 544 F.3d 449 (2d Cir. 2008). In that case, the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit remanded, directing the Board to set “standards that reflect various situations of those seeking such continuances,” id. at 450, which the Board did in Matter of Rajah, supra.

2014 WL 4259167 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAZIN KAYED ABUJEISH
File: AXXX XX2 987 - Oklahoma City, OK
July 23, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Timothy Lee Cook, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jordan, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 21, 2013. The Immigration Judge denied as abandoned the respondent’s application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent failed to timely file his application for relief. The appeal will be dismissed in part and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record reflects that at a hearing on April 21, 2011, the respondent, through prior counsel, Ms. Melinda Alizadeh-Fard, admitted the factual allegations contained in the Notice to Appear (Form I-862), including the allegation that the respondent was admitted as a nonimmigrant spouse of a United States citizen with authorization to remain in the United States for a temporary period not to exceed August 31, 2008, and that he remained in the United States beyond that date without authorization (I.J. at 1; Tr. at 5; Exh. 1). The respondent, through Ms. Alizadeh-Fard, conceded removability as charged under section 237(a)(1)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B), as an alien who, after admission, remained in the United States for a time longer than permitted. Also at the April 21, 2011, hearing, the respondent, through Ms. Alizadeh Fard, indicated that he wished to pursue adjustment of status based on his marriage to a United States citizen, to whom he had been married for three years at the time of the hearing (Tr. at 7).
 
At the next hearing on September 27, 2011, the respondent appeared with Ms. Alizadeh-Fard, and informed the Immigration Judge that the I-130 petition filed on his behalf by his current spouse had been approved. The Immigration Judge continued the case until March 22, 2013, and ordered the respondent to file his application for adjustment of status by June 1, 2012 (Tr. at 19-20). The Immigration Judge informed the respondent, through Ms. Alizadeh-Fard, that if his application was not filed by the deadline set, he would deem the application abandoned and order the respondent removed (Tr. at 20). Ms. Alizadeh-Fard indicated that she understood and agreed to those consequences (Tr. at 20). The respondent did not thereafter file his application by the date set by the Immigration Judge, and accordingly, the Immigration Judge issued a decision deeming his application for relief abandoned and ordering the respondent removed.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent asserts that Ms. Alizadeh-Fard provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to file his adjustment of status application in a timely manner. The respondent also asserts on appeal that he is eligible for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b). In support of the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim, the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition, the record contains an undated letter from Ms. Alizadeh-Fard to the Immigration Court in Dallas, indicating that, although she was at the September 27, 2011, hearing, she was not aware of the June 1, 2012, deadline for filing the respondent’s adjustment of status application. The record does not support this statement inasmuch as Ms. Alizadeh-Ford, as previously noted, acknowledged the June 1, 2012, deadline at the September 27, 2011, hearing, and indicated that she understood the consequences of not meeting that deadline (Tr. at 20). We also note that the record does not contain a Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before the Immigration Court (Form EOIR-28) from Ms. Alizadeh-Ford before the Immigration Judge so as to establish that she was qualified to speak on behalf of the respondent.
 
With respect to the respondent’s application for adjustment of status, we conclude that, notwithstanding any potential ineffective assistance provided by Ms. Alizadeh-Fard, the respondent cannot establish that he suffered prejudice on account of such ineffective assistance. See Alzainati v. Holder, 568 F.3d 844, 851 (10th Cir. 2009) (requiring a showing of prejudice to support a due process violation claim). Specifically, the respondent cannot establish statutory eligibility for adjustment of status under section 245(a) based on his current marriage inasmuch as he entered as a K-3 nonimmigrant based on his marriage to another United States citizen. We have held that section 245(d) of the Act bars K visa holders from adjusting to permanent resident status on any basis other than the marriage to the citizen who petitioned on their behalf. See Matter of Valenzuela, 25 I&N Dec. 867, 869 (BIA 2012). The record does not contain evidence that the respondent was admitted to the United States in a manner other than as a K-3 nonimmigrant, and therefore he does not appear to be eligible to adjust his status through his current marriage.
 
However, based on the totality of the evidence before us, we will remand the record to the Immigration Judge to provide the respondent with an opportunity to apply for voluntary departure, or any other relief for which he may be eligible. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2014 WL 4259409 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE RAMON PALA-DUY
File: AXXX XX8 775 - San Antonio, TX
July 22, 2014
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marcia Kasdan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Grace Garza
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of April 16, 2013.1 In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered deported in absentia on January 9, 1996. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts.
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent alleges he did not appear because immigration officials recorded his address incorrectly as “New York” rather than “Jamaica.” However, as noted by the Immigration Judge, the notice of hearing (NOH) for the January 9, 1996, hearing, was properly served on the respondent’s counsel (Exh. 2). On December 22, 1995, counsel filed a motion to withdraw, informing the Immigration Judge that the respondent had been notified of his hearing, and noting that the respondent had failed to maintain reasonable communication with counsel (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge granted the motion to withdraw on January 9, 1996, and counsel was served with the in absentia order.
 
In denying the motion to reopen, the Immigration Judge correctly found that notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent (I.J. at 2). See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 292.5(a). On appeal, the respondent has not addressed the Immigration Judge’s finding that notice on counsel was proper.
 
On appeal, the respondent again alleges he did not receive notice or the in absentia order. We note that the January 9, 1996, in absentia order was properly served on his former counsel. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. Here, however, the respondent has not alleged ineffective assistance by former counsel; nor has the respondent fulfilled any of the Lozada criteria. Consequently, the respondent has failed to perfect a claim of ineffective assistance of his counsel, and there is no basis to reopen under “exceptional circumstances.”
 
*2 The respondent’s motion, to the extent that it seeks new relief in the form of adjustment of status, was filed more than 90 days after he was ordered deported from the United States in absentia. His request for reopening to that extent is therefore untimely. See Matter of Monges, 25 I&N Dec. 246 (BIA 2010); Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The decision was not mailed to the respondent until April 25, 2013.

2014 WL 4259419 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AAMIR ABBAS A.K.A. AAMIR ABBASH
File: AXXX XX1 911 - Chicago, IL
July 21, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew Kriezelman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Seth B. Fitter
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on May 14, 2013, when we reconsidered, pursuant to our sua sponte authority, our denial of the respondent’s first motion to reopen and again denied reopening. On April 16, 2014, the respondent, a native and citizen of Pakistan, filed a second motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. In the motion, he asked that his case be remanded so that he may pursue adjustment of status on the basis of an approved visa petition filed by his United States citizen wife. On May 6, 2014, the respondent filed a request for a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes both the respondent’s motion to reopen and his request for a stay of removal. The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A), states that an alien may file one motion to reopen. The respondent’s current motion, however, is his second motion to reopen. The respondent’s motion therefore is numerically barred.
 
Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, in turn, states that motions to reopen “““shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal.”1 We entered the final administrative order of removal in the respondent’s case on November 21, 2012. The respondent did not file his current motion until more than a year later. The respondent’s motion to reopen therefore is untimely.
 
The respondent claims that his motion is exempt from the time and numeric limits because it is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit, the circuit in which the respondent’s case arises, has held that the 90-day deadline for filing a motion to reopen is a statute of limitations and therefore is subject to equitable tolling. See Gaberov v. Mukasey, 516 F.3d 590, 594 (7th Cir. 2008). To establish that a situation, like being a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, warrants equitable tolling, an alien must establish that he or she acted with due diligence during the period he or she is seeking to have tolled. Id. Specifically, in this case, the respondent must establish that he could not reasonably have been expected to file his motion earlier. See El-Gazawy v. Holder, 690 F.3d 852, 850 (7th Cir. 2012). The respondent has not met this burden.
 
*2 The respondent claims that he did not discover the ineffective assistance of his prior attorney until he contacted his current attorney about the petition for review that his prior attorney had filed with the Seventh Circuit. The respondent, however, has not explained why our May 14, 2013, decision in his case did not put him on notice of his prior attorney’s alleged failure to inform him that he needed to submit an application for adjustment of status with his motion to reopen to pursue adjustment.2 The respondent also has not provided specific details, such as the date he contacted his current attorney or the date he tried to determine the status of his petition for review. In light of these facts, we cannot conclude that the respondent has met his burden of establishing that he could not reasonably have been expected to file his motion to reopen sooner.
 
In addition, the respondent has not satisfied the procedural requirements necessary to obtain reopening on the basis of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and he has not provided sufficient information to establish that he was, in fact, the victim of ineffective assistance. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Marinov v. Holder, 687 F.3d 365, 368-69 (7th Cir. 2012) (confirming validity of Lozada requirements). To obtain reopening on the basis of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must submit an affidavit detailing the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what former counsel did or did not represent to the respondent in this regard. Id at 639. The respondent, however, has provided little, if any, information regarding his agreement with his former attorney. Further, the respondent appears to have stated incorrectly that his petition for review was dismissed due to the inaction of his former counsel. The Seventh Circuit records do indicate that the court ordered the respondent to show cause why the case should not be dismissed for lack of prosecution, but the records also indicate that the respondent’s case was dismissed pursuant to a motion submitted by his current counsel rather than due to the inaction of his former counsel.3 See Abbas v. Holder, No. 13-2283 (7th Cir. June 3, 2014). Without more information, we cannot conclude that the respondent has satisfied the first requirement of Matter of Lozada, supra, or that he has established that the alleged errors of his former attorney, in fact, constitute ineffective assistance.
 
The respondent also has not satisfied the second Lozada requirement. In particular, he has not established that he has notified his former counsel of the charges against him. The respondent has submitted a letter directed to his former attorney, but he has not submitted sufficient evidence to establish that this letter was mailed or that his former counsel had adequate notice of the charges and an opportunity to respond.
 
*3 Finally, the respondent has not met his burden of establishing that he was prejudiced by his former attorney’s conduct. See El-Gazawy v. Holder, supra, at 860 (stating that establishing prejudice means showing that errors actually had the potential for affecting the outcome of the proceeding). As we noted above, the respondent’s petition for review was not dismissed due to the neglect or inaction of his former attorney. In addition, the respondent has not established that he was the beneficiary of an approved visa petition when we reconsidered and again denied his motion to reopen to pursue adjustment, and the respondent has not established that he warranted a grant of reopening at that time in light of the other factors in his case. See section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a) (listing statutory requirements for adjustment of status); see also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471-73 (BIA 1992) (indicating that an alien seeking reopening for further consideration of an application for relief bears a “heavy burden” and must present evidence of such a nature that the Board is satisfied that, if proceedings are reopened, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case).
 
Based on the foregoing, the respondent has not met his burden of establishing that the filing deadline or numeric limitations on motions to reopen should be equitably tolled in his case. Further, he has not established that reopening is warranted pursuant to our sua sponte authority. See, e.g., Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We therefore deny the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely and numerically barred.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In referring to the motions deadline, the respondent incorrectly cites the deadline for filing a motion to rescind an in absentia order. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). The respondent was not ordered removed in absentia. Accordingly, this deadline is not applicable to him. The deadline contained in section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act applies to his motion.

2

The respondent alleges that his prior attorney committed several errors including failing to inform him that an adjustment application needed to be included with his motion to reopen, failing to include the application in the motion, not responding to the respondent’s calls or inquiries while the petition for review was pending, and not filing the necessary briefs and responses with the Seventh Circuit. As we note later in our decision, however, the respondent has not provided an affidavit describing the agreement he had with his former counsel regarding representation. The letter notifying the respondent’s former attorney of the charges against him also is only dated 3 days before the respondent filed his current motion, and the respondent has not submitted proof of mailing. It therefore is not clear that the respondent’s former attorney has received sufficient notice of the charges against him. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).

3

We note that the respondent has not provided any information regarding the agreement he reached with his former counsel regarding the petition for review. Without more information on this issue, we do not have a sufficient basis for concluding that the lack of action on the petition for review was due to attorney error rather than to inaction or error on the part of the respondent.

IN RE: JACOB DENEDO
File: AXXX XX6 817 - Bloomington, MN
July 15, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vincent P. Martin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Colin P. Johnson
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent has filed an untimely appeal concerning an Immigration Judge’s order denying a motion to reopen, accompanied by a request to have the Board accept the appeal late. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) filed a “Motion for Summary Dismissal of Untimely Appeal.” The request to have the Board consider the appeal out of time will be denied, and the appeal will be dismissed as untimely.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Nigeria. He was charged with being subject to removal under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), for having been convicted in military court of a crime involving moral turpitude.
 
In an order mailed on July 13, 2011, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen the removal proceedings, which were conducted in absentia. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of the Immigration Judge’s decision. See •8 C.F.R. § 1003.38. The appeal was filed almost three years late, on April 21, 2014.
 
At the time the motion to reopen was filed with the Immigration Judge, the respondent was represented by attorney Bobby Gordon Onyemeh Sea. In 2012, Sea was convicted of filing false federal income tax returns. He was disciplined by the Supreme Court of Minnesota, and has been disbarred from practice by this Board.
 
The respondent claims that Sea “did not contact him again” about his removal case, after he retained Sea’s services in April, 2011. As a result, the respondent claims, the untimely appeal should be accepted despite it being filed years out of time.
 
A party claiming that equitable tolling applies to a deadline in immigration proceedings must show that he acted with reasonable diligence. See Valencia v. Holder, 657 F.3d 745, 748-49 (8th Cir. 2011). The respondent provides no explanation as to why he took no action to contact Sea, and inquire about the status of his case, between 2011, and 2014, when he was detained by immigration authorities.
 
The respondent, moreover, does not meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent did not give former counsel the opportunity to respond to the allegations, and did not indicate that a disciplinary complaint was filed. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639; see also Ortiz-Puentes v. Holder, 662 F.3d 481, 484 (8th Cir. 2011); Valencia v. Holder, supra, at 748.
 
*2 The appeal will, therefore, be dismissed as untimely.
 
ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s order mailed on July 13, 2011, is final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
David B. Holmes

IN RE: TERNA ANDREW ITYONZUGHUL
File: AXXX XX6 822 - Houston, TX
July 14, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carolina Ortuzar-Diaz, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charlotte K. Lang
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s April 12, 2013, order denying his motion to reopen proceedings after he was ordered removed in absentia at a hearing on October 23, 2012. The Board reviews findings of fact under a clearly erroneous standard, while all other issues are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i)-(ii). The record will be remanded for the preparation of a new decision.
 
We find it necessary to remand the record for the preparation of a new decision as we conclude that the Immigration Judge’s April 12, 2013, order does not contain sufficient specificity for adequate review. See generally Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen asserting that exceptional circumstances should excuse his failure to appear for the October 23, 2012, hearing. See generally section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“the Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). The respondent asserted that various factors, including ineffective assistance of counsel, contributed to his failure to appear. In response, the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) filed a brief opposing the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge subsequently denied the respondent’s motion to reopen in a form order citing “the reasons indicated in the [DHS’s] opposition.” We conclude that this order does not contain sufficient findings of fact or conclusions of law for us to adequately review the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. We will therefore remand the record for the entry of a new decision. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for the entry of a new decision.
 
Sharon Hoffman

2014 WL 3889478 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUCERO CRUZ-BAUTISTA A.K.A. GUADALUPE LUCAS-BAUTISTA
File: AXXX XX9 718 - Newark, NJ
July 11, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elizabeth M. Trinidad, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s January 16, 2013, decision denying her motion to reopen removal proceedings.1 The respondent’s request for a waiver of the appellate filing fee is granted; her request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(e)(7), 1003.8(a)(3). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, under the “““clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was placed in removal proceedings following the issuance of a Notice to Appear (Form I-862) on July 15, 2010 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). On September 28, 2010, the respondent attended a hearing conducted by the Immigration Judge and received warnings about the consequences of failing to appear at her following hearing (I.J. at 4; Exh. 3B). On March 29, 2011, the respondent did not appear for her scheduled hearing before the Immigration Court (I.J. at 1). The Immigration Judge conducted the March 29, 2011, hearing in absentia and entered an order of removal in a decision signed on March 31, 2011 (I.J. at 1).2
 
On October 22, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen her removal proceedings, asserting that her failure to appear at the scheduled hearing was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 1-3; Motion to Reopen at 2-3). The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for purposes of her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 4). However, the Immigration Judge denied the motion upon concluding that the respondent had neither demonstrated that her failure to appear was due to “exceptional circumstances” nor that ineffective assistance of counsel prevented her from timely moving to reopen proceedings (I.J. at 3-4).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in considering the documents submitted by her prior counsel and according them greater weight than the evidence she submitted to support her claim of ineffective assistance (Respondent’s Brief at 7-11). The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge did not properly evaluate whether the conduct of her prior counsel, Ms. Cheung, and an associated paralegal, Mr. Hernandez, amounted to ineffective assistance, such that the filing period for her motion to reopen should be equitably tolled (Respondent’s Brief at 11-17).
 
Upon de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not demonstrate that her failure to appear at her March 29, 2011, hearing was due to an exceptional circumstance beyond her control (I.J. at 3). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); see also section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). The Board has held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order, where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). There is no dispute that the respondent fulfilled the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra (I.J. at 4). However, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent did not establish that she was defrauded or that Ms. Cheung and Mr. Hernandez misadvised her about the consequences of failing to appear at her scheduled hearing (I.J. at 4).
 
We discern no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not demonstrate that she was the victim of fraud or that her failure to appear at the scheduled hearing was attributable to the deficient advice of former counsel (I.J. at 4). See Borges v. Gonzales, 402 F.3d 398, 407 (3d Cir. 2005) (observing that “[a] finding of fraud is a factual determination”). We acknowledge the respondent’s appellate assertion that the Immigration Judge should not have considered Ms. Cheung’s documentary submission (Respondent’s Brief at 7-10). Yet, we are not persuaded that an assessment of that documentary submission was fundamentally unfair or that the statements of an aggrieved party subject to a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel may not be considered in the context of a motion to reopen (Respondent’s Brief at 7-10). See, e.g., Matter of Rivera, supra (considering the statements in an affidavit provided by the alien’s former counsel in assessing an ineffective assistance claim); cf. Borges v. Gonzales, supra, at 404 (indicating that the Board evaluated both the alien’s and his former counsel’s affidavits in ruling on the ineffective assistance of counsel claim).
 
*3 Moreover, although the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge credited Ms. Cheung’s and Mr. Hernandez’s statements over her own, the record reflects that the Immigration Judge decided that reopening was not warranted based on the respondent’s version of events, as set forth in the respondent’s initial affidavit (I.J. at 4; Motion to Reopen, Tab E; Respondent’s Brief 10-11).3 In particular, the Immigration Judge observed that the respondent admitted that she knew she would be removed if she did not appear for her scheduled hearing (I.J. at 4; Motion to Reopen, Tab E, at 42). We find no clear error in this observation, given that the respondent had previously appeared for a hearing before the Immigration Judge (on September 28, 2010), at which time she received warnings about the consequences of a failure to appear (I.J. at 4; Exh. 3B; Motion to Reopen, Tab E, at 29).
 
Additionally, the Immigration Judge determined that, through her discussions with Ms. Cheung and Mr. Hernandez, the respondent was presented with accurate information regarding her eligibility for relief and the consequences of failing to appear at the March 29, 2011, hearing (I.J. at 4). Specifically, the Immigration Judge observed that the respondent was told that she could apply for voluntary departure, but she was unwilling to depart the United States (I.J. at 3). The respondent’s affidavit confirms that she was advised that she could request voluntary departure at her March 29, 2011, hearing (Motion to Reopen, Tab E, at 37).
 
The respondent objects to the Immigration Judge’s statement that “[t]here is no evidence that [she] was advised to stay away from the court in March, 2011” (I.J. at 3; Respondent’s Brief at 11). We concur with the respondent that her affidavit constitutes evidence in this regard; however, she stated therein that Mr. Hernandez told her that she could opt not to return to court and hide from authorities or appear in court and pursue voluntary departure (I.J. at 3-4; Motion to Reopen, Tab E, at 37, 42; Respondent’s Brief at 11). In view of the contents of the respondent’s affidavit, we find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent “made a strategic and knowing choice to evade her immigration hearing and the immigration authorities” (I.J. at 4).
 
Based on the Immigration Judge’s assessments, we will uphold the ruling that the respondent did not establish exceptional circumstances that would warrant reopening based on her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The statements in the respondent’s affidavit, if accepted as true, reflect that she received unscrupulous advice in several respects. However, considering the facts in a light most favorable to the respondent, we cannot conclude that the Immigration Judge erred in deciding that the respondent did not demonstrate that her failure to attend her scheduled hearing was the result of former counsel’s deficient performance (I.J. at 3-4).
 
*4 In light of the foregoing, we will affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the repsondent’s motion to reopen. Because we hold that the respondent did not establish that her failure to appear at the hearing held in absentia was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel, we need not reach the issue whether she demonstrated sufficient grounds for equitable tolling of the motion filing deadline (I.J. at 4-5; Respondent’s Brief at 11-17). See Matter of S-H-, supra, at 464.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

The Immigration Judge’s written decision was signed on January 15, 2013, but mailed to the parties on January 16, 2013 (I.J. at 5). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b).

2

The record reflects that this decision was mailed to the parties on April 5, 2011.

3

Although the Immigration Judge noted that she considered all submissions, her decision does not reflect reliance upon, or citation to, any specific statement proffered by Ms. Cheung or Mr. Hernandez (I.J. at 1).

2014 WL 3889813 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OMAR ANTONIO VEGA-CRUZ
File: AXXX XX6 011 - San Antonio, TX
July 3, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kathryn E. Cimone, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Stuart D. Alcorn
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, signed April 15, 2013, denying his motion to reopen. The respondent’s appeal, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent was served with a Notice to Appear (“NTA”) which advised him, among other things, that he was required to immediately notify the Immigration Court, by filing an Alien’s Change of Address Form (Form EOIR-33), whenever he changed his address during the course of these removal proceedings (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). See section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1)(F). When the respondent was released from DHS detention in September 2012, he reported that he was intending to reside on Tree Road in Centereach, New York (Exh. 2). As such, on January 22, 2013, the Immigration Court mailed a Notice of Hearing in Removal Proceedings (“NOH”) to the respondent on Tree Road to advise him that a hearing had been scheduled in these proceedings on February 6, 2013 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 3). As the respondent did not appear at the removal hearing on February 6, 2013, he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia (I.J. at 1). See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent filed his motion to reopen on March 28, 2013 (I.J. at 1). In his motion, the respondent claimed to be residing on Smith Street in Centereach (Respondent’s Affidavit at ¶ 3). The respondent also presented an Order of Release on Recognizance (Form I-220A) which ordered him to report to a “Duty Officer” at 26 Federal Plaza in New York City on March 19, 2013.
 
Reopening is not warranted on account of lack of notice. As stated above, the respondent reported to immigration officers that, upon his release from detention, he would be residing on Tree Road. However, in his motion to reopen, he claimed that he was residing on Smith Street. As recognized by the Immigration Judge, the respondent’s affidavit did not state when he moved to Smith Street (I.J. at 1).1 The respondent’s affidavit made no claim that he ever resided on Tree Road or had access to the mail delivered at that address. Moreover, in the Form EOIR-33 which the respondent filed in support of his motion, he reported his old address as “DHS.” Thus, it appears that the respondent may have moved directly to Smith Street upon his release from DHS detention. Accordingly, as the respondent’s motion to reopen did not establish that he did not evade delivery of a properly sent NOH by not keeping the Immigration Court apprised of his address, reopening is not warranted on account of lack of notice. See section 239(a)(2)(B) of the Act (providing that an alien need not be provided with notice of his removal hearing if he has failed to report his address); section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act (providing that removal proceedings may be reopened on account of lack of notice where “““the failure to appear was through no fault to the alien”); Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354, 360 (5th Cir. 2009) (“This court has repeatedly held in unpublished opinions that an alien’s failure to receive actual notice of a removal hearing due to his neglect of his obligation to keep the [I]mmigration [C]ourt apprised of his current mailing address does not mean that the alien “““did not receive notice” under [section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act].”); Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 675 (BIA 2008) (“Once a respondent has received a Notice to Appear, he must comply with this statutory responsibility. Therefore, a respondent cannot evade delivery of a properly sent Notice of Hearing by relocating without providing the required change of address and then request reopening of in absentia proceedings on the basis of a claim that he did not receive notice.”).
 
*2 Reopening is also not warranted on account of exceptional circumstances causing the respondent’s failure to appear at his removal hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. While the respondent claims that he was confused by the aforementioned Form I-220A, which required him to report to a Duty Officer in New York City, the NTA explained to the respondent that he had been placed in removal proceedings before an Immigration Judge in San Antonio (Exh. 1). Overall, considering the totality of the circumstances, despite the respondent’s claim that he had recently entered the United States, that he does not speak English, and that he did not receive the NOH, we are not persuaded that it was beyond his control to contact the Immigration Court to confirm the date, time, and location of his removal hearing and appear at said hearing. See section 240(e) of the Act (defining “exceptional circumstances” as circumstances Which are “beyond the control of the alien”). As recognized by the Immigration Judge, the respondent had “ample opportunity” to consult with legal representatives regarding his rights and obligations (I.J. at 2). Even though the respondent claimed that, upon his release from detention he retained counsel (Respondent’s Affidavit at ¶ 12), he has not brought forth an ineffective assistance of counsel claim.
 
For the reasons set forth above, we conclude that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

While counsel for the respondent claims that he resided on Tree Road until March 2013 (Respondent’s Br. at 8), such a claim has not been supported by evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).
