2014 WL 3889567 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSEPH NJUGUNA MUNIU
File: AXXX XX6 658 - Chicago, IL
June 27, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Swaray E. Conteh, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michelle M. Venci
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Kenya, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of July 17, 2013. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on April 25, 2013. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts.
 
Here, as noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent, through counsel, filed a change of venue motion, containing his new address in South Bend, IN, to which the February 7, 2013, Notice of Hearing (NOH) was mailed. In his motion to reopen, the respondent admitted that after the change of venue, he moved to Indianapolis. He does not explain the South Bend address in his motion to reopen. Nor does he allege that he filed a change of address to any of his Indianapolis addresses.
 
An alien bears an affirmative responsibility to keep the agency informed of his correct, current address. See generally Dominguez v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 284 F.3d 1258, 1260 (11th Cir. 2002). In fact, under section 240(a)(1)(F) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1)(F), an alien must immediately provide the government with an address at which he may be contacted and must immediately provide notice of any change of the alien’s address. See section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1)(F); see also Dominguez, 284 F.3d at 1260. Given this duty, “notice to the alien at the most recent address provided by the alien is sufficient notice,” justifying an in absentia removal. Dominguez, 284 F.3d at 1260.
 
The respondent is not entitled to rescission of a removal order where the failure to receive actual notice of the time of the hearing is the result of his failure to comply with the obligation to keep the Immigration Court apprised of his current mailing address. The alien who evades notice cannot reopen the removal hearing. See Peralta-Cabrera v. Gonzales, 501 F.3d 837, 843-44 (7th Cir. 2007); Sabir v. Gonzales, 421 F.3d 456, 458-59 (7th Cir. 2005).
 
*2 We agree with the Immigration Judge that service of the NOH was properly mailed to the last address provided by the respondent, whether it was valid or not. Furthermore, as is the case here, if an alien does not fulfill his obligation to report his address change, notice of the hearing is not required pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229a(b)(5)(B), and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Finally, service by mail is sufficient if the record contains proof of attempted delivery to the last address provided by the alien in accordance with section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1)(F). Section 239(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(c). As the record contains proof of attempted delivery to the address provided by the respondent as reflected in the change of venue motion, the respondent can be charged with receiving proper notice, regardless that the respondent may not have actually received the NOH. Consequently, the NOH was properly mailed to the respondent at the South Bend address, and the respondent has not established that non-delivery was not due to his own fault. See Joshi v. Ashcroft, 389 F.3d 732, 736 (7th Cir. 2004) (the fact that the intended recipient did not actually receive notice does not contradict evidence that delivery was attempted and the notice requirement thus satisfied.).
 
On appeal, the respondent now admits that he at some point lived at the South Bend address; that the change of venue motion provided the South Bend address; and he further alleges a lack of communication between him and his former counsel. To the extent he is alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, we find no merit. The Board has established threshold requirements for bringing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The alien must submit an affidavit establishing that he had an agreement with counsel to represent him, as well as the terms of representation; the alien must present evidence that he informed counsel of the allegations against him and gave an opportunity to respond; and there must be a statement indicating whether a complaint has been filed against appropriate disciplinary authorities and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). See also Marinov v. Holder, 687 F.3d 365, 368 (7th Cir. 2012). Here, the respondent has fulfilled none of the Lozada requirements.
 
We find that the respondent has not established an exceptional circumstance herein to warrant the sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
*3 For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 3889567 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GABRIEL QUEZADA-MACIAS A.K.A. CHON HECTOR HERNANDEZ
File: AXXX XX2 785 - Chicago, IL
June 26, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Al Kola, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Minnie D. Yuen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of May 24, 2013. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on March 28, 2013. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts.
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent alleges he did not appear “because of the exceptional circumstance of not being aware of the change of court dates.” As noted by the Immigration Judge, the notice of hearing for the March 28, 2013, hearing, was served on the respondent’s counsel and counsel appeared at the March 28, 2013, hearing, at which time counsel informed the Immigration Judge that the respondent had been notified of his hearing (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge correctly found that notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent (I.J. at 2). See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 292.5(a).
 
On appeal, the respondent again alleges he did not receive notice of his hearing and takes issue with the Immigration Judge’s acceptance of his former counsel’s assertion (apparently that counsel advised the respondent of his hearing) (Respondent’s Appeal Brief, page 4). We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. Here, however, the respondent has not alleged ineffective assistance by former counsel; nor has the respondent fulfilled any of the Lozada criteria. Consequently, the respondent has failed to perfect a claim of ineffective assistance of his counsel, and there is no basis to reopen under “exceptional circumstances.”
 
*2 For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise Manuel
FOR THE BOARD

2014 WL 3889541 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HONG LIU YANG
File: AXXX XX8 213 - Chicago, IL
June 26, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Thomas J. Mayer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Robin J. Rosche
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of China, was ordered removed in absentia on February 9, 2010. On October 23, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings which the Immigration Judge denied on December 21, 2012. The respondent filed a timely appeal of that decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision for the reasons cited to therein. In the motion to reopen proceedings that was filed with the Immigration Judge the respondent did not allege that the notice for her February 9, 2010, hearing was defective in any way and did not contend that exceptional circumstances prevented her from attending the hearing. Rather, she she sought reopening in order to pursue adjustment of status because she is the beneficiary of a recently approved I-130. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The rescission requirements need not be satisfied in certain circumstances when an alien is seeking reopening to apply for previously unavailable relief and the oral warnings for failure to appear were not provided. However, such motions are subject to the general time limits for reopening. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the motion was untimely. Further, this situation is not exceptional such that sua sponte reopening is warranted.
 
The respondent, on appeal, argues for the first time that she did not receive notice and that she received ineffective assistance from her former counsel. However, we have before us for review the denial of the motion that was filed with the Immigration Judge on October 23, 2012. To the extent that the respondent is now making a claim seeking rescission of the in absentia order based on a claim of lack of notice due to ineffective assistance of counsel, such motions must be filed with the Immigration Judge and are subject to specific time and number bars. Section 240(b)(5) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (an order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed before the Immigration Judge). Accordingly, we will dismiss the appeal.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD

2014 WL 3889537 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AMONCIO CRUZ-GOMEZ
File: AXXX XX2 131 - Memphis, TN
June 26, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ellis D. Bingham, III
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 22, 2013, denying his motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the scheduled hearing on March 12, 2013. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred by analyzing this case as a Lozada claim, as the facts indicate that neither the attorney nor the respondent received notice of the hearing. The respondent states that he does not allege misconduct by his former counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). He also asserts that as there is no transcript of the proceeding, it is impossible to discern whether oral notice of the hearing was provided. However, a transcript is not ordinarily prepared where a motion to reopen is filed, since the motion will be decided on the basis of its merits rather than on the basis of a review of the record. See Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996) (noting transcript ordinarily not present when motion to reopen filed).
 
The respondent does not dispute that he and his former counsel, Robert B. Ogletree, Esquire, were present in court on March 20, 2012 and received notice of the individual hearing scheduled for August 8, 2013. See Exh. 2. However, he claims that he did not receive notice for the March 12, 2013, hearing. The Immigration Judge indicates in his decision dated August 22, 2013, that the respondent’s claim that he did not know about the March 12, 2013, hearing is incorrect, as the respondent was told in the Spanish language, through an interpreter, that he would be returning to the court two more times and the Immigration Judge stated the dates of the two hearings (I.J. at 3). The Immigration Judge also indicates that Mr. Ogletree was provided oral and written notice of the respondent’s two hearing dates, one to file his application on March 12, 2013, and one for his individual hearing on August 8, 2013. The certificate of service for each notice of hearing indicates that the respondent’s attorney and counsel for the Department of Homeland Security received personal service of the notices. See Exh. 2. Therefore, the record supports the finding that the respondent was provided oral notice and his former counsel was provided oral and written notice of the master hearing scheduled for March 12, 2013. Notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). The respondent indicates that he is not alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, and therefore, we will not address that issue. We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
*2 We further find no basis for exercising our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations). Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 3889537 (BIA)
End of Document

2014 WL 3889543 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANCA GABRIELA DANCIU
File: AXXX XX6 024 - Chicago, IL
June 26, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christopher W. Helt, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Minnie Yuen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)] - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
The respondent moves for reconsideration of the Board’s December 13, 2013, decision dismissing her appeal from the Immigration Judge’s October 23, 2012, decision denying the respondent’s request for a continuance. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(b), 1003.29. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). We will deny the respondent’s motion for reconsideration.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We will deny the respondent’s motion for reconsideration because she has not demonstrated any errors of fact or law in the Board’s December 13, 2013, decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). There is no error in the Board’s determination that the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) properly charged the respondent as an arriving alien (Exh. 1). 8 C.F.R. §§ 1245.2(a)(1)(ii), (4)(ii)(B). The respondent, through her attorney, admitted the allegations and legal charge of removability in the Notice to Appear (“NTA”), which included the charge that she is an arriving alien (Exh. 1; Tr. at 11). 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c); Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (“Absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made before, during, or even after a proceeding by an attorney acting in his professional capacity binds his client as a judicial admission.”). The respondent has raised no allegation that her attorney below provided ineffective assistance of counsel.1
 
In pleading to the NTA, the respondent also admitted the allegations that she was paroled into the United States pursuant to an advance parole and for the purpose of pursuing a pending adjustment application, and that the USCIS denied the adjustment application (Exh. 1; Tr. at 11). Thus, by operation of 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(4)(ii)(B), the respondent is an arriving alien (December 13, 2013, Board Decision at 1-2). See also Matter of Silitonga, 25 I&N Dec. 89 (BIA 2009).
 
*2 Nothing in the record support the respondent’s contention that upon the DHS’s termination of her advance parole, her status reverted to that of a J-1 nonimmigrant (Respondent’s Motion for Reconsideration at 10). 8 C.F.R. § 212.5(e)(2)(i). Although the respondent was admitted in 2001 on a J-1 visa, there is no record evidence reflecting that when the respondent was granted advance parole in April of 2003, she was still in J-1 nonimmigrant status (Exh. 1; see also Respondent’s November 20, 2012, Filing of Respondent’s Admission Documents Before Immigration Court).
 
Moreover, the respondent’s reliance on the Board’s decision in Matter of Arrabally & Yerrabelly, 25 I&N Dec. 771 (BIA 2012) is inapt (Respondent’s Motion for Reconsideration at 4-5, 10-11). In Matter of Arrabelly & Yerrabelly, supra, the Board held that a respondent who departs the United States temporarily pursuant to a grant of advance parole does not make a “““departure . . . from the United States” within the meaning of section 212(a)(9)(B)(i)(II) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9)(B)(i)(II). That holding has no application here, however, because the DHS has not charged the respondent as an arriving alien due to her departure from the United States, nor has the DHS charged the respondent as inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(B)(i)(II) of the Act. Rather, the respondent is charged as inadmissible because she returned pursuant to advance parole in order to pursue a pending adjustment of status application which has been denied (Exh. 1). By operation of 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(4)(ii)(B), the respondent is an arriving alien.
 
Further, contrary to the respondent’s argument in her motion, this case does not fit within the narrow regulatory exception that allows arriving aliens to pursue an adjustment application before the Immigration Court (Respondent’s Motion for Reconsideration at 10). 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(1)(ii). As the Board’s prior opinion correctly notes, the respondent is not seeking renewal of her prior application to adjust, which the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) denied in 2009 (December 13, 2013, Board Decision at 1-2; October 23, 2012, I.J. at 2; Exh. 1). On the contrary, the respondent seeks a continuance of these proceedings to pursue a new adjustment application based on a new I-130 filed by her second husband (December 13, 2013, Board Decision at 2-3). Thus, this case does not fall within the regulatory exception at 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(1)(ii), and the Immigration Court does not have jurisdiction over the respondent’s adjustment of status application.
 
*3 The respondent has not demonstrated any error in the Board’s December 13, 2013, decision holding that she was properly charged as arriving alien, and that the Immigration Court has no jurisdiction over her second application for adjustment of status based on the pending visa petition Form I-130, filed by her second husband (December 13, 2013, Board Decision at 1-2). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1245.2(a)(1)(ii), 1245.2(a)(4)(ii)(B).
 
The respondent has also not demonstrated any factual or legal error in the Board’s affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s denial of her requested continuance. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. Both the October 23, 2012, Immigration Judge decision and the December 13, 2013, Board decision properly applied the factors articulated in Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785, 790-91 (BIA 2009), and correctly found the facts based on the record presented. We recognize, as did the Board in its December 13, 2013, decision, that certain facts have changed since the Immigration Judge’s October 23, 2012, decision, namely that the respondent married the father of her child and that he filed a visa petition on her behalf.2
 
However, there is no evidence that the visa petition has been approved by the USCIS, and there is likewise no evidence that the respondent’s husband has naturalized (December 13, 2013, Board Decision at 2-3). Thus, even if the visa petition is approved, a visa would not be immediately available to the respondent (Id. at 2-3). See section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(2)(i)(A). Further, as noted above, the Immigration Court does not have jurisdiction over the respondent’s application to adjust her status. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1245.2(a)(1)(ii), 1245.2(a)(4)(ii)(B). Thus, the respondent has not demonstrated any error in the Board’s affirmance of the denial of her requested continuance. Further, the respondent has not demonstrated any legal or factual error in the Board’s decision declining to administratively close the respondent’s proceedings (December 13, 2013, Board Decision at 3). Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688, 696 (BIA 2012).
 
As the respondent has not demonstrated any factual or legal error in the Board’s December 13, 2013, decision, we will deny her motion for reconsideration.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion for reconsideration of the Board’s December 13, 2013, decision is denied.
 
*4 Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We do not agree with the respondent’s characterization of the Board’s December 13, 2013, decision as “presum[ing],” without record support, that the respondent admitted to the arriving alien charge (Respondent’s Motion for Reconsideration at 4). The record clearly reflects that the respondent admitted the allegations and charge in the NTA, including the arriving alien charge (Tr. at 11; Exh. 1).

2

The respondent incorrectly contends that her motion for reconsideration filed before the Immigration Judge, which alleged these new facts, remains pending ((Respondent’s Motion for Reconsideration at 9). On the contrary, although the Immigration Judge did not address the motion before she filed her appeal, the Board addressed the motion in its December 13, 2013, decision, treating it as a motion to reopen and remand, and denying it (December 13, 2013, Board Decision at 2 and n.2).

2014 WL 3889549 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILTON JOSE PAYANO-HENRIQUEZ
File: AXXX XX1 901 - Houston, TX
June 25, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jorge Guttlein, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated May 22, 2013, denying his motion to reconsider. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
On January 30, 2006, the respondent’s former counsel was personally served with a Notice of Hearing in Removal Proceedings (“NOH”) in order to place the respondent on notice that a removal hearing had been scheduled in these proceedings on February 22, 2006 (Exh. 5). As the respondent did not appear at the hearing, he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A).
 
On March 19, 2013, the respondent moved for reopening on the basis of a claim that he did not receive the aforementioned NOH. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act. His motion to reopen did not raise any claim to ineffective assistance of counsel and was not accompanied by any the evidence required by this Board’s decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). On April 3, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen upon concluding that, as the respondent’s former counsel was personally served with the NOH, notice was effective.1 See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Men Keng Chang v. Jiugni, 669 F.2d 275, 277 (5th Cir. 1982); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien). The respondent filed his motion to reconsider, which is the subject of the current appeal, on May 2, 2013.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The respondent has not established any legal or factual error in the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2). In particular, the respondent has not established that the Immigration Judge’s finding that the NOH was personally served upon his former counsel is factually erroneous, nor that the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent is legally erroneous.
 
*2 The respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel were not properly presented to the Immigration Judge. A motion to reconsider is an alien’s opportunity to specify the errors of law or fact in the previous order and not an opportunity to present new evidence, nor an opportunity to reiterate or expand upon arguments that have been previously raised and considered. See Matter of 0-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). While the Act, vis-à-vis the filing of a motion to reopen, grants an alien who is subject to an order of removal an opportunity to present new evidence, the respondent used his single opportunity to present a claim to reopening when he filed his initial motion to reopen on March 19, 2013. See section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). As such, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s claim to reconsideration on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Medrano, 20 I&N Dec. 216 (BIA 1990, 1991) (holding that a motion to reconsider which is based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied).
 
The respondent has attached numerous documents to his appeal brief. The Board does not consider evidence first provided on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv); Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984) (“The Board is an appellate body whose function is to review, not to create, a record.”). Additionally, to the extent that the submission of evidence on appeal can be construed as a request for a remand, such a request is number-barred. See Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000). As discussed above, the respondent used his single opportunity to request reopening when, on March 19, 2013, he filed his aforementioned motion to reopen on account of lack of notice which, for the reasons stated above, the Immigration Judge properly denied.
 
For the reasons set forth above, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent filed his Notice of Appeal from a Decision of an Immigration Judge (Form EOIR-26) with this Board on June 14, 2013. As such, any challenge to the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his motion to reopen is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIO ORDAZ-DE JESUS A.K.A. MARIO ORDAZ-DEJESUS
File: AXXX XX7 843 - Chicago, IL
June 16, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kristine E. Michel, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Amy E. Greene
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 1, 2013, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen. The respondent asserts that he established ineffective assistance of his former counsel because he did not fully understand the consequences of voluntary departure and he would have sought different relief from removal.
 
The record reflects that the Immigration Judge issued a decision on January 17, 2013, granting the respondent pre-conclusion voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). The respondent filed a motion to reopen on May 17, 2013, claiming ineffective assistance of his former counsel, Rafael Diaz, Esquire, because he did not inform the respondent of any potential relief from removal or discuss a potential request for prosecutorial discretion. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. (BIA 1988). The respondent claims that although he indicated to the Immigration Judge that he understood what voluntary departure meant, he maintains that he did not fully understand the consequences of voluntary departure because of the lack of advice from Mr. Diaz. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, finding that the respondent has not identified any relief that should have been sought before the court, and that the Department of Homeland Security has jurisdiction over requests for prosecutorial discretion.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen before the Immigration Court was filed untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).1 The respondent concedes that he indicated during the proceedings that he understood and accepted pre-conclusion voluntary departure. Moreover, the respondent has not established that he complied with the notice requirements of Lozada, as he informed his former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel just three days before filing the motion to reopen with the Immigration Court. See Marinov v. Holder, 687 F.3d 365, 369 (7th Cir. 2012) (court held that Board did not abuse its discretion in upholding denial of motion to reopen where timing of bar complaint denied him any realistic opportunity to respond to the allegations).
 
*2 Furthermore, the respondent did not establish that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s actions. The respondent has not identified on appeal any type of relief from removal for which he is eligible to apply. He states that he would have sought different relief from removal, but he does not state what type of relief. The respondent also indicates that he could have sought prosecutorial discretion with the Department of Homeland Security. However, a request for prosecutorial discretion is within the jurisdiction of the Department of Homeland Security, and the Immigration Judge and the Board of Immigration Appeals do not have any authority to grant such relief. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3), a motion to reopen for the purpose of acting on an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation.

IN RE: OSAMA MIKHO ORAHA
File: AXXX XX2 979 - Phoenix, AZ
June 13, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James B. Rudolph, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brent Landis
Senior Attorney
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)] - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)] - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a citizen of Canada, appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 15, 2013, decision in which he denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely filed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen because it was untimely filed. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, has held that an allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel may provide a basis for equitably tolling the relevant filing deadlines where the respondent has exercised due diligence in discovering the ineffective assistance of counsel and filing the motion to reopen. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir 2003) (allowing equitable tolling of deadlines on motions to reopen “when a petitioner is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud, or error, as long as the petitioner acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error”).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not establish that he exercised due diligence in pursuing his ineffective assistance of counsel claim (I.J. at 5-6, 8). See Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090, 1096 (9th Cir. 2007) (“The more difficult situation is whether [the respondent] acted with due diligence to definitively learn of the fraud after he became suspicious of the fraud.”). We find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent did not state when he began to suspect prior counsel’s representation had been deficient (I.J. at 6). Thus, we concur with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not meet his burden to demonstrate that the time for filing his motion to reopen should be equitably tolled.
 
*2 The Immigration Judge also denied the respondent’s motion to reopen because the motion was not supported by evidence that he is prima facie eligible for relief from removal (I.J. at 6-7, 8). We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s motion was not supported by an adjustment application and evidence of his prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §1255(a). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). On appeal, the respondent asserts his eligibility for adjustment of status, but he does not explain his failure to proffer documentation establishing such eligibility in support of his motion to reopen. Consequently, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Teresa L. Donovan
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 3795558 (BIA)

2014 WL 3795542 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JACOBO LOPEZ-MEDRANO
File: AXXX XX4 192 - San Antonio, TX
June 13, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Thomas Esparza, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Stuart D. Alcorn
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; adjustment of status
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 21, 2013, denying his motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status as the spouse of a United States citizen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The record reflects that on August 7, 2000, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent voluntary departure until December 5, 2000. The respondent did not depart during the voluntary departure period. He filed a motion to reopen on April 5, 2012, based on an approval notice dated December 5, 2001, regarding the visa petition filed on his behalf by his United States citizen spouse. The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status. He asserts that the ineffective assistance of his prior counsel, Mansel Turton, Esquire, prejudiced his ability to present and prosecute an application for adjustment of status.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s motion was filed untimely, as it was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s August 7, 2000, decision. Furthermore, we have determined that the Board and the courts do not have authority to recognize equitable exceptions to the section 240B(d) bar beyond those specifically provided in the voluntary departure provisions. Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87, 92 (BIA 2007); section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d). We acknowledge that the 10-year bar has elapsed since the voluntary departure order was issued. However, the respondent in the instant matter was aware of the requirements of the voluntary departure order, as the respondent was advised by the Immigration Judge of the limitation on discretionary relief for failure to depart by the stated deadline. However, he did not leave within the voluntary departure period.
 
The respondent claims on appeal he is eligible to have his proceedings reopened because he was improperly advised while being represented. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The Immigration Judge correctly determined that at the time of his hearing in August 2000, the respondent did not establish that any relief was available to him, other than voluntary departure. The respondent argues that the motion to reopen is not untimely because of equitable tolling. As noted by the Immigration Judge, the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has not determined if equitable tolling of statutory deadlines for ineffective assistance of counsel applies in immigration cases.1 We agree with the Immigration Judge that even if the Fifth Circuit Court of Appeals allowed equitable tolling in these types of cases, the respondent has not exercised due diligence in seeking to reopen proceedings. The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed more than eight years after his last application to the government, Form I-212, was denied by the Department of Homeland Security in January 2005. See DHS Response to Motion to Reopen, Tab C.
 
*2 Furthermore, as a matter of discretion, we find that the facts presented in this case do not warrant an intervention of our sua sponte authority to reopen the proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Fifth Circuit Court of Appeals has stated that an alien’s request for equitable tolling on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel is construed as an invitation for the Board to exercise its discretion to reopen the removal proceeding sua sponte. See Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 21 6, 220 (5th Cir. 2008).

2014 WL 3795538 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIO ISRAEL SURUS
File: AXXX XX1 448 - Atlanta, GA
June 13, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Leo Jerome Lahey, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Renae M. Hansell
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Israel who was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on December 13, 2012, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, signed January 31, 2013, denying his motion to reopen.1 The respondent’s appeal, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security, will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent has not articulated a basis which warrants reopened proceedings. See sections 240(b)(5)(C), (c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229(b)(5)(C), (c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). The respondent, through counsel, acknowledged that he was scheduled for a hearing on December 13, 2012, when he moved for a continuance on December 4, 2012. Thus, he has not established that he did not have notice of his removal hearing. While, at the time of his removal hearing, the respondent had a pending motion to change venue (which was present in the Record of Proceedings at the time of the entry of the in absentia order of removal) (I.J. at 2), “exceptional circumstances” did not excuse his failure to appear as “[t]he filing of a motion to change venue does not excuse the appearance of an alien or representative at any scheduled hearing.” Immigration Court Practice Manual, § 5.10(c) at 102 (June 10, 2013); see also Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996). While counsel for the respondent concedes that he “failed to press upon the respondent the necessity of his presence in the Atlanta Court if the change of venue was not granted” (Respondent’s Br. at 4), the respondent has not brought forth an ineffective assistance of counsel claim (I.J. at 2). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
To the extent that the respondent is offering new evidence on appeal, i.e., evidence that he has filed for post-conviction relief, the pendency of post-conviction relief claims does not affect the finality of a conviction for immigration purposes. See Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506 (BIA 1992). As such, a remand is not warranted for the Immigration Judge to consider said evidence. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992). To the extent that the respondent appears to be also requesting sua sponte reopening, such measures have not been shown to be warranted. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, we decline to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. The following order is entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge’s decision to order the respondent’s removal from the United States was premised upon his March 2010 conviction for attempted possession of a controlled substance (hydrocodone) (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). See section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II).

2014 WL 3817755 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUILLERMO GAITAN QUIROZ A.K.A. GUILLERMO GAYTAN-QUIROZ
File: AXXX XX3 887 - Oklahoma City, OK
June 11, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael Robert Abdoveis, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On March 14, 2014, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen proceedings in which the Board dismissed his appeal on August 1, 2013. He also seeks a stay of removal. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely as it was filed more than 90 days after the Board’s decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). However, he contends that reopening is appropriate as he had ineffective assistance from former counsel. In support of his motion, he has submitted evidence reflecting that he filed a bar complaint against former counsel, and informed him of this fact (Motion, attachment at 1-7). Mickeviciute v. INS, 327 F.3d 1159, 1161 n. 2 (10th Cir. 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The letter to former counsel states that, because counsel did not file a criminal history chart, the respondent was unable to meet his burden of proving eligibility for voluntary departure and adjustment of status (Motion, attachment at 6-7).
 
However, to succeed on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent must not only satisfy the procedural requirements for such a claim. Rather, he must also show that he was prejudiced by counsel’s reported ineffective performance. Ochieng v. Mukasey, 520 F.3d 1110, 1115 (10th Cir. 2008) (rejecting ineffective-assistance claim based on failure to show prejudice). Prejudice is shown by demonstrating that, but for counsel’s ineffective performance, the result of his case would have been different. Duran-Hernandez v. Ashcroft, 348 F.3d 1158, 1163 (10th Cir. 2003). We find that the respondent has not met this burden.
 
The failure to submit records regarding the respondent’s criminal history was only one of many factors that the Immigration Judge considered in rendering his adverse credibility and discretionary determinations. The respondent’s testimony that he was arrested for a firearm offense (Tr. at 42-6) was not a factor in his inconsistent testimony regarding his dates of his marriage and travel into the United States, his knowledge of his wife’s filing of fraudulent tax returns, his own failure to file tax returns for 24 years, his initial false report in his adjustment application of having no arrests, and his on-going decision to drive without a license or insurance in violation of law (I.J. at 10-20). While the Immigration Judge did “consider” that the respondent had a conviction for a firearms offense, due to the absence of any records of this “conviction” he did not find that it was an aggravated felony (I.J. at 12, 16, 18). Rather, it was mentioned as one of many factors in his decision. Moreover, the Immigration Judge based his consideration of the case on the respondent’s own testimony that he was arrested in 1990 for possession of a firearm in a drinking establishment, for which he paid a fine (I.J. at 16; Tr. at 42-6). We note that the records submitted with the motion confirm that he was arrested in 1997 and charged with four firearms offenses, all of which were dismissed. The respondent has not explained why he testified otherwise, nor has he addressed any of the numerous other bases for the Immigration Judge’s adverse determinations.
 
*2 In sum, given the record presented, we do not find that the respondent has shown that the result of his case would have been different if former counsel had submitted records regarding his criminal history. As we find that the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by former counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance, reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is not warranted. Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 3817755 (BIA)
End of Document

2014 WL 3795489 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS MIGUEL PARRA
File: AXXX XX0 127 - Imperial, CA
June 9, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Shahid Ali, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reissuance; reopening
 
*1 The respondent’s motion is untimely. The Board entered the final administrative order in these proceedings on October 6, 2011, when it dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent filed his motion to reopen and reissue the Board’s 2011 decision and request for a stay of removal, on February 28, 2014, over 2 years after the Board’s final decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The motion and stay of removal will be denied.
 
The respondent asserts that the Board should reissue its earlier decision because he would like to file a petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit (Motion at 1-2). The Board has on occasion reissued its decisions, but generally only due to Board error or administrative problems involving receipt of the Board’s decision. Federal regulations require the Board to serve its final decision on the alien, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(f). “Service” is defined as either “physically presenting or mailing a document to the appropriate party or parties.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.13.
 
The respondent does not allege that he did not receive the Board’s decision, but rather that the time and numerical limitations for motions to reopen should be tolled because he is the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The time and number limits for motions to reopen may be equitably tolled where an alien raises a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003). However, it will only apply where the alien shows that he was prejudiced by his attorney’s conduct. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004); Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
The respondent has not complied with any of the Lozada1 procedural requirements. See generally Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893 (9th Cir. 2008). Furthermore, the respondent has not demonstrated that he acted with due diligence in pursuing his claim prior to filing the instant motion on February 28, 2014, over 2 years after the Board’s prior decision. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra, at 897 (holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). Furthermore, we see no reason to exercise our discretion to reopen proceedings sua sponte where the respondent has not articulated any available forms of relief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the motion to reopen and reissue our decision and the request for a stay of removal will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reissue is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Matter of Lozada, 19 I & N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), requires a successful ineffective assistance of counsel claim be accompanied by: “(1) an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement with former counsel concerning what action would be taken and what counsel did or did not represent in this regard; (2) proof that the alien notified former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance and allowed counsel an opportunity to respond; and (3) if a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities is claimed, a statement as to whether the alien filed a complaint with any disciplinary authority regarding counsel’s conduct and, if a complaint was not filed, an explanation for not doing so.”).

2014 WL 3697779 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JORGE PATRICIO LEMA LEMA
File: AXXX XX7 508 - New York, NY
May 30, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
H. Raymond Fasano, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the August 14, 2013, Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings which had been conducted in absentia on March 22, 2013. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed no reply to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews findings of fact by an Immigration Judge under the clearly erroneous standard of review, and may review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
Here, the Notice of Hearing (NOH) for the March 22, 2013, hearing was personally served on the respondent’s former counsel (I.J. at 1; Exh. 2). Notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Garcia v. INS, 222 F.3d 1208, 1209 (9th Cir. 2000) (per curiam) (notice to counsel sufficient); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 292.5(a).
 
In his motion, the respondent contends that his former counsel failed to inform the respondent of the hearing. Ineffective assistance of counsel may be an exceptional circumstance warranting rescission of an in absentia removal order. See Aris v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 595 (2d Cir. 2008). To succeed on a motion to reopen on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel, the alien must substantially comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 46 (2d Cir. 2005) (requiring substantial compliance with Lozada to preserve an ineffective assistance of counsel claim). Here, although the respondent provided his affidavit with the motion, it does not set forth what agreement, if any, he had with his former counsel. Nor has he fulfilled the other Lozada requirements, as noted by the Immigration Judge, and thus the respondent has failed to substantially comply with the Lozada requirements.
 
We find no merit to the respondent’s appellate contention that the Immigration Judge should have held an evidentiary hearing to determine whether the respondent’s previous attorney provided ineffective assistance. The respondent has cited to no authority for this argument, and moreover, given his failure to substantially comply with the Lozada requirements, no evidentiary hearing was warranted.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent now requests that the Board sustain the appeal and remand to allow the respondent to request that DHS exercise its prosecutorial discretion (citing Memorandum from Immigration and Customs Enforcement Director John Morton titled “Exercising Prosecutorial Discretion Consistent with the Civil Immigration Enforcement Priorities of the Agency for the Apprehension, Detention, and Removal of Aliens” (June 17, 2011) (hereinafter “Memorandum from ICE Director John Morton”)). Under the Morton Memo, the DHS may exercise prosecutorial discretion at any stage of an enforcement proceeding. Thus, the entry of this order does not prevent the respondent from pursuing such immigration benefits directly from the DHS. See Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 350 (BIA 1982). If the DHS elects to grant the respondent deferred action (or other immigration benefits as a matter of prosecutorial discretion), the parties are not precluded from filing a joint motion to reopen in the future. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(iii). However, we find no basis to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion to reopen.
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 3697779 (BIA)
End of Document

2014 WL 3697737 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ESPERANZA DEL CARMEN OROZCO A.K.A. ESPERANZA CARMEN OROZCO
File: AXXX XX4 097 - San Diego, CA
May 29, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Deborah Hoover Hupfer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rhana Ishimoto
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 7, 2013, denying her motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for her failure to appear for the scheduled hearing on May 27, 1999. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal the respondent contends that she has established ineffective assistance of counsel for her failure to appear. She asserts that notice was served only on her former counsel, and that she was not provided oral notice of the hearing in her native language. The respondent claims that she has complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent also indicates that she was not provided with a transcript of the proceedings. However, a transcript is not ordinarily prepared where a motion to reopen is filed, since the motion will be decided on the basis of its merits rather than on the basis of a review of the record. See Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996) (noting transcript ordinarily not present when motion to reopen filed).
 
The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying her motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the alien has satisfied the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). However, in order to equitably toll the filing deadline for a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate that she has exercised due diligence during the entire period she seeks to toll. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 679 (9th Cir. 2011) (holding that equitable tolling is available when an individual is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud or error, and exercised due diligence in discovering such circumstances).
 
*2 The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed untimely. The respondent has not demonstrated the due diligence required for equitable tolling of the filing deadline based on the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on May 27, 1999, yet she did not seek reopening until January 8, 2013. The respondent does not indicate in her affidavit that she attempted to contact her former counsel after her last appearance in court, and she states that she did not contact another attorney until late 2004. The respondent states on appeal that she made one personal trip to the Immigration Court to inquire about her next hearing date, but the filing windows were closed when she arrived. She has not indicated that she inquired thereafter with the Immigration Court to determine the status of her case. The respondent avers that since becoming aware of the in absentia order of removal in 2005, she has been actively trying to resolve the matter by retaining three different attorneys since then. However, she does not indicate that she attempted to determine the status of her case, or to consult with any other attorneys, between March 1999 and late 2004. The respondent has not met her burden of showing that she had been diligent during the entire period she seeks to toll. See id. at 679 (explaining that if an individual is “ignorant of counsel’s shortcomings, whether petitioner made reasonable efforts to pursue relief is a consideration when assessing diligence). The respondent has not demonstrated that she is entitled to equitable tolling, as she has not established that she acted with due diligence in this matter.
 
Moreover, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent has not established ineffective assistance of counsel. The record reflects that a notice of hearing dated March 3, 1999, was served personally on the respondent in court and she was provided with oral notice of the hearing scheduled for May 27, 1999, and of the adverse consequences of a failure to appear. Both the Record of Master Calendar Hearing and the notice of hearing dated March 3, 1999, contain the respondent’s signature underneath an ink stamp with the words “receipt by respondent,” further verifying that she received actual notice in court. As the respondent was personally served with the notice of hearing in court, she received proper notice of the hearing scheduled for May 27, 1999. See Khan v. Ashcroft, 374 F.3d 825 (9th Cir. 2004) (court held alien’s due process rights were not violated by failing to provide notice in his native language since he had actual notice and was personally served with notice at a hearing). Contrary to the respondent’s contention on appeal, the written notice to the respondent was only required to be provided to the respondent in the English language. In any event, the notice of hearing reflects that the respondent was provided oral notice of the contents of the hearing notice in her native language, or in a language she understands, by the Immigration Judge (Exh. 10). Section 240(e)(1), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1).
 
*3 The Immigration Judge indicates that she listened to the audio tapes of the May 27, 1999, proceedinss, and the respondent’s former counsel did appear for the hearing on that date. The Immigration Judge correctly determined that since the respondent was provided with both written and oral notice in court of the next hearing date, the ineffective assistance claim against her former counsel for failing to provide her notice of the May 27, 1999, hearing is unsubstantiated. The respondent also has not established exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear for the hearing, as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1).1
 
We further find no basis for exercising our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
Finally, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent has not established eligibility for any relief, nor has she submitted any applications for relief. We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1).

2014 WL 3698186 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN MARTINEZ-ALVAREZ
File: AXXX XX9 323 - Dallas, TX
May 29, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frances M. Cruz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s June 28, 2012, decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed an appeal brief. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
We review the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether or not the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On February 6, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied as abandoned the respondent’s application for adjustment of status because the application was not filed by the court-imposed deadline of February 1, 2012. By separate order, also dated February 6, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied a motion to withdraw as counsel and a request for additional time to file applications, which was filed on February 3, 2012. On April 20, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen sua sponte pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23, which the Immigration Judge denied on May 14, 2012, for failure to pay the filing fee for motions. A subsequent motion to reopen was filed on June 20, 2012, which the Immigration Judge denied on June 28, 2012, concluding that the motion was untimely and number-barred, and determining that there was an insufficient basis for sua sponte reopening. The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for sua sponte reopening.
 
“An Immigration Judge may upon his or her motion at any time . . . reopen or reconsider any case in which he or she has made a decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). The power to reopen or reconsider is limited to exceptional situations. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
We find that sua sponte reopening is appropriate in this case. Although the respondent did not fully comply with the evidentiary requirements for asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988),1 we note that he did submit an affidavit outlining the circumstances surrounding his representation by the Mathur Law Offices in this case, as well as his attorney fee agreement with the Mathur Law Offices, invoices and case notations from the firm, and attorney email correspondence.
 
*2 The motion to withdraw reflects that the respondent hired the Mathur Law Offices, and was assigned Cristobal Colindres as his attorney. The motion further states that Mr. Colindres ended his employment at the Mathur Law Offices on September 19, 2011, and that after his departure, he notified the firm that motions to substitute as counsel should be filed in all of his pending cases, including the respondent’s case, and that if pending filing deadlines were not met, his clients could have their claims denied as abandoned. In requesting to withdraw from the case and in seeking additional time for the respondent to file his application, Mr. Colindres noted that he received information from a court clerk on February 3, 2012, that the Mathur Law Offices had filed motions to substitute as counsel in all of his pending cases except the respondent’s case. Email correspondence between Mr. Colindres and representatives at the Mathur Law Offices, filed with the motion to withdraw and with the motion to reopen, confirms that in January 2012, before the filing deadline, Mr. Colindres inquired about the status of the motions to substitute and was told, incorrectly, that they had been filed in all of his pending cases. The respondent states in his affidavit that he last met with Attorney Mathur at the Mathur Law Offices on November 18, 2011, and that while he was informed that Mr. Colindres no longer worked for the firm, he was not informed about the need to file his applications with the court by February 1, 2012.
 
The evidence in the record therefore reflects that the respondent’s legal representation with the Mathur Law Offices was in transition shortly before the filing deadline, and that the Mathur Law Offices did not file a motion to substitute counsel after Mr. Colindres left the firm or submit the respondent’s application by the February 1, 2012, deadline. Based on the particular circumstances of this case, we thus conclude that sua sponte reopening is appropriate so that the respondent may have his application for adjustment of status considered on the merits. See Matter of J-J-, supra. Accordingly, the appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.2
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

Under Matter of Lozada, supra, a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel should be supported by an affidavit, reflect whether a complaint had been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and include a response to the allegations by former counsel or report counsel’s failure or refusal to respond. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

The respondent may be eligible for deferred action. 2014 WL 3697739 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LAI MING MA
File: AXXX XX8 151 - San Francisco, CA
May 29, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Xiaosheng Huang, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kathleen W. Taylor
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of April 10, 2013. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s March 26, 2013, motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia on July 12, 2011. The respondent has proffered evidence and we construe her appellate submission as a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief in opposition to the appeal and motion. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent was provided notice of the date, time, and location of her scheduled hearing before the Immigration Judge. As correctly noted by the Immigration Judge, the Notice to Appear (NTA) was personally served on the respondent (Exh. 1). The written notice of hearing (NOH) was mailed to the respondent, and she does not dispute receipt. In her motion, the respondent alleges that the NTA was stolen from her. However, the fact that the respondent lost or had her NTA stolen subsequent to receipt does not vitiate service.1
 
A motion to reopen alleging exceptional circumstances for a failure to appear at a removal hearing must be filed within 180 days of the order of removal, and only one such motion is allowed. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(i) & 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent’s motion claiming that she did not appear at her hearing because the NTA was stolen was properly found untimely, as it was filed well beyond 180 days after the removal order was entered.
 
On appeal, the respondent now alleges that: she was the victim of a second robbery, on April 19, 2011, at which time her NOH was also taken; she suffered an auto accident on April 24, 2011, and since then she has suffered “bad health;” and Mr. Michael Lin, Esquire, who represented her in her “personal injury case,” and who filed the motion to reopen, provided ineffective assistance. (Respondent’s Affidavit, page 1). Regarding her allegations against Mr. Lin, the respondent maintains that he failed to provide evidence with the motion to reopen, and she further alleges that Mr. Lin told her she did not have to appear at her immigration hearing. The respondent included; a copy of a letter to the Nevada Bar Counsel; an undated letter to Mr. Lin; her affidavit; police reports for theft incidents on February 16, 2011, and April 19, 2011; and an April 24, 2011, traffic accident report indicating that the respondent’s car was hit in a parking lot.
 
*2 Before we will consider an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, however, the respondent first must satisfy threshold procedural requirements established in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and adopted by the U.S. Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case lies. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 900 (9th Cir. 2003). She must provide “(1) an affidavit by the alien setting forth the agreement with counsel regarding the alien’s representation; (2) evidence that counsel was informed of the allegations and allowed to respond; and (3) an indication that a complaint has been lodged with the bar, or reasons explaining why not.” Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.
 
In Lozada, we explained that the notice requirement serves both to notify an alien’s former counsel of the ineffective assistance allegations and to give former counsel an opportunity to contest them before the Immigration Judge or Board. The participation of an alien’s former counsel, in turn, provides a mechanism by which the Immigration Judge or Board may more accurately assess the merits of an alien’s ineffective assistance claims. “[T]he potential for abuse is apparent,” we cautioned, “where no mechanism exists for allowing former counsel, whose integrity or competence is being impugned, to present his version of events if he so chooses, thereby discouraging baseless allegations.” Lozada, supra at 639.
 
Here, the respondent’s letter to Mr. Lin did not substantially comply with the Lozada requirement that she notify her former attorney of ineffective assistance allegations and submit any subsequent response from the attorney with the motion. We first note that the letter is not dated. In addition, the letter seeks a refund from Mr. Lin, but does not inform him that a Bar complaint and motion to reopen with the Board were filed. Although the respondent may have put Mr. Lin on notice concerning the substance of her ineffective assistance allegations, if she actually sent Mr. Lin a copy of the complaint letter,2 she offers absolutely no evidence that Mr. Lin received notice of the concomitant motion to reopen and Bar complaint.3 Because the respondent gave Mr. Lin no notice of the motion to reopen and no opportunity to respond to her allegations before the Board, we conclude that the respondent has not substantially satisfied Lozada’s notice requirement. Id. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 598 (9th Cir. 2004) (undated letter to prior counsel insufficient to meet notice and opportunity to respond requirements).
 
*3 To the extent the respondent maintains that she sustained injuries preventing her from timely filing a motion to reopen, she has failed to adequately establish she sustained any injuries from her April 24, 2011, auto accident. The report does not indicate any injuries were sustained; nor did the respondent proffer any medical evidence to corroborate her allegation that she is in “bad health.”
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Elise Manuel
