2014 WL 3697745 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SEYDA ELIZABETH ERAZO-PEREZ
File: AXXX XX1 477 - San Antonio, TX
May 28, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sharon Gray, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Clay Martin
Senior Attorney
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s March 28, 2013, decision denying her motion to reconsider. The respondent had requested that the Immigration Judge reconsider his May 24, 2012, decision denying her motion to reopen proceedings. The Board reviews findings of fact under a clearly erroneous standard, while all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i)-(ii). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent was ordered removed in absentia after she did not appear for a scheduled removal hearing on January 3, 2006. The respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings on April 4, 2012, alleging that she did not receive proper notice of the removal hearing.1 In a decision dated May 24, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen on the basis that notice was properly sent to the address provided by the respondent as reflected on the Notice to Appear. The Immigration Judge concluded that although the hearing notice was returned as undeliverable, the respondent had not fulfilled her obligation to keep the immigration court properly apprised of her current address, and thus was not entitled to rescission of the prior in absentia order. See Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354 (5th Cir. 2009). As the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent received personal service of the Notice to Appear, which contains notice of this requirement. See generally section 239(a)(1)(F)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“the Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1)(F)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.15(d). On March 23, 2013, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider, which was denied by the Immigration Judge. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s March 28, 2013, denial of her motion to reconsider.
 
An alien seeking to reopen proceedings based on improper notice may file a motion to reopen at any time. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii). A motion to reconsider should identify errors of fact or law in an Immigration Judge’s prior decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2); see also Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). A motion to reconsider is not an opportunity to present new evidence, nor an opportunity to reiterate or expand upon arguments that have been previously raised and considered. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, supra.
 
*2 We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The respondent’s motion to reconsider the Immigration Judge’s May 24, 2012, decision was not properly filed until March 23, 2013, and was therefore untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). We further affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reconsider on the basis that her motion did not identify an error of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s prior decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2); see also Matter of Cerna, supra. While the respondent emphasized in her motion to reconsider that she did not receive notification of her hearing, she did not identify error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that notice was proper because it was sent to the address provided by the respondent, and that the respondent had not informed the court of a change of address as required. See Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, supra. To the extent that the respondent’s motion might be characterized as a subsequent motion to reopen, the Immigration Judge correctly concluded that the motion is numerically barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (stating that a party may file only a single motion to reopen). We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reconsider on these grounds, and the respondent’s appeal will therefore be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

Statements by counsel do not themselves constitute evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980)

2014 WL 3697758 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS MARIA SOLIS-GARCIA A.K.A. LUIS M. SOLIS A.K.A. LUIS SOLIS
File: AXXX XX7 574 - York, PA
May 27, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alexander Arandia, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William E. Lore
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 29, 2013. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was placed in removal proceedings on October 31, 2012. His first hearing was on December 4, 2012. He was given a continuance to get an attorney. On January 29, 2013, he appeared for a second hearing without an attorney and opted to not get another continuance to find one. He admitted the factual allegations in the Notice to Appear. The Immigration Judge thereafter found him removable as charged for having been convicted of a controlled substance violation and an aggravated felony involving drug trafficking and conspiracy.
 
On appeal, the respondent says that he should have been given more time to find an attorney. Respondents in immigration proceedings have the statutory and regulatory “privilege of being represented” by counsel of their choice at no expense to the Government. See sections 240(b)(4)(A), 292 of the Act; 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(4)(A), 1362. See also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.16(b), 1240.3, 1240.10(a), 1240.11(c)(1)(iii). In order for a waiver to be valid, an Immigration Judge must generally: (1) inquire specifically as to whether petitioner wishes to continue without a lawyer; and (2) receive a knowing and voluntary affirmative response. See Matter of C-B-, 25 I&N Dec. 888, 889-890 (BIA 2012).
 
*2 In the present case, the respondent was asked if he wanted another continuance to find an attorney. In response, he said: “Without a lawyer, maybe the Lord can give the Judge understanding and maybe perhaps the Judge can understand my case” (Tr. at 4). The respondent did not ask for more time to find an attorney. Instead, he opted to proceed without one. Consequently, we find that the statutory and regulatory privilege of legal representation was waived.
 
On appeal, the respondent also says that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction. He is free to file a motion to vacate his criminal conviction before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010).2 However, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). A post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
Also on appeal, the respondent says that the Immigration Judge should have advised the respondent to collaterally attack his conviction. However, it is not the Immigration Judge’s responsibility to act as the respondent’s lawyer. Moreover, the respondent cannot collaterally attack the legitimacy of his otherwise valid state or federal criminal conviction in immigration proceedings. See Matter of Ponce De Leon-Ruiz, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (BIA 1996, 1997; AG 1997); Matter of Gabryelsky, 20 I&N Dec. 750 (BIA 1993); Matter of Fortis, 14 I&N Dec. 576, 577 (BIA 1974). Neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board has the authority to look beyond the record of conviction to determine the guilt or innocence of an alien in immigration proceedings. See Matter of Reyes, 20 I&N Dec. 789 (BIA 1994); Matter of Fortis, supra. Rather, such collateral attacks must be addressed to the appropriate criminal court.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. _, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

IN RE: CORNELIO LARA RUIZ A.K.A. CORNELIO LARA-RUIZ
File: AXXX XX6 495 - Charlotte, NC

APPEAL

Jaime Jasso, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Lecker
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the July 27, 2012, decision of the Immigration Judge denying his motion to reconsider. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On March 19, 2012, the respondent, through counsel, requested and was granted pre-conclusion voluntary departure with an alternate order of removal. He subsequently filed a motion to reopen proceedings on June 29, 2012, which the Immigration Judge denied, inter alia, because it was untimely, and the respondent claimed ineffective assistance of counsel, but had not complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741, 748 (4th Cir. 2006).
 
The respondent subsequently filed a motion to reconsider. A motion to reconsider must specify “[e]rrors of fact or law in the prior decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(C). A party seeking reconsideration requests that the original decision be reexamined in light of alleged factual or legal errors, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57-58 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402-03 (BIA 1991).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s subsequent motion to reconsider because it did not identify an error of law or fact in the prior decision (Decision on a Motion, July 27, 2012). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of O-S-G-, supra; Matter of Cerna, supra. On appeal, the respondent largely raises the same or similar arguments. However, we have considered those arguments and find them unpersuasive when viewed within the record as a whole before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (a motion to reconsider is not a process by which a party may submit, in essence, the same brief presented on appeal and seek reconsideration). The respondent’s disagreement with the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen did not provide a basis for a motion to reconsider. See id. The Immigration Judge did not err in finding that the respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely, and that he failed to comply with the procedural requirements to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Nor did the respondent demonstrate a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel that was sufficient to waive the Lozada requirements. See Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133 (2d Cir. 2007) (persuasive authority). Therefore we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reconsider.
 
*2 We also uphold the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen as a matter of discretion (I.J. Order of July 13, 2012). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of Barrera, 19 I&N Dec. 837, 844 (BIA 1989). The regulation states that “[t]he Immigration Judge has discretion to deny a motion to reopen even if the moving party has established a prima facie case for relief.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). For the foregoing reasons the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: DAVID KIRWA KURGAT
File: AXXX XX3 420 - Kansas City, MO
April 25, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christopher B. McKinney, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Justin Howard
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)] - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
 
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; continuance; voluntary departure
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s March 28, 2012, decision denying the respondent’s request for a continuance, pretermitting his application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), and denying his application for voluntary departure. During the pendency of his appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
Through his motion, the respondent contends that he should be permitted to apply for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act because his application was pretermitted by the Immigration Judge due to ineffective assistance of counsel. See Respondent’s Motion at 5-9. According to the respondent, his prior counsel failed to complete and submit a Form I-864 Affidavit of Support despite the fact that he had provided prior counsel with his entire file and his sponsor had provided prior counsel with all of the necessary documentation to complete the form via fax prior to the respondent’s proceedings on March 28, 2012, resulting in pretermission of his application. See id. at 6. The respondent further claims that prior counsel advised him that a continuance would be granted at the hearing on March 28, 2012, such that his witnesses need not appear in court. See id. As such, the respondent alleges that he provided prior counsel with all of the necessary information needed to complete the adjustment of status application but prior counsel failed to do so, instead relying on a request for a continuance at the March 28, 2012, hearing. See id. at 5-9.
 
A successful motion based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy two conditions. First, the claim must comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). Briefly, the Lozada requirements are (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit detailing counsel’s failings, (2) that counsel be informed of the allegations, and (3) that the motion show that disciplinary charges have been filed with the appropriate authority, or if not, adequately explain why not. Id. Second, the alien “““must also show that he or she was prejudiced by the actions or inactions of counsel.” See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003). In this case, the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 As to the issue of prejudice, the respondent claims that he and his sponsor provided all of the information needed to complete the Affidavit of Support prior to the March 28, 2012, hearing. In his response, prior counsel submitted evidence showing that the sponsor faxed to him, inter alia, a summary of her prior year’s tax return and an employment letter.1 However, prior counsel claims that for various reasons this information was insufficient to complete the Affidavit of Support. Along with his motion, the respondent has provided an affidavit from the sponsor. In her affidavit, the sponsor states she was told by prior counsel that he had received her documents and that he was going to file the Affidavit of Support before the next hearing. Thus, the respective claims in regard to this sponsor by the respondent and prior counsel are in contradiction.
 
The respondent also asserts through his motion that he provided prior counsel with his entire file from a previous counsel before the March 28, 2012, hearing. In this regard, prior counsel states in his responses to both the respondent and the bar officials that he did not receive the file until after the March 28, 2012, proceedings. For his part, the respondent provided a fax he sent to prior counsel from detention on February 15, 2012, and states that, along with that fax, he faxed over his entire file. While the fax submitted was only a page in length, it refers to a purported previous fax, and on the next page is a letter dated February 17, 2012, saying “I hereby fax to you” the respondent’s conviction records. Again, as to this issue, the respective claims by the respondent and prior counsel are in contradiction.
 
The respondent also asserts that prior counsel advised him that court appearances by his witnesses were not necessary on March 28, 2012, on the basis of a request for a continuance. See Respondent’s Motion at 6. In this regard, the respondent has provided affidavits from these witnesses stating their understanding that prior counsel had indicated that they need not attend the March 28, 2012, hearing. However, prior counsel disputes this claim vigorously in his responses, asserting that he would never advise a client in this way because he could never know that an Immigration Judge would in fact grant a continuance. Again, the facts on the issue are in dispute.
 
Under these circumstances, we conclude that for purposes of the instant appeal and motion, the respondent has met his burden of establishing a prima facie ineffective assistance of counsel claim, requiring a remand to the Immigration Judge to determine the truth in regard to the various issues and claims by both the respondent and prior counsel. In this regard, on remand, the Immigration Judge should develop the record and find facts pertinent to the ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The Immigration Judge should also consider the respondent’s arguments on appeal that he misstated the respondent’s criminal record in denying voluntary departure. In this regard, we also note that it is incumbent on the Immigration Judge to balance all of the favorable and unfavorable facts in assessing the respondent’s moral character and in exercising discretion.
 
*3 Accordingly, the motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD

Although the employment letter is apparently not on letterhead and there is no explicit indication of the title of the person who signed the document, there is a business address at the bottom of the letter and the notation “RN ADON” appears next to the signatory’s name, which may indicate a title and/or position.

MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:

APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The pending timely filed motion seeking reconsideration of the Board’s January 15, 2014, decision dismissing the respondent’s appeal is denied.1 To the extent the pending motion rests on previously raised issues related to the respondent’s assertion that his conviction for aggravated battery in violation of K.S.A. 21-3414(a)(2)(A), does not qualify as an aggravated felony crime of violence, and that the Immigration Judge improperly denied his request for a continuance to pursue post-conviction relief based on the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has not identified any factual or legal error warranting further consideration of either the Immigration Judge’s or the Board’s decisions in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (discussing the requirements for motions to reconsider); cf. Larin-Ulloa v. Gonzales, 462 F.3d 456, 465-66 (5th Cir. 2006) (finding that the Kansas offense of aggravated battery was a crime of violence under 18 U.S.C. § 16(b), despite the fact that physical force need not be used in all cases, because the risk of the intentional use of force is inherent in the crime). We do not find the respondent’s asserted disagreement with or disapproval of the outcome of the Board’s previous decision sufficient to demonstrate either that the Board improperly evaluated or disregarded the facts and evidence presented or committed legal error. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.2
 
Neil P. Miller

2014 WL 1652412 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FIDEL MARTINEZ MONTES
File: AXXX XX8 667 - Orlando, FL
April 9, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Neil St. John Rambana, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John Gihon
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the January 10, 2013, Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings which had been conducted in absentia on November 4, 2008. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews findings of fact by an Immigration Judge under the clearly erroneous standard of review, and may review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent filed a motion to reopen in absentia proceedings on December 13, 2012, alleging ineffective assistance by former counsel Mr. James Tipler. The Immigration Judge denied the motion, noting that the record reflected that the respondent was represented by Mr. Jeffery Toney in these immigration proceedings.
 
A motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1273-74 (11th Cir. 2005) (quoting Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).
 
On appeal, the respondent has proffered new evidence pertaining to his allegation that Mr. Jeffery Toney provided ineffective legal assistance. However, the U.S. Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case lies, has held that an alien seeking to show ineffective assistance must meet each of the three screening requirements of Lozada, even where counsel’s ineffective assistance was clear on the record. See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222-23 (11th Cir. 2003). While the respondent included an affidavit from his wife on appeal, detailing his former attorney’s allegedly ineffective representation, thus satisfying the first procedural requirement of Lozada, he failed to comply with Lozada’s second and third prongs. The respondent did not submit, in support of his motion to reopen, any information to the Immigration Judge or to the Board on appeal, showing that he had notified Mr. Toney of the allegations and that a complaint has been filed with the relevant Florida disciplinary authorities.1 Id. Therefore, reopening is not warranted as the respondent failed to comply sufficiently with Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Although the respondent proffered letters from the Florida State Attorney’s office indicating that the respondent was victimized by Mr. Tipler, and that Mr. Toney was associated with Mr. Tipler, there is no indication that the respondent has filed a complaint with the Florida Bar against Mr. Toney.

2014 WL 1652386 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMESH PATEL
File: AXXX XX4 135 - Harlingen, TX
April 7, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pierre I. Eloi, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Gabriel A. Couriel
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the March 18, 2013, Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings which had been conducted in absentia on October 3, 2011. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews findings of fact by an Immigration Judge under the clearly erroneous standard of review, and may review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent filed a motion to reopen in absentia proceedings on November 26, 2012,1 alleging ineffective assistance by his former counsel.2 The Immigration Judge denied the motion, noting, inter alia, that the respondent’s former counsel appeared at the October 3, 2011, hearing and stated that he had notified the respondent of his hearing. The Immigration Judge further found that the respondent did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel and he had failed to comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
Here, the Notice of Hearing (NOH) was mailed to the respondent’s former counsel (Exh. 3), and he appeared at the October 3, 2011, hearing. Notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a).
 
Although the respondent asserts that his absence from the hearing was attributable to the ineffectiveness of his former attorney, as noted by the Immigration Judge, he has not alleged or shown that he complied with any of the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496 (5th Cir. 2000); Soadjede v. Ashcroft, 324 F.3d 830, 833 (5th Cir. 2003). See also Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644 (5th Cir. 2014) (holding that the requirement, for raising claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, that counsel have been informed of the allegations and allowed to respond, is only met when counsel is informed of the charge before allegations of ineffective assistance are presented to Board).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent argues that his case was not properly analyzed under Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008). We find no merit to this contention. Notice was properly provided to the respondent’s counsel. His counsel indicated that he had notified the respondent. Thus, it is the respondent’s burden to establish his former counsel provided ineffective assistance, and he has failed to do so in accordance with Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise Manuel
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JATINDER SINGH
File: AXXX XX2 930 - Harlingen, TX
March 31, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Genet Getachew, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 11, 2013. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on July 11, 2011. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On September 23, 2010, the Notice to Appear (NTA) was personally served on the respondent. The NTA advised the respondent of the consequences of failing to appear for his removal hearing. On October 29, 2010, the respondent was released from the custody of the Department of Homeland Security. Upon his release, the respondent provided the Immigration Court with an address in New York at which he could be served. On November 30, 2010, a Notice of Hearing (NOH) was mailed to the respondent’s counsel of record. The NOH notified the respondent that he was scheduled to appear for a hearing on July 11, 2011.
 
On April 4, 2011, the respondent’s former counsel, Norma Sepulveda, Esquire, moved to withdraw from this matter. In the motion, Ms. Sepulveda stated that she had sent a copy of the NOH to the address the respondent had provided in New York, and she had called the respondent at the phone number he had provided. Despite these attempts, Ms. Sepulveda was unable to contact the respondent.
 
On July 11, 2011, the respondent did not appear for his hearing. On that date, the Immigration Judge granted Ms. Sepulveda’s motion to withdraw and ordered the respondent removed in absentia. On February 15, 2013, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings and rescind the in absentia order. The Immigration Judge denied the motion and this appeal followed.
 
The respondent had proper notice of the removal hearing pursuant to sections 239(a)(2), (c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229(a)(2), (c), because the NOH was mailed to his former counsel, Ms. Sepulveda. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 259 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). Further, the respondent’s motion is untimely because it was filed more than 180 days after the in absentia order was entered. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i).
 
*2 The respondent nevertheless argues that the 180-day deadline is subject to equitable tolling due to ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Specifically, the respondent asserts that Ms. Sepulveda did not inform him of the July 11, 2011, hearing. The respondent also argues that equitable tolling is warranted because, after he learned of the in absentia order, the respondent hired an attorney1 to file a motion to reopen proceedings on his behalf, but this attorney never filed the motion.
 
However, the respondent’s motion does not demonstrate that he has informed Ms. Sepulveda, or the attorney who promised to file a motion to reopen on his behalf, of their alleged defective representation and allowed them to respond. Id. at 639. The motion merely contains certified mail receipts of correspondence sent to both attorneys. The motion does not contain what was actually sent to these attorneys. Similarly, the respondent has not shown that he lodged a complaint with disciplinary authorities regarding former counsel or the attorney hired to file the motion to reopen. Id. The motion only contains a certified mail receipt of correspondence sent to the Chief Disciplinary Counsel in Austin, Texas. The motion does not contain what was sent to this office. As a consequence, the respondent has not established that he has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. See Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647-48 (5th Cir. 2014) (holding that an alien must strictly comply with the Lozada requirements). We therefore find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s motion is untimely because the respondent has not shown that equitable tolling is warranted in this instance. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. Further, the respondent’s motion does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant reopening his proceedings under either the Immigration Judge’s or this Board’s sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD

This attorney did not represent the respondent during his removal proceedings, and she is not representing the respondent before the Board.

IN RE: FELIPE GOMEZ-ALONZO
File: AXXX XX4 055 - Chicago, EL
March 26, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Royal F. Berg, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Colleen Peppard
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated November 21, 2013. The Immigration Judge denied as untimely the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. On appeal, the respondent urges that his motion was timely, and further that he established exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear at his hearing. The Department of Homeland Security submitted a response to the respondent’s appeal, agreeing with the respondent’s appellate argument that his motion was timely, and indicating its non-opposition to a remand for the Immigration Judge to consider whether the respondent established exceptional circumstances. The DHS further urged that the respondent had not established exceptional circumstances. We will sustain the appeal and remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further fact-finding.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion as untimely, inasmuch as he found that the motion was filed more than 180 days after his in absentia order. However, the record reflects that the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed on October 31, 2013, less than 180 days after his May 7, 2013, in absentia order, and he raised a claim of exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Accordingly, we conclude that the respondent’s motion is timely, and we will vacate the Immigration Judge’s decision finding otherwise.
 
While the respondent demonstrated that he timely filed the present motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia removal order, he must also demonstrate that his failure to appear at his hearing was because of exceptional circumstances, which may include ineffective assistance of counsel. See id. The respondent asserted in his motion that his attorney1 received the Notice of Hearing of the May 7, 2013, hearing date; however, his attorney did not inform him of his hearing date.2 The Immigration Judge, however, did not make any findings of fact with respect to whether the respondent established exceptional circumstances. Given our limited fact-finding ability on appeal, we will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for consideration of whether the respondent established exceptional circumstances to explain his failure to appear at his May 7, 2013, hearing. See Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994) (finding that an Immigration Judge must fully explain the reasons for denying a motion in order to allow the respondent a fair opportunity to contest the decision and the Board an opportunity for meaningful appellate review); see also Matter of A-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 468, 477 (BIA 1999). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained and the Immigration Judge’s November 21, 2013, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: These removal proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further consideration of the respondent’s motion to reopen consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Elise Manuel

2014 WL 1401606 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JORGE LUIS ALVARADO LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX0 007 - Los Angeles, CA
March 21, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF APPLICANT:
 
Jose Alfredo Hernandez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On December 17, 2013, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his application for adjustment of status. On January 23, 2014, the respondent moved the Board to reopen the proceedings. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent seeks reopening for further consideration of his application for adjustment of status based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against David M. Paz Soldan, the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). The motion will be denied.
 
A party who seeks to reopen proceedings to pursue relief previously considered bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992). The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has stated that an inquiry into whether counsel provided ineffective assistance must begin by determining whether competent counsel would have acted otherwise before examining whether an alien was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective actions of counsel. See Maravilla-Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004). In addition, “substantial prejudice” must be established and such prejudice means that an alien must show that the alleged ineffectiveness may have affected the outcome of proceedings. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004), amended 404 F.3d 1105 (9th Cir. 2005); Lata v. I.N.S., 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
During his removal hearing, the respondent sought adjustment of status based on a visa petition filed on his behalf in 1991, and approved in 1992. At the hearing, however, the Department of Homeland Security introduced evidence that by 1997 the visa petition been terminated pursuant to section 203(g) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1153(g). See Exhibit 13. Inasmuch as a visa was not immediately available to the respondent, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s application for relief. See section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a).
 
*2 In the instant motion, the respondent claims that Attorney Paz Soldan, whom he engaged in 2008, did not properly argue that the termination of the visa was improper because the respondent did not receive notice of the visa’s termination.1
 
As we emphasized in our prior decision, arguments regarding the termination of a visa under section 203(g) of the Act are not reviewable in removal proceedings. The respondent has not cited to any authority that holds that section 203(g) rulings are reviewable in removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R.§§ 245.2(a)(1), (a)(5)(ii); Singh v. Clinton, 618 F.3d 1085 (9th Cir. 2010); Zixiang Li v. Kerry, 710 F.3d 995 (9th Cir. 2013); Matter of Aurelio, 19 I&N Dec. 458 (BIA 1987). Therefore, regardless of whether the termination of the visa was improper, a visa was not immediately available to the respondent at the time of his removable hearing. Hence, the respondent could not establish prima facie eligibility for the relief. Under the circumstances, the fact that Attorney Paz Soldan did not argue that the visa’s termination was improper did not constitute prejudicial error as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel in these removal proceedings.2 See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, supra.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

It appears that the Department of State notified the respondent’s former attorney, Charles T. Hindley, Esquire, of the visa’s termination.

2

Insofar at the respondent alleges that Attorney Paz Soldan failed to properly prepare for the removal hearing, we note that the Immigration Judge described the respondent’s case for adjustment of status as “well-documented,” and noted that the respondent (through counsel) objected to the introduction of the evidence of the visa’s termination. See Immigration Judge’s Decision at 2.

IN RE: ANGELA REYES RODRIGUEZ DE JESUS

APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rosaura Gonzalez Rucci, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jose Rivera
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent, a native of the Dominican Republic and a citizen of the Netherlands Antilles, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, mailed July 20, 2012, denying her motion to reconsider. The Department of Homeland Security is opposed to the respondent’s appeal. The record will remanded.
 
On March 7, 2012, the respondent appeared before the Immigration Judge and was granted the privilege of voluntarily departing the United States on or before July 5, 2012. See section 240B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c. On April 9, 2012, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen claiming ineffective assistance of counsel with regard to the processing of an immigrant visa petition her husband had filed on her behalf.1 The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen in a decision mailed on April 24, 2012. On May 22, 2012, the respondent moved for reconsideration. The Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider is the subject of the present appeal.
 
A motion to reconsider is an alien’s opportunity to specify the errors of law or fact in the previous order and not an opportunity to present new evidence, nor an opportunity to reiterate or expand upon arguments that have been previously raised and considered. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). While the Act, vis-à-vis a motion to reopen, allows an alien who is subject to an order of removal to present new evidence, the respondent used her single opportunity to present new evidence when she filed her initial motion to reopen on April 9, 2012. See section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A). As such, to the extent that the respondent attached evidence to her motion to reconsider and has presented evidence to this Board on appeal, we conclude that the presentation of said evidence subsequent to the filing of the respondent’s motion to reopen is number-barred. See Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000).
 
While the presentation of additional evidence is number-barred, we conclude that it is appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge in order to further consider the merits of the respondent’s motion to reconsider. In denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider, the Immigration Judge found no legal or factual error in her prior conclusion that the respondent had not complied with the evidentiary standards set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Under Matter of Lozada, a motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not.
 
*2 Here, in support of reopening, the respondent presented a personal affidavit, a copy of a complaint letter addressed to the Supreme Court of the Virgin Islands, and, through counsel, indicated that the complaint letter had been filed and her former counsel had been informed of the complaint being filed. In her motion to reconsider, the respondent identified evidence that she had previously offered in support of reopening and argued that her motion to reopen substantially complied with Lozada’s evidentiary requirements. The Immigration Judge, in denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider, faulted her for not presenting evidence that the complaint letter had been actually filed and questioned whether it was proper to file a letter with the Supreme Court of the Virgin Islands (as opposed Virgin Islands Bar Association) (I.J. at 1).
 
The Lozada requirements are meant to serve as a threshold and a screening mechanism to help Immigration Judges and this Board assess the substantial number of ineffective assistance claims that they receive, but need not be rigidly enforced where their purpose is fully served by other means. Fadiga v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 488 F.3d 142, 156 (3d Cir. 2007). “[O]nly in rare circumstances have courts refused to reopen immigration proceedings solely because [an alien] failed to file a bar complaint.” Xu Yong Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 134 (3rd Cir. 2001). However, in the present case, the Immigration Judge appears to have concluded that the respondent was required to strictly comply with Lozada in order to have her claims considered. As the respondent need only establish that the evidentiary requirements set forth in Lozda have been fully served by other means, we will remand the record for the Immigration Judge to further consider the issue of whether the respondent has sufficiently complied with the threshold evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada such that the underlying merits of her ineffective assistance of counsel claims should be considered.
 
In her decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen, the Immigration Judge also held that the respondent had not established that the result in this case would have been different if the errors had not occurred. See Fadiga v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 159 (holding that an alien claiming ineffective assistance of counsel in removal proceedings must show that there was a “““reasonable likelihood that the result would have been different if the claimed error had not occurred”); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 638 (“One must show, moreover, that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance.”). However, the Immigration Judge did not address this prior holding in her decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider. We observe that, in support of her claim that she was prejudiced by her former counsel, the respondent argued in her motion to reconsider that her motion to reopen contained approximately 70 pages of evidence concerning the bona fides of her marriage and said evidence was not considered by the Immigration Judge in her decision denying the motion to reopen. As such, upon remand, the Immigration Judge should, among other things, further determine whether the respondent demonstrated legal or factual error in her prior holding that the respondent was not prejudiced by her former counsel.
 
*3 For the reasons set forth above, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s motion to reconsider.2
 
Elise Manuel
FOR THE BOARD

See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(b)(1)(iii), (e)(1).

2

The respondent remains subject to removal from the United States pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s alternate order of removal, entered on March 7, 2012, to the Netherlands Antilles or the Dominican Republic.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Susan Church, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s October 29, 2013, decision denying his request for a continuance and ordering him removed from the United States. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This case was last before the Board on September 11, 2013, when we dismissed as untimely the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s July 1, 2013, decision finding him removable as charged and denying his request for a continuance. The record reflects that the respondent sought a reissued decision from the Immigration Judge, and the Immigration Judge reissued his July 1, 2013, decision on October 29, 2013. The respondent thereafter filed a timely appeal. On appeal, the respondent contends that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel during his proceedings and that a continuance is warranted to allow him to pursue collateral attacks on his criminal conviction because the lab that was involved in his conviction has been accused of misconduct. Specifically, on July 22, 2010, the respondent was convicted for the offense of distribution of cocaine in Massachusetts (I.J. at 1-2; Exhs. 2 and 3).
 
A successful motion based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy two conditions. First, the claim must comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). Briefly, the Lozada requirements are (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit detailing counsel’s failings, (2) that counsel be informed of the allegations, and (3) that the motion show that disciplinary charges have been filed with the appropriate authority. Id. Second, the alien “must also show that he or she was prejudiced by the actions or inactions of counsel.” See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003).
 
In this case, the respondent contends that prior counsel was ineffective because he failed to appear in Immigration Court on a few occasions, failed to timely file an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s original decision, and failed to pursue relief on behalf of the respondent. See Respondent’s Brief at 2-4 and 8). In support of his assertions, the respondent has submitted a copy of a letter addressed to the Massachusetts Board of Bar Overseers dated February 18, 2014, and a copy of a letter addressed to prior counsel dated February 19, 2014, providing him with a copy of the letter to the Bar Overseers and notifying him that the letter was “scheduled to be sent.” See Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent also asserts that his is an “extreme case” negating the need to show prejudice and that prior counsel should have pursued withholding of removal under section 241(b)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b)(3), and protection under the Convention Against Torture, 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16-.18 (2013), based on the fact that he is the only potential kidney donor for his father. See Respondent’s Brief at 8.
 
*2 Notwithstanding the respondent’s contentions on appeal, we find he has not met his burden in establishing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Specifically, under the circumstances of this case, the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by any actions and/or inactions of prior counsel. See Matter of Assaad, supra. As to the respondent’s contention that prior counsel failed to file a timely appeal resulting in the Board’s dismissal of that appeal on September 11, 2013, we point out that the respondent was able to get the Immigration Judge to reissue his decision and he thereafter timely appealed. Further, as to the respondent’s assertion that prior counsel should have sought withholding of removal and/or protection under the Convention Against Torture, we note that the respondent’s basis for his claims is that he is the only potential kidney donor for his father and this does not form a valid basis for those types of relief and protection. In addition, the record reflects that the respondent was able to obtain a stay of removal after the Immigration Judge reissued his decision on October 29, 2013, in order to seek medical testing. The respondent’s remaining arguments in this regard relate to his prior counsel’s actions during his criminal proceedings; a matter over which we have no jurisdiction. Thus, the respondent has not shown that the result of his prior proceedings would have been any different but for the actions of his prior counsel. See id.
 
The respondent next contends that the Immigration Judge erred in not granting his request for a continuance so that he could pursue a collateral attack on his drug conviction. See Respondent’s Brief at 5-7. The respondent contends that his drug conviction “stems” from malfeasance and misconduct by the laboratory that conducted tests on the evidence in his criminal case. See id. However, the respondent concedes that his motion to set aside his conviction based on these assertions was denied by the criminal court. See id. at 6. While the respondent maintains that he intends to file a motion to reconsider with the criminal court, he has not submitted evidence indicating that he has done so or that his criminal conviction has otherwise been vacated or set aside. See id. As found by the Immigration Judge, the respondent’s criminal conviction remains final for immigration purposes. Under these circumstances, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s request for an additional continuance (I.J. at 2). See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983) (alien must show good cause for continuance).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 1401565 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: MBE OLUIGWE JACKS
File: AXXX XX4 419 - Houston, TX
March 19, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ify J. Ugbala, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John Donovan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 At a hearing held on January 30, 2012, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s request for voluntary departure. The respondent timely filed a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, which the Immigration Judge denied on March 23, 2012. The respondent’s appeal from that decision will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen, for the reasons set forth in her decision. Although the respondent complied with the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), he did not establish that his former attorney’s actions were prejudicial to his case. See id. at 640; see also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 167 (5th Cir. 2006); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). At the time of his final hearing, the respondent was not prima facie eligible for any form of relief aside from voluntary departure. United States Citizenship and Immigration Services had denied the visa petition filed on the respondent’s behalf by his United States citizen wife; the Immigration Judge did not have jurisdiction over any appeal from that decision. Moreover, as the Immigration Judge noted, the pendency of any such appeal would not have established good cause for a further continuance of the respondent’s removal proceedings (I.J. at 2). Insofar as the respondent asserts on appeal that his former attorney erred by failing to file a motion to terminate proceedings, we note that in fact his former attorney did file such a motion on September 13, 2011, and it was denied on the merits by the Immigration Judge. In light of the foregoing, as the respondent was not shown to have experienced actual prejudice by his previous attorney’s actions, we agree with the Immigration Judge that he did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel so as to warrant reopening of his proceedings. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640; Matter of Assaad, supra.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
*2 FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JOSE CRISTIAN ARGUETA-MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX7 964 - Houston, TX
March 14, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Julie Goldberg, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, mailed December 13, 2013, denying his motion to reopen. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand.
 
Due to the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, we conclude that the record should be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of his claims to reopening. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In particular, upon remand, the Immigration Judge should further consider (1) whether the respondent has now established that rescission of the in absentia order of removal, entered on December 14, 2005, is warranted on account of lack of notice, (2) whether he has now established that changed country conditions in El Salvador warrant reopening in order to allow him to pursue an Application for Asylum and for Withholding of Removal (Form I-589), and (3) whether he has now established that sua sponte reopening is warranted. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(ii), (c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii), (c)(7)(C)(ii); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). While we conclude that the respondent has established that the Immigration Judge should have an additional opportunity to consider his claims to reopening, we express no opinion regarding the underlying merits of his claims to reopening at the present time. The following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is granted and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s motion to reopen and the entry of a new decision.1
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

However, as the respondent has presented a claim to reopening on the basis of lack of notice, his removal shall be stayed pending disposition of the motion by the Immigration Judge. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act.

2014 WL 1278460 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE EDUARDO MONTOYA-RENDEROS A.K.A. JOSE E. RENDEROS A.K.A. JOSE EDUARD MONTOYA-RENDERO
File: AXXX XX0 672 - Adelanto, CA
March 7, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Wayne Spindler, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 24, 2013. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was charged with being removable for being present in the United States without having been admitted or paroled (Exh. 1). The respondent (through counsel) admitted the factual allegations in the Notice to Appear and conceded removability as charged (Tr. at 7). The respondent requested no relief from removal. He requested only pre-conclusion voluntary departure, and the Immigration Judge granted that relief (Tr. at 8-10).
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in not considering him for Temporary Protected Status (TPS) and for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture.1 The respondent argues that, despite his express concession of ineligibility for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture, and despite his express agreement to forgo all relief, he wished to be considered for relief from removal.
 
First, we note that, at the hearing, the respondent (through counsel) indicated that the respondent had no fear of return to El Salvador (Tr. at 8). Second, we note that counsel for the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) advised the Immigration Judge (and the respondent) that he had twice been granted TPS, had it expire in 2003 and 2006, had not thereafter renewed it, and had been convicted of a misdemeanor assault (Tr. at 8).2 In response to this, the respondent did not request TPS or request a continuance to consider applying for TPS. Third, the respondent was present at that hearing and personally indicated that he agreed to waive all applications for relief from removal in order to be granted pre-conclusion voluntary departure (Tr. at 9). We conclude that the waiver of relief is binding on the respondent. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal concession made by counsel is binding on the alien). Cf. Hoodho v. Holder, 558 F.3d 184, 191 (2d Cir. 2009) (holding that facts admitted by a party are “judicial admissions” that bind that party throughout the litigation). Moreover, we note that the respondent does not assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the counsel that is representing the respondent on appeal is different than the counsel that represented the respondent below.

2

An alien is statutorily ineligible for TPS if he has been convicted of any felony or 2 or more misdemeanors. See 8 C.F.R. § 244.4.

2014 WL 1278459 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAFAEL OMAR TORRES MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX1 273 - El Paso, TX
March 7, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Evelyn Espinoza, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lorely Ramirez Mravetz
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Remand; Adjustment of status
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 20, 2012, denying his motion to reopen based on new evidence in support of his application for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255. While the appeal was pending, the respondent submitted a motion to remand to further apply for adjustment of status and based on eligibility for Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (DACA).1 The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief in opposition. The motion will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including the Immigration Judge’s determination of credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen for the reasons stated therein. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
The respondent was placed in removal proceedings under a Notice to Appear dated June 24, 2008. The respondent admitted that he was removable as charged and sought adjustment of status (Tr. at 5). The Immigration Judge granted the respondent five continuances on October 14, 2008; January 6, 2009; February 24, 2009; May 12, 2009; and April 13, 2010, in order to submit his adjustment application (Form I-485) (Tr. at 2, 6, and 9). When granting the last continuance, the Immigration Judge warned the respondent’s counsel that he must submit the “485” no later than April 20, 2010 (Tr. at 9). At his final removal hearing, the respondent submitted an approved I-130 as the derivative beneficiary of a petition filed on behalf of his mother, but did not submit any I-485 (Tr. at 22). His counsel claimed that he was trying to obtain the original I-485 submitted in 2005 and denied in 2006 (Tr. at 22). In a decision dated April 22, 2010 (I.J.#1), the Immigration Judge held that nothing prevented the respondent from submitting an I-485, that there was no “good cause” for any further continuances, and that the respondent should be granted voluntary departure upon posting bond, with an alternate order or removal to Mexico. The respondent appealed.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent submitted the old I-485 and Supplement A to Form I-485 from 2005, which he had obtained since the time of his hearing (Resp. Br. at Tab II). In our decision dated May 5, 2011, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent could have submitted a new I-485 at his prior hearing, and we further affirmed the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent did not establish “good cause” for a continuance (B.I.A. at 1). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. We also declined to consider the respondent’s additional evidence or to remand the case, because the respondent did not establish that the additional evidence was material and was not available and could not have been presented at the prior hearing (B.I.A. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). However, we remanded the case to the Immigration Judge for the limited purpose of granting a new period of voluntary departure and to provide the required voluntary departure advisals (B.I.A. at 2).
 
In an order dated October 4, 2011, the Immigration Judge provided the required advisals and granted a new 60-day period of voluntary departure. Just prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen accompanied by a new I-485 based on the same approved I-130 submitted during the respondent’s prior removal hearing.2
 
In a decision dated January 20, 2012 (I.J.#2), the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen to submit a new I-485, because the respondent did not establish that the new evidence “is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(v). (I.J.#2 at 1-2). The respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen to submit a new I-485 is now the subject of this decision.
 
The respondent, who has been represented by counsel throughout these proceedings, indicated in his Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) that he intended to file a separate written brief or statement. However, the respondent did not file such a brief or statement by the deadline of April 6, 2012, set by this Board, nor did the respondent reasonably explain his failure to do so. The respondent’s appeal is therefore subject to summary dismissal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(E).
 
Even if we were to consider the respondent’s appeal, we would nonetheless dismiss the appeal. The respondent states in his Form EOIR-26 that the Immigration Judge erred in concluding that the respondent did not present new facts and material evidence that were not available and could not have presented at his last master hearing, because his “originally submitted I-485 and Supplement A to Form I-485...constituted new and material evidence...” (EOIR-26 at 2).
 
*3 The respondent has misstated the contents of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 20, 2012, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen dated December 2, 2011. The respondent’s motion to reopen dated December 2, 2011, sought consideration of a new I-485 dated September 23, 2011. The respondent had not previously submitted a new I-485 (although the Immigration Judge held and we previously affirmed that he could have). Thus, the Immigration Judge’s decision, which is the subject of this appeal, properly considered only the new I-485 attached to the respondent’s motion to reopen, not the originally submitted I-485 and Supplement A to Form I-485 from 2005, as suggested by the respondent in his EOIR-26.
 
On appeal, the respondent has not meaningfully challenged the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 20, 2012, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen dated December 2, 2011, which sought reopening to consider the respondent’s new evidence of an I-485 dated September 23, 2011. Even if the respondent mentioned the new I-485 on appeal, we would agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not show that the new I-485 was material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing (I.J.#2 at 1). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(v)(3). The respondent has never challenged, and in fact acknowledged, the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that he could have submitted a new I-485 at his removal hearing, but did not do so (I.J.#1 at 3; B.I.A. at 1; Tr. at 22; Resp. Motion at 3). Moreover, the respondent did not establish that the new I-485 submitted along with his motion to reopen “could not have been...presented at the former hearing.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(v)(3).
 
To the extent that the respondent claims that his originally submitted I-485 and Supplement A to Form I-485 from 2005 is new and material, we point out: 1) as discussed above, that it was not the subject of the Immigration Judge’s decision currently on appeal; and 2) that the respondent already submitted the original I-485 and Supplement A from 2005 when he first appealed to this Board in 2010 (Resp. Br. at Tab II). We have already ruled in our decision dated May 5, 2011, that the evidence submitted with the respondent’s first appeal was not “new and previously unavailable” (B.I.A. at 2). We consider our prior decision, finding that the original I-485 and Supplement A to Form I-485 from 2005 is not material evidence that was unavailable and could not have been presented, to continue to govern this case. Therefore, we decline to revisit this issue.
 
*4 In summary, neither the respondent’s originally submitted I-485 from 2005, nor the respondent’s new I-485 from 2011, is material and was unavailable and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(v)(3). Therefore, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We now turn to the respondent’s motion to remand submitted to this Board while his appeal was pending. To the extent that the respondent’s motion to remand is again for the purpose of remanding to further consider the respondent’s application for adjustment of status as the derivative beneficiary of an I-130 filed on behalf of his mother, the motion to remand is denied, because the respondent’s right to apply for such relief was fully explained and an opportunity to apply was afforded at the original removal hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Also, the relief of adjustment of status is not sought on the basis of circumstances that have arisen after the hearing. Id. Rather, adjustment of status is sought for the same reasons as discussed above in our dismissal of the respondent’s appeal.
 
The respondent also seeks a remand for consideration of DACA. The grant of DACA status is a form of prosecutorial discretion exercised by the DHS, not by this Board or the Immigration Judge. DACA status may be granted even if a final order of removal exists. The respondent has not demonstrated any relief that may be granted by this Board or the Immigration Court. Therefore, the respondent’s motion to remand will be dismissed.
 
Effective January 20, 2009, an Immigration Judge who grants an alien voluntary departure must advise the alien that proof of posting of a bond with the Department of Homeland Security must be submitted to the Board of Immigration Appeals within 30 days of filing an appeal, and that the Board will not reinstate a period of voluntary departure in its final order unless the alien has timely submitted sufficient proof that the required bond has been posted. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(3). See Matter of Gamero, 25 I&N Dec. 164 (BIA 2010). The Immigration Judge provided the respondent with the required advisals and granted the respondent a 60-day voluntary departure period, conditioned upon the posting of a $500 bond (I.J. Order dated October 4, 2011). The record before the Board, however, does not reflect that the respondent submitted timely proof of having paid that bond. Therefore, the voluntary departure period will not be reinstated, and the respondent will be removed from the United States pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s alternate order. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*5 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed and the motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s order granting voluntary departure, dated October 4, 2011, is vacated, and the alternative order of removal is in effect.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

The respondent has subsequently submitted a notice of receipt by the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) for his Consideration of Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (Form I-797C).

2

In the motion to reopen dated December 2, 2011, the respondent’s counsel acknowledges that he may have erred, presumably in not submitting a new I-485 at his original hearing; however, the respondent never claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel (Resp. Motion at 3).
