2014 WL 1278443 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RENE RODRIGUEZ A.K.A. RENE DELGADO RODRIGUEZ
File: AXXX XX8 066 - Arlington, Virginia
February 27, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lynsay Gott, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ian D. Fiske
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s May 20, 2013, written decision which denied his motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has requested summary affirmance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgment and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993).
 
Following a hearing conducted in absentia on June 23, 2009, at which the respondent failed to appear and the DHS presented evidence, an Immigration Judge found him subject to removal as charged, determined that he had abandoned all potential applications for relief, and ordered him removed from the United States. See generally section 240 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a.
 
On December 4, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen in which he alleged that his prior counsel had failed to notify him about the June 23, 2009, hearing. He claimed that he had difficulty communicating with his attorney in the time period preceding the hearing, and that he did not understand what his attorney’s assistant meant when she told him that his case had been “closed” because he failed to attend the hearing when she contacted him shortly after the June 2009 hearing. The respondent also alleged that his attorney’s representation was deficient in other respects, and that he had committed ineffective assistance of counsel, although he declined to file a bar complaint because of his “limited financial capacity.” He attached a letter from his prior attorney dated October 10, 2012, in which the attorney reiterated his statement made to the Immigration Judge at the June 23, 2009, hearing that he had spoken with the respondent 1 week before to remind him about the hearing (Exh. S). Finally, he attached a copy of an informal admonition issued by the District of Columbia bar counsel against this attorney in 2003 in connection with an unrelated immigration matter (Exh. T).
 
*2 The DHS filed a response in opposition to the motion to reopen, noting that the respondent had failed to file a bar complaint and had failed to exercise due diligence to establish ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the motion on May 20, 2013, based upon the reasons set forth in the DHS opposition, notably the respondent’s failure to comply with the standards for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and his “failure to take action for several years” (I.J. at 1).
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that he filed a bar complaint against his prior attorney on May 30, 2013, which was 10 days after the Immigration Judge’s decision, and that he also filed a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge. However, the record does not contain any evidence that the respondent filed a motion to reconsider, and he has not attached a copy of his bar complaint.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent has not established that his absence at the scheduled hearing was the result of a lack of notice because his attorney received proper notice of the hearing. Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996) (alien seeking to reopen in absentia proceedings based upon her unsuccessful communications with attorney about hearing date did not establish exceptional circumstances where she failed to satisfy all of requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (notice to alien’s counsel constitutes notice to alien); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.26(c)(2), 1292.5(a).
 
Turning to the respondent’s request for equitable tolling, it is not clear that the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit, the circuit in which this case arises, would apply equitable tolling in these circumstances. In addition, he does not cite any applicable Fourth Circuit precedent on this issue either in his motion or on appeal. However, in other circuits which have applied equitable tolling when an alien has demonstrated ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien is required to exercise due diligence, which has not been established on this record. See, e.g., Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006); Bead v. Holder, 703 F.3d 591 (1st Cir. 2013); Neves v. Holder, 613 F.3d 30, 36 (1st Cir. 2010); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001).
 
*3 Although the respondent claims that he did not understand what his attorney’s assistant meant when she informed him that his case had been “““closed” in June 2009 for failing to attend his hearing, this information was sufficient to alert him to further inquire about the status of his removal proceeding, and his waiting more than 3 and one half years before he filed a motion to reopen does not represent due diligence. In addition, the record does not contain any evidence that he filed a bar complaint as claimed in 2013, and his prior attorney’s conduct in an unrelated immigration case more than 10 years ago does not, without more, establish that he committed ineffective assistance in the respondent’s case.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 1278443 (BIA)
End of Document

2014 WL 1278367 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ENRIQUE LOPEZ TORRES VARGAS A.K.A. ENRIQUE LOPEZ-TORRES A.K.A. ENRIQUE LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX1 657 - Atlanta, GA
February 25, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jordan G. Forsythe, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joy Lampley
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)] - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section (212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I))
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 23, 2012, denying his motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the scheduled hearing on August 18, 2005. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge committed error and abused his discretion by not considering the lack of notice and the availability of adjustment of status to the respondent. The respondent asserts that he did not receive proper notice of his hearing, and that he received ineffective assistance of counsel.1
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear was issued on December 30, 2004, and served personally on the respondent on that date. The respondent appeared for hearings on February 10, 2005, March 17, 2005, and April 7, 2005. During the hearing on April 7, 2005, the respondent received oral and written notice of the next hearing scheduled for June 30, 2005. However, on May 31, 2005, a new notice of hearing was sent to the respondent at his most recent provided address, setting forth the hearing date as August 18, 2005. The Notice to Appear reflects that the respondent received oral and written notice of the consequences of a failure to appear and of the requirement to notify the Immigration Court immediately by using Form EOIR-33 whenever he changed his address or telephone number during the proceeding. Furthermore, during the proceedings on February 10, 2005, March 17, 2005, and April 7, 2005, the respondent was provided oral and written notice of the hearings and of the consequences of a failure to appear. The record reflects that the notice of hearing dated May 31, 2005, was returned to the Atlanta Immigration Court as “““Attempted - Not Known.” The respondent did not appear for the hearing on August 18, 2005, and the Immigration Judge issued an in absentia order of removal. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on May 2, 2012, seeking reopening of the proceedings sua sponte, based on his eligibility for adjustment of status.
 
*2 The Immigration Judge acknowledged that the notice of hearing sent to the respondent’s address was returned “attempted unknown.” However, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent intentionally moved without providing an address to the Immigration Court or the Department of Homeland Security, thus making his whereabouts unknown to the government and thereby rendering the enforcement of the order of removal to Peru difficult, if not impossible. He stated that the respondent has been a fugitive from justice and appeared only after he married a United States citizen and qualified for a benefit.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the record supports the finding that the respondent moved and did not provide his new address to the Immigration Court. The respondent was advised orally and in writing of the consequences of a failure to appear when he was served personally with the Notice to Appear and when he appeared in Immigration Court at three different hearings. Moreover, from the date of his last appearance in court in April 2005, until the filing of the motion to reopen in May 2012, the respondent did not contact the Immigration Court to determine the status of his case, nor did he seek reopening prior to acquiring eligibility for adjustment of status.2 The respondent did not receive notice of his hearing because he failed to comply with his obligation to provide the Immigration Court with current address information, and there was no record evidence submitted by the respondent showing that the address provided was in fact his mailing address and that he had not moved. The respondent has not established lack of proper notice as the notice of hearing was sent to the most recent address provided by the respondent, and he intentionally moved and failed to notify the court of his new address despite repeated warnings of the consequences of a failure to appear.
 
Therefore, the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed untimely whether the respondent is seeking reopening under the rescission provisions pertaining to in absentia orders of removal, or the general motions to reopen provisions. See Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000); section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(4)(ii), 8 C.F.R. 1003.23(b)(1). Subsequent to the filing of the appeal in this matter, on October 26, 2012, the respondent filed the approval notice of an immediate relative visa petition (Form I-130) filed by his spouse, as a supplemental document to the brief on appeal. The approval of a visa petition filed on the respondent’s behalf does not constitute an “exceptional situation” sufficient to warrant reopening of the respondent’s removal proceedings pursuant to the Board’s sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1977) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship).
 
*3 The respondent also contends on appeal that that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “““exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the alien has satisfied the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). However, the respondent has not complied with any of the Lozada requirements, nor has he submitted any evidence in support of his claim that he exercised due diligence in this matter. See Avila-Santoyo, 713 F.3d 1357 (11th Cir. 2013) (court held that the 90-day deadline to file a motion to reopen is subject to equitable tolling, and reasoning is equally applicable to the 180-day deadline to seek reopening of an in absentia removal order). Therefore, the respondent has not established that reopening is warranted on this basis. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise Manuel
FOR THE BOARD

However, the record does not contain an asylum application, and the Immigration Judge only mentioned that the respondent indicated at a master calendar hearing on April 7, 2005, that he had no fear of returning to Peru.

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDELMA M MONTEJO TRUJILLO
File: AXXX XX9 654 - Kansas City, MO
February 25, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Subhash Chandra, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jennifer A. May
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s April 16, 2012, decision denying her motion to reopen. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
This case was originally before the Immigration Judge on April 10, 2006, when the respondent was granted voluntary departure. Subsequently, on June 5, 2007, the Board returned the record to the Immigration Court without further action, noting the respondent’s then counsel had waived appeal during the proceedings. On April 5, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge alleging she was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel during her prior proceedings and that her decision to take voluntary departure was not voluntary. On April 16, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen in a terse decision. In particular, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion as untimely, noting that she “was receiving legal advice from an attorney after [her original attorney] whom she complains about.” The Immigration Judge summarily concluded there is “nothing to justify [the respondent’s] six year delay before deciding to file [the] motion.”
 
We find that the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion is insufficient. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (2013) (de novo review). In particular, the Immigration Judge did not specifically address in any meaningful way the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel or that she did not want voluntary departure, and we consequently cannot properly adjudicate the merits of an appeal of that decision. In this regard, the Immigration Judge did not address any of the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and/or whether the respondent met her burden of proof under Lozada. Nor does the Immigration Judge’s decision reflect any consideration of the respondent’s arguments concerning her reasons for not learning of the alleged ineffective assistance of her first attorney until she had contacted a third attorney (current counsel), her reasons for not contacting current counsel sooner, and her reasons for not making a claim under Lozada against the second attorney that she consulted. We cannot uphold the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely where he did not meaningfully address and/or consider her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel which may excuse the untimeliness of the motion. Thus, we find a remand is warranted for the Immigration Judge to fully consider and adjudicate the arguments made in the respondent’s motion to reopen, as well as her accompanying motion for a change of venue, and to issue a new decision.1
 
*2 Accordingly, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

However, we find no merit in the respondent’s contention that jurisdiction to adjudicate her motion to reopen resided in the Immigration Court in Chicago, Illinois, rather than Kansas City, Missouri. The record indicates that although an Immigration Judge in Chicago presided over the previous proceedings by televideo, those proceedings were docketed in Kansas City and the parties appeared at the Immigration Court there. Therefore, the Immigration Judge in Kansas City properly adjudicated the respondent’s motion to reopen. The filing instructions to which the respondent cites relate only to the physical location of the Immigration Court with administrative control over the record of proceedings, not to adjudication authority. We recognize that the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit has issued decisions addressing proper venue in proceedings conducted by televideo, but those decisions determined the venue for a petition for review in the court of appeals under section 242(b)(2) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b)(2), rather than the authority to decide a motion to reopen at the administrative agency level. See Cordova-Soto v. Holder, 732 F.3d 789 (7th Cir. 2013); Ramos v. Ashcroft, 371 F.3d 948 (7th Cir. 2004). Nor has the respondent established any prejudice with regard to her apparent contention that the Immigration Judge’s April 16, 2012, decision did not inform her of her right to appeal, inasmuch as she filed a timely appeal of that decision.

Considering the totality of the circumstances, including the lack of a response from the Department of Homeland Security, the respondent’s claims of improprieties during the course of his removal proceedings and ineffective assistance of counsel, and the potential to be granted relief from removal, we conclude that these removal proceedings should be reopened. See section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 122a(c)(7)(C). While the respondent does not dispute that he is subject to removal from the United States because he is an alien who is present in this country without being admitted or paroled by an immigration officer or who arrived at any time or place other than as designated by the Attorney General, we conclude that, upon remand, the respondent should be provided with an additional opportunity to establish that he warrants relief from removal. See section 240(c)(4)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A). Accordingly, the following order is entered.

2014 WL 1278385 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANTIAGO ARDINES GUTIERREZ
File: AXXX XX7 510 - Los Angeles, CA
February 24, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jerry Shapiro, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; termination
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Panama, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s October 25, 2011, decision denying his motion to reconsider a prior order denying his motion to reopen the removal proceedings. After filing his appeal, moreover, the respondent submitted an appellate pleading in which he requests that the proceedings be reopened and the record remanded with a view to the termination of the proceedings in light of intervening law. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion to reopen and remand will be denied.
 
On June 9, 2010, the respondent failed to appear for a scheduled removal hearing after having been notified of the hearing date, and thus the Immigration Judge ordered him removed in absentia based on conviction records proffered by the Department of Homeland Security. Section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5). On January 13, 2011, the respondent moved to reopen the proceedings, alleging that his failure to appear was attributable to exceptional circumstances, including ineffective assistance of counsel. In a decision dated February 25, 2011, the Immigration Judge denied the motion, concluding that it was untimely, that the respondent had received proper notice of his hearing, and that he had not established that his failure to appear was the result of exceptional circumstances beyond his control. On April 25, 2011, the respondent filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) with this Board, seeking to challenge the Immigration Judge’s decision of February 25, 2011, but the Board declined review because the appeal was untimely filed.
 
On August 2, 2011, the respondent filed a second motion in Immigration Court, this time requesting sua sponte reopening or reconsideration and arguing that the 180-day deadline for rescission of in absentia removal orders should be equitably tolled on the ground that he had received ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s second motion in an order dated October 25, 2011, finding that the motion was untimely and that the 180-day filing deadline was not subject to tolling because the respondent had not established any compelling equitable reason for failing to file his first motion to reopen within 180 days after his in absentia order was issued, as required by section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. The respondent now appeals from the Immigration Judge’s October 25, 2011, decision.
 
The only issue properly before us is whether the Immigration Judge erred when he denied the respondent’s August 2, 2011, motion for sua sponte reopening or reconsideration. We discern no error. The motion was untimely because it was filed considerably more than 180 days after entry of the respondent’s in absentia removal order, and the Immigration Judge provided substantial reasons for concluding that equitable tolling of that deadline would be inappropriate. Finally, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that sua sponte reopening is not warranted on the merits, and that such an extraordinary remedy is unavailable to the respondent in any event because he departed the United States in 2011. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (“A motion to reopen or to reconsider shall not be made by or on behalf of a person who is the subject of removal ... proceedings subsequent to his or her departure from the United States.”).1
 
*2 During the pendency of this appeal, the respondent filed what is essentially a third motion to reopen, in which he argues that his removal proceedings must be reopened and terminated based on the Supreme Court’s decision in Vartelas v. Holder, 132 S. Ct. 1479 (2012). That argument is not properly before the Board in the first instance, however, because we have never reviewed the order he seeks to reopen, i.e., the respondent’s June 2010 in absentia removal order. Although we have limited authority to reopen or reconsider our own decisions sua sponte, we do not reopen Immigration Judges’ orders that have never been subject to our review.
 
In light of the foregoing, the following orders shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen and remand is denied.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
DISSENTING OPINION: Anne J. Greer, Board Member
I respectfully dissent.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Panama, was admitted to the United States as an immigrant in 1985, and he has resided in this country as a lawful permanent resident (“LPR”) ever since. In January 1996, the respondent was convicted in California of grand theft, counterfeiting a government seal, and forgery. In 2009, after traveling abroad, the respondent presented himself for inspection at a United States port of entry and requested permission to reenter the United States as a returning LPR. Upon discovering the respondent’s 1996 convictions, however, the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) paroled him into the United States and initiated these removal proceedings, in which he is charged with inadmissibility as an applicant for admission convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude. Sections 101(a)(13)(C)(v) and 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1101(a)(13)(C), 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I).
 
*3 On June 9, 2010, the respondent failed to appear for a scheduled removal hearing, and thus the Immigration Judge ordered him removed in absentia based on conviction records proffered by the DHS. The respondent subsequently moved to reopen the proceedings, alleging that his failure to appear was attributable to exceptional circumstances, including ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge denied reopening on two separate occasions, and the matter is now before us on appeal.
 
After the Immigration Judge rendered his October 2011 decision, the Supreme Court decided Vartelas v. Holder, 132 S. Ct 1479 (2012), which held in relevant part that a returning LPR cannot be charged with inadmissibility pursuant to section 101(a)(13)(C)(v) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(13)(C)(v), solely on the basis of criminal convictions entered before April 1, 1997. After Vartelas, an LPR who is in removal proceedings based on a criminal conviction preceding April 1, 1997, such as the present respondent, may only be charged with inadmissibility under section 212(a) of the Act (as opposed to deportability under section 237(a)) if: (1) he or she is returning to the United States after having made a departure that was not “brief, casual, and innocent” within the meaning of Rosenberg v. Fleuti, 374 U.S. 449 (1963); or (2) such a charge is otherwise authorized by clauses (i)-(iv) or (vi) of section 101(a)(13)(C) of the Act.
 
In light of Vartelas, it appears that the respondent--a returning LPR who was treated as an applicant for admission because of his January 1996 convictions--may have been improperly charged with inadmissibility under section 212(a)(2) of the Act. At a minimum, the conviction documents relied upon by the Immigration Judge to support his June 9, 2010, removal order were not sufficient, by themselves, to establish by clear and convincing evidence that the respondent was an “arriving alien,” as charged in the Notice to Appear. I note in this regard that when the respondent’s in absentia removal order was entered the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit--in whose jurisdiction this case arises--had already issued a precedent decision in accordance with Vartelas. See Camins v. Gonzales, 500 F.3d 872 (9th Cir. 2007).
 
As the respondent’s in absentia removal order is inconsistent with Supreme Court precedent and could not have withstood judicial or administrative scrutiny under the Ninth Circuit law in effect at the time of its issuance, I view the majority’s refusal to reopen the proceedings results as a miscarriage of justice. See Matter of Farinas, 12 I&N Dec. 467, 471-72 (BIA 1967) (finding gross miscarriage of justice where alien’s deportation order, which was valid when entered, became invalid by virtue of controlling circuit precedent issued prior to the execution of the order); Matter of Malone, 11 I&N Dec. 730, 731-32 (BIA 1966) (finding gross miscarriage of justice where alien’s deportation order was clearly not in accord with the law as interpreted at the time of issuance). I would therefore reopen the proceedings on the Board’s own motion and remand the record to the Immigration Court for further development of the Vartelas issue, for additional proceedings--including the lodging of new charges, if appropriate--and for the entry of such further orders as may be necessary.
 
*4 I respectfully dissent from the majority’s decision to the contrary.
 
 
Anne J. Greer
  
Board Member
 
Footnotes

1

We recognize that the “post-departure bar” regulation cannot be applied in a manner that conflicts with an alien’s statutory right to file one motion to reopen. Reyes-Torres v. Holder, 645 F.3d 1073, 1076-77 (9th Cir. 2011). We conclude that Reyes- Torres is not controlling here, however, because the respondent’s August 2, 2011, motion sought sua sponte reopening rather than statutory reopening. As noted, statutory reopening is unavailable to the respondent because his motion was untimely and did not qualify for any exception to the 180-day filing deadline prescribed by section 240(b)(5)(C)(i). Accordingly, application of the post-departure bar in this case does not conflict with the respondent’s statutory rights. See Desai v. Attorney General of U.S., 695 F.3d 267, 270-71 (3d Cir. 2012); Zhang v. Holder, 617 F.3d 650, 661 (2d Cir. 2010); Ovalles v. Holder, 577 F.3d 288, 296-97 (5th Cir. 2009); cf. also Ortega-Marroquin v. Holder, 640 F.3d 814, 819 (8th Cir. 2011). Moreover, as the respondent’s motion to reopen was not based on a showing that he failed to appear for his removal hearing due to a lack of notice, Matter of Bulnes, 25 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 2009), is inapplicable here.

2014 WL 1118477 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JULIO ALEXANDER GUZMAN-VASQUEZ
File: AXXX XX2 033 - El Centro, CA
February 18, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Schecter, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John D. Holliday
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 In a decision dated August 23, 2010, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent--a native and citizen of Guatemala--removed from the United States as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony. See section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii). No appeal was taken from that decision. Three years later, in August 2013, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge in which he claimed that he was not removable as charged and that his due process rights had been violated. The Immigration Judge denied the motion in a decision dated September 30, 2013, and this timely appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
By statute, a motion to reopen removal proceedings must be filed within 90 days after entry of the final administrative order of removal, unless some enumerated exception applies. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i). The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed considerably more than 90 days after his final removal order was entered, and the respondent provided no evidence with the motion to suggest that any statutory exception to the 90-day filing deadline applied. Accordingly, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen.
 
In cases arising within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the 90-day filing deadline for motions to reopen is subject to equitable tolling if the moving party demonstrates that he was prevented from filing a timely motion because of deception, fraud, or error. E.g., Luna v. Holder, 659 F.3d 753, 759 (9th Cir. 2011); Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1185-86 (9th Cir. 2004); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003). Equitable tolling is unavailable here because the respondent’s motion was not supported by any evidence that he was prevented from meeting the 90-day filing deadline due to deception, fraud, or error of another. On the contrary, it appears the respondent was prompted to file his motion by the Ninth Circuit’s precedent decision in Flores-Lopez v. Holder, 685 F.3d 857 (9th Cir. 2012), which was decided long after the 90-day deadline had expired.
 
We also conclude that sua sponte reopening would be inappropriate here because the respondent has not identified any exceptional situation warranting that remedy. See generally Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). In this regard, we conclude that the Ninth Circuit’s decision in Flores-Lopez v. Holder, supra, embodied an incremental change in the law, not a fundamental transformation of it. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999). Further, we reject the respondent’s appellate assertion that his removal order was unlawful when entered, such that a failure to reopen would result in a gross miscarriage of justice. Under our precedents, enforcement of a removal order constitutes a gross miscarriage of justice only if the order clearly could not have withstood judicial scrutiny under the law in effect at the time of its issuance or initial execution. See Matter of Farinas, 12 I&N Dec. 467, 471-72 (BIA 1967); Matter of Malone, 11 I&N Dec. 730, 731-32 (BIA 1966). At the time of the respondent’s 2010 removal hearing there was no precedential authority in the Ninth Circuit or elsewhere which required dismissal of the respondent’s removal charge. Further, the respondent did not dispute his removability and elected to waive his right to appeal.
 
*2 The respondent’s appellate brief also challenges the fairness of the Department of Homeland Security’s (“DHS”) decision to reinstate his 2010 removal order. We express no opinion with respect to that issue. The reinstatement process is committed to the discretion of the DHS, and is not subject to review by this Board. See Matter of W-C-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 118, 122 (BIA 2007); Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281, 287 (BIA 1998). We do note, however, that a reinstated removal order “is not subject to being reopened or reviewed” by the Immigration Judge or this Board. See section 241(a)(5) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(a)(5).
 
Finally, the respondent claims that the California conviction underlying his removal order was invalid because he received ineffective assistance of counsel from his criminal defense lawyer. Such claims are not cognizable in immigration proceedings. E.g., De la Cruz v. INS, 951 F.2d 226, 228 (9th Cir. 1991); Matter of Cuellar, 25 I&N Dec. 850, 854-55 (BIA 2012).
 
In conclusion, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 1118477 (BIA)
End of Document

2014 WL 1118483 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NARINDER SINGH
File: AXXX XX0 918 - Harlingen, TX
February 12, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Garish Sarin, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)] - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s September 12, 2013, decision denying his motion to reopen the removal proceedings and to rescind an in absentia removal order issued against him on December 8, 2011. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of India, concedes that he is removable from the United States by virtue of his unlawful presence (Respondent’s Br. at 3). The respondent was physically present when he was served the Notice To Appear (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). In the interim, the respondent was released from detention on bond. On March 8, 2011, the Immigration Court issued a written notice of the December 8, 2011, hearing to the respondent’s lawyer (I.J. at 1). Two days prior to the respondent’s removal hearing, respondent’s former counsel filed a motion to withdraw as counsel explaining to the Immigration Judge that she had made numerous unsuccessful attempts to communicate with the respondent, had provided the respondent written advisals of his hearing, had exhausted all reasonable efforts to contact the respondent and that the respondent had made no attempt to contact her (I.J. at 1-2; Motion to Withdraw at 2). Concluding that the respondent had failed to appear for his removal hearing despite having received proper notice thereof, the Immigration Judge ordered him removed in absentia. Section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5) (2000); 8 C.F.R. 1292.5(a) (stating that whenever an alien is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to the attorney of record).
 
In August 2013, the respondent filed a motion in Immigration Court requesting reopening of the removal proceedings and rescission of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia removal order. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. In that motion, the respondent asserted that he had not received notice--from either the Immigration Court or his attorney--of the December 8, 2011, hearing, and also claimed that his former lawyer had provided ineffective assistance of counsel by not informing him that he was required to appear for his hearing or notifying him that an in absentia removal order had been issued against him.
 
In a decision dated September 12, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, concluding that the respondent had received proper notice of the December 8, 2011, hearing and also finding that his ineffective assistance of counsel claim was deficient despite the fact that he had substantively complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (I.J. at 1-2).1 The respondent’s counsel filed a motion to withdraw after indicating she had made numerous unsuccessful attempts to communicate with the respondent and had provided the respondent with written advisals of his hearing, but that the respondent “made no attempt to contact Counsel” (I.J. at 1-2; Motion to Withdraw at 2; Respondent’s Motion at 33). The Immigration Judge observed that the respondent does not claim that he attempted to contact his former counsel during this period (I.J. at 2).
 
*2 An in absentia removal order may be rescinded only if the alien files a motion to reopen: (1) within 180 days after the date of the removal order in which he establishes that his failure to appear was because of “exceptional circumstances,” as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act; or (2) at any time in which he establishes that he did not have proper notice of the hearing in which the removal order was entered. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act.
 
The record provides support for the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent had received proper notice--through his counsel of record--of his December 8, 2011, removal hearing. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (recognizing that notice to the attorney of record constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5 (notice of hearing sent to an alien’s attorney of record is proper, sufficient notice). Furthermore, even if the respondent could establish “exceptional circumstances” for his failure to appear, his motion to reopen and rescind was filed after the expiration of the 180-day filing deadline set forth at section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. Lastly, the Immigration Judge concluded, and we agree, that sua sponte reopening was not warranted in the respondent’s case (I.J. at 2). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); see also Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216 (5th Cir. 2008).
 
In light of the foregoing, we conclude that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen the removal proceedings and to rescind his December 8, 2011 2001, in absentia removal order.
 
The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, the jurisdiction of the instant case, has not issued a published decision directly adopting the doctrine of equitable tolling in the context of a motion to reopen in absentia proceedings. Cf. Borges v. Gonzales, 402 F.3d 398 (3d Cir.2005) (finding equitable tolling of the statute of limitations for ineffective assistance of counsel is appropriate). Even assuming equitable tolling was available to the respondent, the Immigration Judge determined that he was not entitled to it (I.J. at 1-2).

2014 WL 1118462 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SECUNDINO MARTINEZ A.K.A. SECUNDINO GUTIERREZ MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX2 564 - San Juan, PR
February 10, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rosaura Gonzalez-Rucci, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The final order of removal in this matter was entered by the Immigration Judge on February 17, 2010. Thereafter, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, which alleged ineffective assistance of counsel by the respondent’s prior attorney. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s subsequent motion to reconsider, and the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen with the Board, again alleging ineffective assistance of counsel and requesting the Board to sua sponte reopen proceedings.
 
The respondent’s current motion is number-barred, was not filed within 90 days of the final order of removal, has not been shown to fall within an exception to the filing requirements, and does not establish any exceptional situation that would warrant reopening sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
The focus of the respondent’s motion is that his former attorney knew that the respondent’s sister had filed a visa petition on the respondent’s behalf in 1995 but did not pursue adjustment of status based on that petition. It is the same claim as the one made in the respondent’s prior motion to reopen. However, the respondent has still not demonstrated that his former attorney’s representation resulted in prejudice because he has still not shown he is eligible for adjustment of status.1 See Muyubisnay-Cungachi v. Holder, 734 F.3d 66 (1st Cir. 2013) (providing that a petitioner bringing an ineffective assistance claim must establish both a deficient performance by counsel and a reasonable probability of prejudice resulting from the former representation); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 640 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The respondent has not submitted documentation that the visa petition filed by his sister was approved.2 The respondent has also not demonstrated that he is eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(i) of the Act. Such a waiver would be necessary in light of findings of fraud concerning the respondent’s marriage. The Immigration Judge’s decisions denying reopening and reconsideration addressed these issues but the respondent’s current motion does not mention them. Because the respondent has not demonstrated eligibility for adjustment of status based on his sister’s visa petition, he has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his former attorney’s failure to pursue this relief. Additionally, he has not demonstrated that sua sponte reopening is warranted.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent appears to seek adjustment under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i) because he was not admitted or paroled after inspection by an immigration officer, a requirement for adjustment under section 245(a) of the Act (Exh. 1).

2

A notice submitted by the respondent from the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services USCIS) confirms that a visa petition on behalf of the respondent was received by USCIS on March 30, 1995. However, the USCIS website still indicates that USCIS has not processed the petition and that there may be a clerical problem involving the petitioner’s address. This issue was noted by the Immigration Judge in her decision denying reconsideration.

2014 WL 1120192 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MANUEL BARRIOS ROJAS
File: AXXX XX5 871 - Seattle, WA
February 7, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bernice Funk, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ryan Kahler
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)] - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude (Withdrawn)
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)] - Controlled substance violation (Withdrawn)
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent moves the Board pursuant to section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7), and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen his removal proceedings to apply for adjustment of status. In our decision dated July 31, 2013, we dismissed his appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 16, 2012, decision which found him removable as charged above, denied his motion for a continuance, but granted him voluntary departure. We also granted him 30 days voluntary departure. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings filed on October 29, 2013, is timely.1 He alleges ineffective assistance of former counsels (Larry W. Smith and Brenda C. Diaz). In Matter of Compean, Bangaly, & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (“Compean II”), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), the Attorney General directed the Board to continue to apply the previously established standards for reviewing motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel (pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
The respondent meets the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to allege ineffective assistance of Ms. Diaz. He presents his affidavit and a copy of the complaint filed against Ms. Diaz (in which counsel states that she notified Ms. Diaz of the complaint) [Motion Exhs. B, E]. We have no authority to consider the ineffective assistance of counsel claim against Mr. Smith, whose actions allegedly led to the issuance of the Notice to Appear (Form I-862) against the respondent. Cf. Matter of Compean II, supra, at 3 (concluding that the Board’s discretion to reopen removal proceedings includes the power to consider claims of ineffective assistance of counsel based on conduct of counsel that occurred after a final order of removal had been entered).
 
*2 The respondent shows prejudice. See generally Correa-Rivera v. Holder, 706 F.3d 1128, 1133 (9th Cir. 2013) (prejudice will be found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings). The respondent was found removable under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), based on two convictions. Section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act provides, in pertinent part, that any alien who at any time after admission has been convicted of a violation of any law of a State relating to a controlled substance (as defined in section 102 of the Controlled Substances Act (21 U.S.C. § 802)), other than a single offense involving possession for one’s own use of 30 grams or less of marijuana, is deportable.
 
The first removability conviction is a March 4, 1991, conviction in a California criminal court for using or being under the influence of any specified controlled substance (Exh. 2). Ms. Diaz did not challenge removability based on this conviction (Tr. at 18-19). However, the record of conviction does not identify the controlled substance involved. See Ruiz-Vidal v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 1072, 1076-77 (9th Cir. 2007) (holding that conviction under the California possession statute was not a categorical controlled substance offense because California regulates the possession and sale of many substances not covered by the Controlled Substances Act). Removability has not been shown on the present record.
 
The respondent’s second removability conviction is an October 28, 1993, conviction in a California criminal court for possession of marijuana more than 28.5 grams (Exh. 2). However, the record of conviction does not establish whether the amount involved was for 30 grams or less, or for more than 30 grams. See Rodriguez v. Holder, 619 F.3d 1077, 1079 (9th Cir. 2010); see also Medina v. Ashcroft, 393 F.3d 1063, 1065 n.5 (9th Cir. 2005) (the government bears the burden of establishing that an alien’s conviction does not fall within the exception for possession of 30 grams or less of marijuana). Removability has not been shown on the present record.2 We conclude that the respondent shows prejudice.
 
In sum, the respondent meets the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, and shows prejudice. Because his motion to reopen is timely filed, due diligence is not an issue. We will grant the motion to reopen and remand the record to the Immigration Judge for new determinations on removability under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act and eligibility for adjustment of status.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, and the proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

1

Because the respondent filed a motion to reconsider on August 29, 2013, which was prior to the expiration of the 30-day voluntary departure period we granted in our July 31, 2013, decision [Aug. 30, 2013], the grant of voluntary departure was automatically terminated, and the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d), shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).

2

If upon remand the respondent is found to have two or more controlled substance offenses, he will not be eligible for the personal use exception. See Rodriguez v. Holder, supra (the personal use exception does not apply to an alien with more than one drug conviction).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO JAVIER PACHECO PACHECO
File: AXXX XX6 445 - Omaha, NE
February 7, 2014Z
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lauren Schmoke, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Karen E. Lundgren
Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(5)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(5)(A)(i)] - No valid labor certification
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 17, 2013. The record reflects that following the respondent’s failure to appear for a scheduled hearing on February 22, 2012, the Immigration Judge issued a written decision, ordering him removed in absentia. On March 8, 2013, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings and rescind the in absentia order, alleging that he failed to appear for the hearing due to a lack of notice (I.J. at 1; Respondent’s Motion at 2-3 and Declaration). Following the Immigration Judge’s April 17, 2013, denial of the respondent’s motion, the respondent filed a timely appeal with the Board. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact by the Immigration Judge for clear error, while all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, are reviewed de novo. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i)-(ii) (2014). Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act. See also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161, 165 (BIA 2013).
 
First, the respondent’s motion states that he attended two prior hearings and he therefore had no reason not to appear for the February 22, 2012, hearing (Respondent’s Motion at 2). The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent appeared telephonically with his former attorney for the prior master calendar hearings (i.e., on July 6, 2011, and October 19, 2011) and that both he and his former attorney were notified of the February 22, 2012, hearing orally and by a written notice that was mailed to the attorney’s office (I.J. at 1). See Notice of Hearing, dated October 19, 2011, for February 22, 2012; Department of Homeland Security’s (DHS) Br. at 2-4. Moreover, the respondent has shown no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that it is uncontroverted that his former attorney received notice of the hearing (I.J. at 1). As recognized by the Immigration Judge, it is well-established that notice to an attorney is imputed to his or her client. See I.J. at 1 (citing, inter alia, Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985)).
 
*2 While the respondent concedes that notice to an attorney is imputed to the attorney’s client, he contends that he has “an extremely limited oral understanding” of English and “did not comprehend the date of the hearing” (Respondent’s Br. at 4). Although the respondent did not raise any issue regarding his limited English skills in his motion or declaration, we find that he has not met his burden--even accepting his contention as to his language difficulty--in demonstrating that he lacked notice for the hearing in question. The regulations define the term “service” as “physically presenting or mailing a document to the appropriate party or parties,” while stating that “a Notice to Appear or Notice of Removal Hearing shall be served to the alien in person, or if personal service is not practicable, shall be served by regular mail to the alien or the alien’s attorney of record.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.13. Here, the record reflects that the Notice of Removal Hearing for February 22, 2012, was mailed to the respondent’s attorney of record who was authorized to represent him at that time (I.J. at 1). See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.4(a). Such notice to his former attorney constitutes notice of the scheduled hearing to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a); see also Dominguez-Capistran v. Gonzales, 438 F.3d 876, 878 (8th Cir. 2006) (holding that because the petitioner’s attorney “was properly served notice,” the petitioner is deemed to have been given sufficient notice and that “[t]his is true despite [her attorney’s] poor calendaring and failure to personally remind [her] of her hearing date”).
 
The respondent also argues on appeal that his affidavit must be accepted as truthful by the Immigration Judge unless it is “inherently unbelievable” and that a sworn affidavit stating “that neither he nor a responsible party residing at his address received notice should ordinarily be sufficient to rebut the presumption of delivery” (Respondent’s Br. at 3-4). However, the respondent has cited to no binding case law or other legal authority to support such arguments. Also, the respondent has not refuted the Immigration Judge’s holding that his claim of not receiving notice from his prior attorney of the February 22, 2012, hearing was not supported by any attempt to meet the procedural requirements under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 2). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). We also find that the record here does not demonstrate a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance. See Habchy v. Gonzales, 471 F.3d 858, 864 (8th Cir. 2006) (noting the absence of “a formal complaint against the lawyer, or at least some explanation why Habchy did not take such action”).
 
*3 In sum, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Further, as the respondent has not established any legal or clear factual error in the Immigration Judge’s decision, we find no basis to reopen or remand this matter for any reason. Accordingly, based on the foregoing, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 1118468 (BIA)

2014 WL 1120190 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YESENIA ESMERALDA MEDINA-HERNANDEZ
File: AXXX XX3 520 - Miami, FL
February 7, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patricia M. Mendoza, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Shana Belyeu
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s October 1, 2013, decision denying the respondent’s September 13, 2013, motion to reconsider. The respondent’s motion to reconsider requested review of the Immigration Judge’s August 14, 2013, decision, denying the respondent’s July 24, 2013, motion to reopen.1 The respondent’s request for a waiver of the appellate filing fee is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). Her appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The following factual and procedural history is relevant and undisputed in this case. The respondent was personally served with a Notice to Appear dated February 16, 2004, instituting the instant proceedings (Resp Motion to Reopen at 1; Resp. Motion to Reconsider at 2-3; Exh. 1). She was scheduled for an initial master calendar hearing on April 22, 2004, at which time she was granted a continuance to seek counsel and advised of the consequences attaching to her failure to appear (I.J.1 at 2; Resp. Motion to Reconsider at 3; April 22, 2004, Notice of Hearing (NOH)). At a subsequent master calendar hearing held on August 26, 2004, the respondent appeared with counsel, Maritza Regalado, and her case was continued to January 6, 2005, to allow her to file an application for asylum, pursuant to section 208 of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1158; withholding of removal pursuant to section 241(b)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b)(3); and protection under Article 3 of the Convention Against Torture, 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16.-18 (I.J.1 at 2; Resp. Motion to Reopen at 1; Resp. Motion to Reconsider at 3; August 26, 2004, NOH). When the respondent appeared at her January 6, 2005, hearing, she was advised that Ms. Regalado had been disbarred and was no longer authorized to practice law in the State of Florida (I.J.1 at 2; Resp. Motion to Reopen at Tab D; Resp. Motion to Reconsider at 3-4; Resp. Brief at 5-6). The respondent’s case was then continued to April 7, 2005 (I.J.1 at 2; Resp. Motion to Reopen at 2; January 6, 2005, NOH). When the respondent failed to appear at her April 7, 2005, hearing, she was ordered removed in absentia (I.J.1 at 2; Resp. Motion to Reopen at 1; Exh. 2).
 
*2 On July 24, 2013, the respondent filed a motion to reopen pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i), asserting that she failed to appear at her final hearing due to exceptional circumstances (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 2). Specifically, she asserted that she was afraid to go to her April 7, 2005, hearing because Ms. Regalado told her that she would be ordered removed if she appeared at the hearing without Ms. Regalado (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 2, Affidavit). Moreover, she asserted that she did not file her motion to reopen sooner, as she was not aware of her counsel’s ineffective assistance, including her disbarment and inability to continue representing the respondent, until her subsequently-retained counsel reviewed the record of proceedings in her case on October 3, 2012 (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 2, Affidavit).
 
On August 14, 2013, the Immigration Judge concluded that she did not have jurisdiction to adjudicate the motion to reopen as it was not accompanied by the appropriate filing fee or request for a fee waiver as required by 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.24(b)(1), (2)(v) (I.J.1 at 1). In the alternative, she concluded that she would not grant the motion on its merits as the respondent had not credibly established that the filing deadline generally applicable to cases involving exceptional circumstances could be equitably tolled in this case (I.J.1 at 2). See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act (generally requiring that any motion to reopen seeking to rescind an in absentia order and based on exceptional circumstances be filed within 180 days of the entry of the in absentia order); Avila-Santoyo v. United States Attorney General, 713 F.3d 1357, 1362-64 (11th Cir. 2013) (providing for equitable tolling of the motion to reopen deadlines under certain circumstances); see also Ruiz-Turcios v. United States Attorney General, 717 F.3d 847 (11th Cir. 2013) (discussing the role of equitable tolling where an alien alleges ineffective assistance of counsel). In addition, the Immigration Judge found that, notwithstanding the potential availability of equitable tolling, the respondent did not qualify for reopening of her case as she did not demonstrate her prima facie eligibility for asylum, withholding of removal, or protection under the Convention Against Torture (I.J.1 at 3). In this regard, the Immigration Judge noted that the respondent’s motion was not accompanied by the requisite application for relief and her motion, along with the accompanying affidavit, did not provide any specific details or relevant documentary evidence supporting her claims (I.J.1 at 3; Resp. Motion to Reopen at 2, Tab C).
 
On September 13, 2013, the respondent filed a motion, styled as a motion to reconsider, wherein she argued that the Immigration Judge clearly erred in finding that her motion to reopen was not accompanied by the appropriate filing fee (Resp. Motion to Reconsider at 2-3). In addition, she maintained that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that her request for reopening is not supported by exceptional circumstances (Resp. Motion to Reconsider at 3-4). She also requested sua sponte reopening (Resp. Motion to Reconsider at 7).
 
*3 On October 1, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reconsider maintaining that there was insufficient record evidence establishing that the filing fee was paid when the motion to reopen was filed (I.J.2 at 1). Moreover, she concluded that the respondent’s motion did not otherwise establish that the August 14, 2013, decision contained any errors of law or fact (I.J.2 at 1). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); see also Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991) (defining a motion to reconsider as a motion raising errors of law or fact in a prior decision). Finally, she concluded that, to the extent the respondent was raising new legal arguments, introducing new evidence, or requesting reopening on alternate bases, these arguments were not appropriately part of reconsideration (I.J.2 at 1).
 
Starting with the issue of jurisdiction, we agree with the respondent that the Immigration Judge clearly erred in finding that her motion to reopen was not supported by evidence that the appropriate filing fee had been paid (Resp. Brief at 3). See Concrete Pipe & Products of Cal., Inc., v. Construction Laborers Pension Trust for S. Cal., 508 U.S. 602, 623 (1993) (explaining that clear error review is “significantly deferential to the trier of fact, precluding reversal where the reviewing authority simply views the evidence differently than the fact finder); Anderson v. City of Bessemer City, N.C., 470 U.S. 564, 573-74 (1985) (same); but see United States v. United States Gypsum Co., 333 U.S. 364, 395 (1948) (allowing for reversal of a factual determination where, upon review of the “entire evidence,” the appellate body is “left with the definite and firm conviction that a mistake has been committed”). Here, we note that a filing receipt bearing the respondent’s name and the motion to reopen filing fee was appended to the respondent’s motion to reopen (Resp. Motion to Reopen at Tab D).
 
Notwithstanding the foregoing clear error, we ultimately agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that, upon reconsideration of her August 14, 2013, decision, the respondent did not identify any error of law or fact that would warrant reopening of the instant proceedings (I.J.2 at 1). Specifically, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that, even if the respondent established a meritorious claim for equitable tolling, she has not otherwise satisfied the motion to reopen requirements (I.J.1 at 3). Specifically, the respondent’s motion is not supported by the relevant application for relief as required by 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). In addition, in the absence of a specific affidavit from the respondent or any related supporting documentation outlining the nature of the respondent’s claim, we also agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not otherwise established her prima facie eligibility for relief for asylum, withholding of removal, or protection under the Convention Against Torture (I.J.1 at 3). See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471-73 (BIA 1992) (BIA 1992) (indicating that motions to reopen are generally disfavored and that reopening may be denied where the respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for the relief that she is seeking); see also INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 322 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 110 (1988).
 
*4 Finally, contrary to the respondent’s assertion on appeal, we agree with the Immigration Judge that sua sponte reopening of these proceedings is not warranted (Resp. Brief at 7; I.J.2 at 1). Specifically, as outlined above, because the respondent has not complied with the regulatory requirements applicable to motions to reopen or established her prima facie eligibility for any form of relief, we conclude that sua sponte reopening is not appropriate regardless of any alleged exceptional circumstances presented in this case (I.J.1 at 3). See generally Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge’s August 14, 2013, decision will be referred to hereinafter as I.J.1, and her October 1, 2013, decision, as I.J.2.

2014 WL 1120176 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FERNANDO REYES-BERNAL A.K.A. FERNANDO REYEZ
File: AXXX XX8 023 - Salt Lake City, Utah
January 31, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Skyler Anderson, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 16, 2013. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondents September 9, 2013, motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on August 26, 2013, pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A) (I.J. at 1-2). On appeal the respondent argues that his failure to appear was attributable to “exceptional circumstances” (Respondent’s Br. at 6-14). See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) and 240(e)(1) of the Act (defining “exceptional circumstances”). Specifically, the respondent contends that his failure to attend the August 26, 2013, hearing was attributable to the ineffective assistance of his former counsel.1 See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). However, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
In the respondent’s affidavit submitted in support of the motion to reopen, the respondent asserts that he misunderstood his former attorney’s statement - provided with the aid of a translator - that he would “represent” him in these removal proceedings as an indication that he did not have to attend the master calendar hearing on August 26, 2013 (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab B). However, the respondent concedes that his former attorney did not instruct him not to attend the August 26, 2013, hearing (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab B).
 
Similarly, the affidavit submitted by the respondent’s former attorney reflects that he told the respondent that, if retained, he would “represent him in immigration court” (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab C). The affidavit submitted by the respondent’s former attorney further reflects that he did not “at any time tell him [the respondent] he did not need to go to court” (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab C). The respondent’s former attorney did not appear at the August 26, 2013, hearing because, after the respondent retained him, he failed to record the August 26, 2013, hearing in his office calendar (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab C).
 
“Exceptional circumstances” are defined as “circumstances (such as battery or extreme cruelty to the alien or any child or parent of the alien, serious illness of the alien, or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.” See section 240(e)(1) of the Act. The respondent’s misunderstanding as to the attorney’s representation in this case is not an exceptional circumstance.2 Cf. Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996) (“An order of deportation issued following a hearing conducted in absentia may be rescinded under section 242B(c)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c)(3) (Supp. V 1993), where an alien properly establishes that his failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel which amounts to ‘exceptional circumstances’ within the meaning of section 242B(f)(2) of the Act.”). The respondent acknowledges having notice of the hearing and concedes that his former attorney did not give him erroneous instruction not to attend the August 26, 2013, hearing. The former attorney’s failure to record the hearing date in his office calendar may explain the attorney’s absence at the August 26, 2013, hearing, but it does not excuse the respondent’s absence from Immigration Court on that date. Even if the attorney had appeared and the respondent did not, the Immigration Judge was authorized to proceed in absentia and the respondent’s assumption that Immigration Judge would have continued the hearing under these circumstances is speculative.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s current counsel is a member of the same law office as the respondent’s former attorney against whom he makes the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.

2

The unpublished Board decisions attached to the respondent’s appeal brief involve different factual circumstances and, in any event, are not precedential. See Matter of Zangwill, 18 I&N Dec. 22, 27 (BIA 1981), overruled on other grounds, Matter of Ozkok, 19 I&N Dec. 546 (BIA 1988).

ORDER:
 
*1 On May 14, 2013, the Immigration Judge issued an order of removal against the respondent, and on the same date, informed the respondent and his counsel that a Notice of Appeal was due to be filed no later than June 13, 2013. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38 (Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision). The respondent did not file his Notice of Appeal until December 12, 2013. As such, the appeal is untimely.
 
The respondent requests that we accept the untimely appeal on certification, see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), and remand the record because the respondent’s former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the record does not indicate that the respondent’s former counsel was hired to file a Notice of Appeal, and the contract between the respondent and former counsel involved his bond, case evaluation, and a deportation relief application (Motion, Exh. B). Further, the Immigration Judge specifically addressed the respondent and warned the respondent that a Notice of Appeal must be filed no later than June 13, 2013 (Motion, Exh. E at 52-53), but the respondent did not retain a new lawyer until after the filing date had expired. Given these circumstances, we find that the respondent did not demonstrate that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance regarding the untimely filing of his Notice of Appeal. As such, we decline to accept the appeal on certification.
 
Since we find that the appeal is untimely, the Immigration Judge’s decision is accordingly now final, and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RODRIGO ANCANJO ALVES
File: AXXX XX5 973 - San Antonio, TX
January 31, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mayra Joli, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Elliot R. Selle
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Brazil, has appealed from the September 16, 2013, decision of the Immigration Judge denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings conducted in absentia on July 3, 2013. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including the Immigration Judge’s determination of credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge for the reasons set forth therein, with the following notations. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
The respondent’s order of removal may be rescinded if he demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). “The term ‘exceptional circumstances’ refers to exceptional circumstances (such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of an immediate relative of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.” Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
The respondent does not meaningfully dispute that he had notice of his hearing, as the respondent, through counsel, attempted to file a motion on June 20, 2013, to change the venue of the hearing which was scheduled for July 3, 2013.1 The respondent’s motion to change venue was not granted. A pending but not granted motion does not constitute exceptional circumstances. Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 1985), aff’d Patel v. INS, 803 F.2d 804, 806 (5th Cir. 1986).2
 
The respondent claims in his motion and on appeal that requiring him to travel from Florida to Texas was unconscionable, because his wife recently gave birth and he also had a toddler (Resp. Br. at Tab G). We acknowledge the difficulties that parents may have when required to travel; however, the respondent has not described any serious illness or death that caused him to miss his hearing. The respondent’s situation is less compelling and does not involve circumstances outside the respondent’s control. Therefore, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not met his burden to show that exceptional circumstances existed to excuse his failure to appear. See sections 240(b)(5)(C), (e)(1) of the Act.
 
*2 Due to the nature of our disposition of this matter, we need not address the respondent’s other contentions on appeal and we find no basis to remand this case for further proceedings. INS v. Bagamasbad, 429 U.S. 24, 25-26 (1976) (as a general rule, courts and agencies are not required to make findings on issues the decision of which is unnecessary to the results they reach). Finally, we are not persuaded that these circumstances require the exercise of our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

1

The attempting filing was rejected for failing to comply with filing requirements, but the motion was successfully refiled on June 24, 2013.

2

The respondent does not claim ineffective assistance of counsel on appeal.

IN RE: WILLIAM PHILLIP KAGWE

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
