Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Kenya, timely appeals a June 25, 2012, decision by the Immigration Judge denying reopening of his April 9, 2012, decision which granted the respondent post-conclusion voluntary departure with an alternate order of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) opposes the appeal. In a February 28, 2012, decision, the Immigration Judge had granted the DHS’s motion to pretermit the respondent’s application for adjustment of status. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge had granted the DHS’s motion in part because the respondent had not complied with his request to submit documents in support of his adjustment of status application. In his motion to reopen, the respondent contended ineffective assistance of counsel against his prior counsel, Jim Melo, who represented him before the Immigration Judge. He alleged that attorney Melo did not file supporting documents that he had in his possession. However, as the Immigration Judge pointed out in his decision denying reopening, the respondent has not fully complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (I.J. at 2). Although the respondent submitted evidence that his former counsel was contacted regarding the allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not indicated whether he has received a response from his former counsel. Also, he has not filed a complaint regarding his former counsel’s performance with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Also, the letter to his former counsel does not mention the reasons he is contending ineffective assistance of counsel. Furthermore, the applicant has not demonstrated a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel sufficient to waive the Lozada requirements. See Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133 (2d Cir. 2007) (persuasive authority) (disbarment of attorney obviates need for compliance with Lozada).
 
*2 In addition, as the Immigration Judge noted in his decision denying reopening, the respondent’s prior attorney indicated in his “Motion to Accept Late Filing of Brief” filed on February 27, 2012, that it was the respondent’s fault that supporting documents had not been submitted by the deadline set by the Court (I.J. at 2).
 
The respondent’s assertion that the Immigration Judge did not communicate the fact that he had pretermitted the respondent’s adjustment of status application until April 9, 2012 is unfounded (Resp. Br. at 6). The evidence shows that the Immigration Judge’s February 2012 decision was served on the respondent’s counsel.
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane

IN RE: LUZ ELENA RODRIGUEZ-SOLIS A.K.A. LUZ ELENA THORTON A.K.A. LUZELENA THORNTON

January 30, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Moises Barraza, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Seth B. Fitter
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 10, 2013. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found her ineligible for relief from removal based on her criminal convictions.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).
 
At the hearing, the respondent requested a continuance on the grounds that she was not advised of the immigration consequences of her 2011 theft conviction (Tr. at 11-12). That is one of nine convictions, which serve as the basis for removal and which render her ineligible for relief from removal (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 14-22; Exhs 1, 2).2 On appeal, the respondent does not indicate that she has filed a post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction, let alone assert that the conviction has been vacated. While the respondent is free to pursue a post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). A post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Also on appeal, the respondent indicates that she is prima facie eligible for a U nonimmigrant visa for victims of violent crimes (Form I-918). However, the respondent does not assert that a nonimmigrant U visa petition has been filed and that a law enforcement certification has been obtained.3 In such circumstances, while the respondent is free to pursue the U nonimmigrant visa petition, it does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against her.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

3

In determining whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings to await the adjudication of an alien’s pending U nonimmigrant visa petition, an Immigration Judge should consider (1) the response of the DHS to the alien’s motion to continue; (2) whether the underlying visa petition is prima facie approvable; and (3) the reason for the continuance and other procedural factors. See Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807 (BIA 2012). To establish prima facie eligibility for a U nonimmigrant visa, an alien must have suffered substantial physical or mental abuse as the innocent victim of a qualifying crime for which the alien has been, is being, or will be helpful to law enforcement, which ordinarily requires an approved law enforcement certification. See Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, supra. An alien who has filed a prima facie approvable petition for a U nonimmigrant visa with the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services will ordinarily warrant a favorable exercise of discretion for a continuance for a reasonable period of time. See Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, supra.

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alexander Cane, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated September 18, 2013, denying his motion to reopen. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
The record reflects that, on November 21, 2005, the respondent was detained by Border Patrol after entering this country without authorization. Upon his apprehension, he was unable to provide Border Patrol with a verifiable address in the United States. On November 25, 2005, a Border Patrol Agent personally served the respondent with a Notice to Appear (“NTA”) which placed the respondent on notice that he had been placed in removal proceedings (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). The NTA indicated that the respondent had failed to provide an address in the United States and advised him that, if he did not submit an Alien’s Change of Address Form (Form EOIR-33) and did not otherwise provide an address at which he may be reached during proceedings, then the Government would not be required to provide him with written notice of his hearing (Exh. 1). The NTA further advised that, if he failed to attend his removal hearing, a removal order may be made by the Immigration Judge in his absence (Exh. 1).
 
On March 20, 2006, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States in absentia. As the respondent did not file a Form EOIR-33 or otherwise provide an address at which he may be reached during proceedings, the Immigration Court did not provide the respondent with notice of his removal hearing. The respondent filed his motion to reopen on August 15, 2013.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely under the general rule requiring aliens to file a motion to reopen within 90 days of the entry of a final administrative order of removal. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Likewise, the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel and diminished mental capacity are untimely under the extended 180-day deadline governing claims that “exceptional circumstances” caused an alien’s failure to appear at his hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). To the extent that the respondent wishes to advance a claim to asylum, he has not meaningfully argued that changed country conditions in Guatemala should excuse the untimeliness of his motion. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i).
 
*2 Reopening is also not warrant on account of lack of notice. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Ac; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Inasmuch as the respondent did not provide the Immigration Court with an address, the Immigration Court was not required to place him on notice of his removal hearing before the Immigration Judge entered an order of removal in absentia (I.J. at 1). See section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Act; Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354, 360-61 (5th Cir. 2009). Moreover, there is no requirement that a NTA be in any language other than English. See Cruz-Diaz v. Holder, 388 F.App’x. 429 (5th Cir. 2010); Chavez v. Holder, 343 F.App’x. 955 (5th Cir. 2009).
 
The respondent’s appellate arguments do not persuade this Board that an exceptional situation exists which would warrant sua sponte reopening. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
For the reasons set forth above, we will not disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order is entered
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Philippines who was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on January 6, 2012, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated May 11, 2012, denying her motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security is opposed to the respondent’s appeal.
 
The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. As recognized by the respondent on appeal, the motion, which was prepared by her former counsel, lacked merit. Nonetheless, as the respondent has sought new counsel in a diligent manner, filed a complaint against her former counsel, and established that her former counsel’s ineffective assistance of counsel contributed to her failure to appear at her removal hearing, we are satisfied that the respondent has now presented sufficient evidence to establish that the order of removal entered against her should be rescinded. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, we will reopen these proceedings in order to provide the respondent with an additional opportunity to appear for a removal hearing. The following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The in absentia order of removal entered on January 6, 2012, is rescinded, these removal proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Sharon Hoffman

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDMUNDS RITINS
File: AXXX XX7 274 - New York, NY
January 17, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nicholas J. Mundy, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Latvia, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated September 17, 2013, denying his motion to reopen. We will dismiss the appeal.1
 
The record reflects that, on April 4, 2003, the respondent was personally served with a Notice of Hearing in Removal Proceedings (“NOH”) which placed him on notice that a removal hearing had been scheduled in this matter on April 16, 2003 (I.J. at 2; Exh. 1.1). However, as the respondent did not appear at his scheduled removal hearing, he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent filed his motion to reopen on August 22, 2013.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely under the general rule requiring aliens to file a motion to reopen within 90 days of the entry of a final administrative order of removal. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Likewise, the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel are untimely under the extended 180-day deadline governing claims that “exceptional circumstances” caused an alien’s failure to appear at his hearing (I.J. at 4). See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Even if we were to accept the respondent’s claims that he called his former attorney “every month to check on the status up to or about 2009,” equitable tolling of the applicable filing deadline would not be warranted because he has not demonstrated that he exercised due diligence between 2009 and the filing of the motion to reopen in 2013 (I.J. at 4). See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008) (“We therefore conclude that in order to equitably toll the filing deadline for a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate that he or she has exercised due diligence during the entire period he or she seeks to toll.”). Inasmuch as the record establishes that the respondent was personally served with the aforementioned NOH, reopening is also not warranted on account of lack of notice. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
The respondent’s appellate arguments do not persuade this Board that an exceptional situation exists which would warrant sua sponte reopening. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 For the reasons set forth above, we will not disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order is entered
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s request to stay his removal pending consideration of this appeal is moot. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 107-110 (BIA 2009).

2014 WL 347685 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALEJANDRO DIEGO-CARRERAS A.K.A. ALEJANDRO DIEGO
File: AXXX XX9 531 - Florence, AZ
January 9, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gabriel Sayavedra, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
David C. Whipple
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 29, 2013. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(4)(3)(i), (ii).
 
At the outset, to the extent the respondent has challenged the Immigration Judge’s findings as to his removability and eligibility for relief from removal, we consider that the distinct and formal concession of removability, made by the respondent’s attorney acting in his professional capacity and in the respondent’s presence during the removal hearing, is binding on the respondent as a judicial admission (Tr. at 21-24). Applicable regulations, which have the force and effect of law as to the Immigration Judges and this Board, provide in pertinent part that “the immigration judge may determine that removability as charged has been established by the admissions of the respondent.” 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c). The respondent in the instant case has not established that circumstances exist that would permit the withdrawal of his prior judicial admissions. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made as a tactical decision by an attorney in a deportation proceeding is binding on his alien client); Perez-Mejia v. Holder, 641 F.3d 1143 (9th Cir. 2011). Furthermore, the respondent has not filed an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as it relates to counsel’s judicial admissions under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
Moreover, even if we were to consider arguendo the respondent’s contentions on appeal challenging his removability, we find his arguments to be unavailing. The record includes undisputed evidence showing that the respondent was convicted of Possession of Drug Paraphernalia, in violation of A.R.S. §§ 13-3401, 13-3408, 13-3415, 13-701, 13-702, 13-702.01, 13-707, 13-801, 13-802 and 13-901.01 (Exhs. 4-6). The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, has determined that a conviction for the offense of possession of drug paraphernalia is a controlled substance violation. See Luu-Le v. INS, 224 F.3d 911, 916 (9th Cir. 2000) (a conviction for possession of drug paraphernalia under Ariz. Rev. Stat. §13-3415 is a crime relating to a controlled substance under 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(I)).
 
*2 The respondent did not express any fear of persecution or torture if returned to Mexico (Tr. at 23), and has failed to establish his eligibility for any relief from removal. See section 240(c)(4)(A)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 347685 (BIA)
End of Document

2014 WL 347697 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: COURTNEY BERESFORD LAING
File: AXXX XX8 541 - Detroit, MI
January 3, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Weronika K. Costas, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On October 21, 2013, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen proceedings in which the Board dismissed his appeal on March 30, 2001. The Board granted his request for a stay of removal on October 25, 2013. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely as it was filed more than 12 years after the Board’s decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). However, he asks that the Board sua sponte reopen proceedings as he contends that he is now eligible for adjustment of status and is the primary caretaker for his elderly and infirm mother. He further argues that the proceedings should be reopened because prior counsel provided him with ineffective assistance (Motion at 9-24).
 
However, we initially note that we do not find the facts that his priority date is now current or that he is his mother’s primary caretaker constitute exceptional circumstances warranting the exercise of our discretionary authority to sua sponte reopen proceedings. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “the power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Rather, a priority date’s becoming current following an order of removal is a not unusual circumstance, and becoming potentially eligible for adjustment of status is common and generally not sufficient to warrant a grant of an untimely motion to reopen. Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 105 (BIA 2009). While there is evidence that the respondent’s mother suffers from physical ailments, she is currently employed and there is no evidence that she is wholly dependent on the respondent (Motion, Tab B), or that her situation is such that it constitutes an exceptional situation warranting a sua sponte grant of reopening.
 
To support reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent must satisfy the perquisites for such a claim as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). This includes showing that he was prejudiced by counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance, and that he exercised due diligence in pursuing his rights. Mezo v. Holder, 615 F.3d 616, 620 (6th Cir.2010); Tapia-Martinez v. Gonzales, 482 F.3d 417, 423 (6th Cir. 2007) (quoting Scorteanu v. INS, 339 F.3d 407, 414 (6th Cir.2003)). We need not address whether the respondent has shown substantial compliance with Matter of Lozada, or otherwise shown ineffective assistance of counsel, including prejudice, as we find that the respondent has not shown due diligence.
 
*2 The final administrative order was issued in this case on March 30, 2001, and the United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit dismissed the respondent’s petition for review on January 29, 2003 (Motion, Tab K). While the respondent’s mother reports she visited Ms. Wong in 2003 and 2007, she reports that Ms. Wong told her “something about [the respondent] having to go back to Jamaica” and ultimately left “believing there was nothing” she could do to help fix her son’s immigration situation (Motion, Tab B, paras. 17-22). There is also no evidence that the respondent thereafter made any effort with regard to his immigration case until he was detained in 2013. Under these circumstances, the respondent has not shown due diligence in pursuing his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and the time limits on filing motions to reopen will therefore not be tolled on that basis. Tapia-Martinez v. Gonzales, supra.
 
While the respondent now claims that he fears he would be harmed if he is returned to Jamaica, he does not state when or how he learned the information underpinning this alleged fear, nor shown that he had these fears at the time of his hearing (Motion at 3, para. 19). Rather, the respondent, although he was then present, did not contest counsel’s report at his hearing that he did not fear persecution or torture (Tr. at 16). Further, his claims in this regard are notably vague and unsupported, and do not establish materially changed country conditions, nor has the respondent filed any application for associated relief with his motion, as required. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
As the respondent has not shown an adequate basis for granting this untimely motion to reopen, either based on ineffective assistance of counsel or sua sponte, the motion will be denied. Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 6921570 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICKYLYN SULTAN RIPDOS
File: AXXX XX0 372 - Bloomington, MN
December 26, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nelson L. Peralta, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Laura W. Trosen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Philippines, appeals from the May 21, 2012, decision of the Immigration Judge denying her motion to reopen the proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has submitted a brief in opposition. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether or not the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The proceedings below were continued several times on the basis that the respondent was pursuing a “U” nonimmigrant visa (Tr. at 4-5; 9-11). At the final hearing on January 4, 2012, the respondent accepted voluntary departure until May 3, 2012, and waived appeal (Tr. at 12). Section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a).
 
On May 1, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen the proceedings. She attached a U Nonimmigrant Status Certification (Form I-918), which had been signed by a law enforcement officer on April 19, 2012. The certification was based on the activities of the respondent’s aunt as a human trafficker. The DHS opposed reopening, noting that despite the certification, there was little indication that the respondent would be granted a U visa. The DHS also pointed out that the respondent can pursue a U visa even with an order of removal.
 
On May 21, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied the motion as untimely. The Immigration Judge pointed out that although the respondent had raised the issue of trafficking below, she indicated that she was pursuing the U visa on the basis of her son’s murder. She also considered that only the certification had been filed, and that there was no other evidence to support the grant of a U visa. The respondent, with new counsel, has filed this appeal. She asserts that the Immigration Judge did not accurately recall the discussion during the hearing, that she indicated she would reopen if certification was granted, and that the attorney who previously represented her provided ineffective assistance.
 
We find that the Immigration Judge correctly denied the motion to reopen the proceedings as untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). We further do not find any indication that the Immigration Judge misled the respondent into believing that if certification became available, she would reopen the proceedings regardless of the timing (Tr. at 8-9, 15). The Immigration Judge specifically pointed out that if any subsequent motion to reopen was untimely, reopening could be obtained upon agreement of the parties (Id.).
 
*2 The respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in stating that her visa certification was based on a different situation than originally argued (Resp. Br. at 8-10). The record indicates the respondent initially indicated she would pursue the U visa based on her being trafficked by her aunt, but she later indicated that her son’s murder would be the primary basis put forward for the certification (Tr. at 2-4, 7-10). We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s statement here which would warrant reversing her decision, especially due to the untimely nature of the motion to reopen.
 
The respondent also claims that she was subject to ineffective assistance of counsel below. However, her counsel appears to have been actively pursuing the certification and did obtain several continuances for the respondent. Insofar as she accepted voluntary departure on the final hearing date, the record does not establish that this was more than a tactical decision on her part. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382-83 (BIA 1986). Moreover, the respondent has not substantially complied with the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance in removal proceedings. See Matter of Compean, Bangley & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Ortiz-Puentes v. Holder, 662 F.3d 481 (8th Cir. 2011); Habchy v. Gonzales, 471 F.3d 858 (8th Cir. 2006); see also Rafiyev v. Mukasey, 536 F.3d 853 (8th Cir. 2008) (rejecting Constitutional right in removal proceedings to effective assistance of counsel under the Fifth Amendment, but finding exercise of Board’s discretion to reopen proper where respondent was ineffectively served by counsel). Most notably, the respondent has not submitted her own statement, proof that she notified prior counsel of her allegations, or that she has filed a disciplinary complaint. See Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
To the extent the respondent cites Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009), and Matter of Garcia, 16 I&N Dec. 653 (BIA 1978), neither of these cases extend their reasoning to untimely motions to reopen (Resp. Br. at 11). We note that in Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807 (BIA 2012), we addressed the circumstances of when proceedings should be continued to await the adjudication of a “U” nonimmigrant visa petition. In that case, we noted the importance of an applicant establishing prima facie eligibility for the visa, including submitting a receipt showing that a petition has been filed with DHS. Sanchez-Sosa at 814. In this case, the respondent has not submitted such proof; rather, she has only provided the signed I-918. This further augments against reopening, even if we set aside the time limitation. The appeal will accordingly be dismissed.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 6921560 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: INES LETICIA NORIEGA
File: AXXX XX0 625 - San Diego, CA
December 16, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Karla Patricia Avelino, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 On September 3, 2013, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. On October 3, 2013, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider. We will also treat the motion as a motion to reopen based on the respondent’s allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. For the following reasons, the motions will be denied.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Guatemala. Prior to the filing of these proceedings, the respondent attempted to enter the United States and was removed pursuant to an expedited order of removal. She re-entered illegally and sought to adjust her status under NACARA. The Department of Homeland Security did not reinstate her removal order, and referred her case to an Immigration Judge for consideration of her NACARA claim. The Department of Homeland Security subsequently filed a Notice to Appear with the Immigration Judge, thereby initiating these proceedings.
 
The DHS then filed a motion to terminate proceedings so that it could reinstate the prior order under section 241(a)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(a)(5). The respondent’s prior counsel and her current counsel both concede that the respondent is not eligible for NACARA (Motion at p. 8 “Upon further review of the record the undersigned concedes that the Respondent was not eligible for NACARA in Immigration Court Proceedings.”; Exh. I at p. 3 (44)). The Immigration Judge terminated proceedings and the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal.
 
The respondent filed the current timely motion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. As to reconsideration, the motion is denied because the respondent has not demonstrated a material error of fact or law in our prior decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). As to reopening, the motion is also denied as the respondent did not demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel or prejudice. As the respondent’s former and current counsel both concede, the respondent is not eligible for NACARA. Further, her lack of eligibility is not due to any ineffective actions by her former attorney. The respondent nevertheless requests reopening and a remand because her due process rights have been violated. The respondent argues that she did not agree to termination, and she is entitled to a full and fair hearing.
 
We cannot consider the threshold analysis of whether the respondent’s allegations are inherently unbelievable regarding her allegations of ineffective assistance, see Bhasin v. Gonzales, 423 F.3d 977, 987 (9th Cir. 2005), because the respondent did not submit a signed and dated declaration (Exh. A). Further, it is unclear whether the unsigned, undated declaration included as exhibit A is the statement that was submitted to the state bar so the respondent has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).1
 
*2 Even if there was ineffective assistance of counsel on the face of the record, the motion would still be denied because the respondent has not demonstrated plausible grounds for relief such that she was prejudiced by the actions of former counsel. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003). As noted supra, the parties concede that the respondent is not eligible for NACARA. And while she generally mentions asylum, she does not allege a well-founded fear of future persecution on account of a protected ground. Nor has she submitted a new asylum application or other evidence evidencing her eligibility for relief from removal.
 
The respondent asserts that her former attorney should have requested an exercise of prosecutorial discretion under the DHS’s John Morton Memorandum on Prosecutorial Discretion. However, she has not attached a copy of the memorandum. Arguably, one of the most favorable ways to exercise prosecutorial discretion is to dismiss a case, which is what occurred here, and the respondent may also file an additional request with DHS. As of the date of the issuance of this decision, the record does not contain evidence that DHS has actually reinstated the prior order of removal. Moreover, this is not a situation where the respondent has demonstrated prima facie eligibility for a form of relief that she may only obtain in removal proceedings. Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The motions are denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

1

We note that DHS does not have to terminate current proceedings in order to reinstate a prior order, and could have chosen in the exercise of discretion to continue removal proceedings even though a prior removal order existed. See Villa-Anguiano v. Holder, 727 F.3d 873 (9th Cir. 2013). Former counsel appeared to assert that reinstatement arises by operation of law when, in fact, a number of procedural safeguards and actions are required prior to reinstatement.

2013 WL 6921674 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLOS MANUEL RODRIGUEZ VASQUEZ
File: AXXX XX5 568 - Boston, Massachusetts
December 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sonia R. Lin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brandon L. Lowy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, has filed a timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 25, 2013, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen the removal proceedings in this case to apply for adjustment of status, and alleging ineffective assistance of former counsel.1 While the appeal was pending before the Board, the respondent filed a motion to remand. The appeal will be dismissed. The motion to remand will be denied for lack of jurisdiction.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment in an appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record reflects that at a continued master calendar hearing on February 9, 2011, an Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged in the Notice to Appear (Form I-862) (Exh. 1), based on his admissions, and finding that he had not established his eligibility for any relief or protection from removal, granted the respondent the privilege of pre-conclusion voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a), for a period of 120 days, until June 9, 2011.
 
Specifically, the transcript of the February 9, 2011, hearing (Tr. at 14) reflects that the Immigration Judge had continued the proceedings for a period of nearly one year, to allow the respondent’s United States citizen spouse the opportunity to file a new immediate relative immigrant visa petition (Form I-130) on behalf of the respondent, as she had previously withdrawn an earlier visa petition she had filed on his behalf. In a colloquy with the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 13), respondent’s former counsel advised him that she wished to withdraw her representation of the respondent. She indicated to the Immigration Judge that the respondent and his spouse had failed to cooperate with her in providing the documentation necessary to prepare and file a new immediate relative immigrant visa petition on his behalf.
 
*2 In a supplemental brief, and accompanying affidavits, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge denied him the opportunity to contest and dispute the account given by former counsel. We acknowledge that the transcript of the February 9, 2011, hearing reflects that in an ensuing exchange with the Immigration Judge, the respondent was interrupted by him several times (Tr. at 14). However, we note the respondent’s testimony (when read together without the Immigration Judge’s interjections) does not reflect any attempt to contest and dispute the account provided by former counsel, but rather suggests excuses as to why he did not contact her and requests more time to get the necessary documents to file a new Form I-130.2
 
In addition, contrary to respondent’s appellate contention, it was the Immigration Judge and not former counsel, that brought up the issue of voluntary departure (Tr. at 13-14). The record reflects the Immigration Judge asked if former counsel would continue as his legal representative while he qualified the respondent for a grant of voluntary departure before granting her motion to withdraw (Tr. at 15). The Immigration Judge explained to the respondent that this appeared to be the only form of relief from removal then available to him, unless the respondent wanted to accept an order of removal (Tr. at 14-15). In response, the respondent unequivocally stated that he wanted a grant of voluntary departure (Tr. at 15). Contrary to his appellate contention, there is nothing in the record to indicate former counsel influenced the respondent’s decision to pursue voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge clearly explained to the respondent the conditions and penalties associated with the grant of voluntary departure, including that the respondent had to depart the United States no later than June 9, 2011, or the grant of voluntary departure would be withdrawn, and he would not only be subject to an alternate order of removal, but would be precluded from applying for various forms of relief from removal, including adjustment of status, for a period of 10 years (Tr. at 17). The record of the hearing (Tr. at 17-18) reflects the respondent clearly expressed that he understood the Immigration Judge, and accepted the grant of pre-hearing voluntary departure. Contrary to his appellate contentions, as the respondent repeatedly and unambiguously answered “[y]es, sir,” to each of the Immigration Judge’s queries as to whether he understood (Tr. at 17-18), we find no support for any claimed misunderstanding or confusion on the part of the respondent on this point. However, the respondent failed to depart the United States prior to the expiration of the period of voluntary departure. Instead, the respondent filed a motion to reopen on April 5, 2013, more than 2 years after the Immigration Judge’s final order, alleging, inter alia, ineffective assistance by former counsel at the hearing, and seeking to apply for adjustment of status and other forms of relief from removal. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s untimely motion, in a decision dated June 25, 2013.
 
*3 On de novo review, the respondent presents no arguments on appeal to persuade us to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision. Initially, we agree with Immigration Judge that the respondent’s motion was untimely filed (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Furthermore, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s contention that he exercised due diligence in pursuing his case so as to possibly pursue equitable tolling of the 90-day motions deadline, when the respondent delayed filing his motion by more than 2 years after the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Chedid v. Holder, 573 F.3d 33, 37 (1st Cir. 2009) (if the doctrine of equitable tolling is available, it applies only to those who exercise due diligence). Given the length of the delay at issue, and noting the circumstances that prompted the respondent eventually to consult with an attorney (i.e., he was detained by the DHS pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s outstanding alternate order of removal), the respondent did not exercise due diligence in the pursuit of his rights so as to benefit from the equitable tolling of the statute.3 See Dawoud v. Holder, 561 F.3d 31, 36 (1st Cir. 2009). Furthermore, we do not find that a truly exceptional situation exists in this case, so as to warrant sua sponte reopening, as also urged by the respondent. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); see also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Notwithstanding, even if timely filed, we find the respondent has not established that the motion should have been granted based on his arguments alleging ineffective assistance of former counsel at his hearing before the Immigration Judge. When a motion to reopen is premised on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the alien must meet the three specified criteria in order to assist the Board in assessing the validity of the claim. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The second of those criteria is that “before allegations of ineffective assistance of former counsel are presented to the Board, former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond.” Id. (emphasis added). While the respondent has provided evidence that he informed his former attorney of the allegations against her in a mailing dated August 29, 2013, this was more than 4 months after the initial motion to reopen was filed with the Immigration Court on April 5, 2013.4 We fail to find that this satisfies Lozada’s requirement that the respondent provide notice that allows his former counsel sufficient time to timely respond to the allegations before filing his motion with the Immigration Judge. See Asaba v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 9, 12 (1st Cir. 2004) ((citing Matter of Lozada, supra at 639 and Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 599 (9th Cir. 2004)). The respondent has failed to comply with this critical Lozada requirement.
 
*4 Moreover, assuming arguendo the respondent had met all the Lozada criteria, the respondent has not demonstrated that reopening of the proceedings is warranted on that basis. In order to advance a successful claim for ineffective assistance of former counsel, an alien must demonstrate prejudice i.e., he “must show that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case.” See Saakian v. I.N.S., 252 F.3d 21, 25 (1st Cir. 2001) (ineffective assistance of counsel exists where, as a result of counsel’s actions (or lack thereof), “the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case.”); see also Orehhova v. Gonzales, 417 F.3d 48, 52 (1st Cir. 2005) (citing Bernal-Vallejo v. INS, 195 F.3d 56, 63 (1st Cir.1999) (the alien must show at least a reasonable probability of prejudice)); Toban v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 40, 46 (1st Cir. 2004) (alien alleging denial of due process must show prejudice in all but the most “extreme case[s]”); LeBlanc v. INS, supra at 694 (no need to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel where “[t]here has been no violation of fundamental fairness”).
 
Contrary to the respondent’s appellate contentions, the record of the February 9, 2011, hearing (Tr. at 13), reveals that respondent’s former counsel moved to withdraw as his legal representative arguing before the Immigration Judge that she had been unable to prepare and file the Form I-130 on behalf of the respondent, as had been previously directed by the Immigration Judge at the February 3, 2010, hearing (Tr. at 11), because the respondent and his spouse had not cooperated with her repeated requests to provide necessary documentation in support of that visa petition. See, e.g., 8 C.F.R. § 103.2(b) (A visa petition must be accompanied by all required documents).
 
Further, notwithstanding his appellate contentions as to the interruptions by the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 14) of his testimony that followed, there is no indication that he was disputing the account provided by his former counsel, as opposed to offering excuses and seeking more time to obtain the necessary documentation to file the visa petition (Tr. at 14). The respondent has presented no evidence in support of any alleged attempts to contact former counsel (e.g., correspondence, telephone records, etc.) from the time of the February 3, 2010, hearing, until the February 9, 2011, hearing date. Whereas the affidavits that accompanied the respondent’s supplemental brief allege that the respondent and his spouse were repeatedly unable to contact former counsel by phone, there is no indication as to how many calls were made to former counsel, what are the dates and times of these calls, and what efforts they made to follow up on those unsuccessful attempts to reach her. Specifically, the respondent has not provided copies of any letters that were sent to former counsel inquiring about the case. As such, respondent’s former counsel cannot be faulted for not having had the Form I-130 filed or ready to be filed by the February 9, 2011, hearing, where that failure appears to be due in large part to the respondent’s own failure to provide her with the necessary documentation for filing the Form I-130 before the hearing. See Punzalan v. Holder, 575 F.3d 107, 110 (1st Cir. 2009). Moreover, the respondent has not demonstrated that he had any plausible basis for any other form of relief in this case, other than the pre-hearing voluntary departure granted by the Immigration Judge. We conclude, therefore, that the respondent has failed to demonstrate that he suffered prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance of former counsel at the hearing under the particular circumstances presented in this case.5 See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*5 In accordance with the foregoing, we find that the respondent has failed to demonstrate that a motion to reopen removal proceedings is warranted under the circumstances, or that the Immigration Judge erred in denying it.6
 
Finally, with respect to the respondent’s motion to remand, the motion will be denied as it is not properly before us for consideration. The Board has jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen his removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(b)(3). However, the Board generally does not have the authority to reopen a case that has never been properly before us in the first instance. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974). One of the conditions associated with the grant of pre-hearing voluntary departure is the respondent waive appeal of all issues in the Immigration Judge’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i). As the record reflects that the respondent accepted the Immigration Judge’s grant of pre-hearing voluntary departure (Tr. at 17-18), and there is no indication that an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 9, 2011, decision, was ever filed with the Board, it became the final administrative order entered in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39. Thus, the Board never acquired initial jurisdiction over this case, and the filing of an appeal of an Immigration Judge’s denial of a motion to reopen, does not give the Board jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s motion to remand. Admittedly, the regulations allow for the consolidation and consideration of motions to reopen submitted while there is a pending appeal before Board. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4). However, this does not extend the Board’s jurisdiction to consider motions to reopen when it has never taken initial jurisdiction over proceedings. As the Immigration Judge’s February 9, 2011, decision, was the final administrative order entered in this case, the motion should have been addressed to the Immigration Court.
 
Therefore, as the respondent’s motion is not properly before us for consideration, it will be denied. Moreover, in view of our decision, the respondent’s request for a stay of removal will be denied. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent is presently represented by Sonia R. Lin, Esquire, who filed an appellate brief in support of the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen, as well as a motion to remand alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. The ineffective assistance of former counsel claim primarily concerns the respondent’s legal representation by attorney Zoila Gomez at the hearings before the Immigration Judge, and derivatively from the actions of attorney John Garzon in the filing of the motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge.

2

Specifically, we find the hearing transcript as to that exchange (Tr. at 14) provides a clearer indication as to the actual intent of the respondent, wherein he states that “. . . what I’m saying is can you give me a chance, because I have to put in and make the application because I was . . .” At which point the Immigration Judge declined any further continuances noting that he had already postponed the case for one year, and the respondent did not do what he was told to do. The respondent appears to acknowledge that he did not comply with the Immigration Judge’s directive as to the filing of the Form I-130, and says, “Yeah, I was trying to make the documents. However . . . I didn’t have . . .” At this juncture, the Immigration Judge notes that if the respondent was unable to do it in one year, there was no reason to believe that he would ever be able to do it. At which point the respondent apologizes and states, “[y]eah, sorry.”

3

We observe, moreover, as to the request for equitable tolling of the timely filing requirement of the regulations, the United States Court of Appeals for the First Circuit has indicated that it has “not yet decided whether equitable tolling applies to the statute’s ninety-day deadline, despite multiple opportunities to do so.” See Bolieiro v. Holder, 731 F.3d 32, 39 (1st Cir. 2013) (citing Romer v. Holder, 663 F.3d 40, 43 (1st Cir. 2011) and Da Silva Neves v. Holder, 568 F.3d 41, 42 (1st Cir. 2009)). And even if it were available, “equitable tolling is a ‘sparingly’ invoked doctrine.” Jobe v. INS, 238 F.3d 96, 100 (1st Cir. 2001) (en banc) (setting forth five-factor test used to determine entitlement to equitable tolling).

4

Moreover, this deficiency is not cured by the fact that a copy of the letter was later included with the respondent’s appellate brief and motion to remand, which is dated August 30, 2013, and only one day before the date of the letter sent to his former counsel.

5

In addition, even assuming arguendo the respondent has demonstrated that prior counsel who filed the motion to reopen in this case was ineffective in his representation due to defects in the motion he had filed, the respondent has not shown that he suffered prejudice as a result of that ineffective representation so that it affected the outcome of his case, i.e., the claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel at the hearing did not establish that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing, and moreover, the motion to reopen was untimely filed and not fitting within any exceptions. See also Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 105 (BIA 2009) (untimely motions to reopen to apply for adjustment of status generally do not fall within any of the statutory or regulatory exceptions for motions to reopen and are generally denied).

6

Furthermore, the respondent’s failure to depart from the United States within the time period allowed for voluntary departure under the Immigration Judge’s final order rendered him statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status for a period of 10 years from that failure to depart. See section 240B(d)(1)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d)(1)(B); see also Jupiter v. Ashcroft, 396 F.3d 487, 491 (1st Cir. 2005) (holding that the plain language of INA § 240B(d) renders an alien ineligible for adjustment of status where the alien has previously failed to adhere to a voluntary departure order).

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILLIAM SAINT COME
File: AXXX XX3 842 - Boston, MA
December 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William E. Graves, Jr., Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
 
APPLICATION: Contested removal; motion to terminate
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Haiti who was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident in August 1986, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated July 31, 2013, ordering his removal from this country. In a subsequent decision, dated August 12, 2013, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent was subject to removal from the United States under the provisions of section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), as a result of his March 2011 conviction for assault and battery with a dangerous weapon. The respondent has filed a separate motion to terminate. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not replied to the respondent’s appeal, nor his motion to terminate.
 
We agree with the respondent that termination of these removal proceedings is appropriate. On appeal, the respondent has presented evidence that, due to ineffective assistance of counsel, the Massachusetts courts have allowed him to withdraw his guilty plea. See Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010). As such, we conclude that, at the present time, the respondent’s conviction does not have a sufficient degree of finality to warrant his removal from this country. See section 101(a)(48) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48). Our decision is without prejudice to the filing of a new Notice to Appear. In particular, in the event that the respondent’s ongoing criminal proceedings result in a new conviction for immigration purposes, the DHS is not precluded from re-commencing removal proceedings. The following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to terminate is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: These removal proceedings are terminated without prejudice.
 
John Guendelsberger

2013 WL 6921671 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KAMLESHWAR PRASAD
File: AXXX XX1 885 - Arlington, VA
December 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard W. Chang, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Adam L. Berg
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration; adjustment of status
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of India, appeals the December 23, 2011,1 denial of a motion to reopen and reconsider seeking adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). The Immigration Judge held that the respondent did not demonstrate prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review issues of law, discretion, or judgment de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent asserts that he was admitted to the United States on May 11, 2000, and he retained Earl S. David, Esquire, on April 5, 2001, to file an application for labor certification on his behalf before the April 30, 2001, “““grandfathering” deadline. See section 245(i)(1)(B)(i) of the Act. The record shows that the labor certification application was filed on July 13, 2001. On April 22, 2009, United States Citizenship and Immigration Services denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status because he could not demonstrate that a labor certification had been filed on his behalf on or before April 30, 2001. See id. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s renewed application for adjustment of status on September 29, 2011, and ordered his removal. The respondent filed no appeal with the Board. In the motion at issue, the respondent argued that the filing deadline of section 245(i)(1)(B)(i) of the Act should be equitably tolled due to the ineffective assistance of Mr. David.
 
The respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly, and J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). An applicant for reopening must further demonstrate prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988).
 
As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent has identified no legal authority supporting equitable tolling of the deadline of section 245(i)(1)(B)(i) of the Act. The respondent relies upon Piranej v. Mukasey, 516 F.3d 137 (2d Cir. 2008), which is not binding in this case arising within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit. See Matter of Anselmo, 20 I&N Dec. 25, 31 (BIA 1989) (explaining that the Board historically follows a court’s precedent in cases arising in that circuit). In any event, in remanding for fact-finding and a determination of whether Piranej had complied with Matter of Lozada, the court declined to address whether ineffective assistance of counsel could serve as a basis for equitable tolling of the April 30, 2001, grandfathering deadline under section 245(i) of the Act. Piranej, supra, at 145. Another court has answered this question in the negative by affirming the Board’s holding that the deadline under section 245(i) operates as a statute of repose, and thus is not subject to equitable tolling. Balam-Chuc v. Mukasey, 547 F.3d 1044, 1048-50 (9th Cir. 2008). Similarly, the Fourth Circuit has held that the principles of equitable tolling do not apply to statutes such as section 245(i)(1)(B)(i) of the Act “where strict satisfaction of a time limit may be required as a precondition to jurisdiction over a matter.” Harris v. Hutchinson, 209 F.3d 325, 328 (4th Cir. 2000). Therefore, as the respondent has not shown prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(i), we affirm the denial of his motion to reopen and reconsider.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD

While the Immigration Judge signed the decision on November 2, 2011, the coversheet accompanying the decision shows that it was issued on December 23, 2011.

2013 WL 6921682 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BERTHLAND BERTO
File: AXXX XX3 073 - New York, NY
December 12, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Glenn H. Bank, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Ghana, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s September 7, 2012, decision denying his motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The motion is untimely because it was not filed within 90 days of the final administrative order of removal.1 See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The motion is also number-barred because it is the second motion to reopen filed by the respondent. See section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established an exception to the time and number limitations. The respondent has not shown that ineffective assistance of counsel provides an exception in his case. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000) (ineffective assistance of counsel may warrant equitable tolling of the filing deadline for a motion to reopen removal proceedings). The respondent contends that his first counsel provided ineffective assistance by, inter alia, requesting voluntary departure on his behalf.2 However, he does not contend, and the record does not support, that he was not removable or that he was eligible for any other relief at that time. Moreover, even crediting the respondent’s assertion that he informed his first counsel that he was engaged to be married, he has not shown that it was an unreasonable tactical decision to pursue voluntary departure under the circumstances of this case, including the potential availability of an unlawful presence waiver under section 212(a)(9)(B)(v) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9)(B)(v).
 
*2 The respondent has likewise not shown that his second counsel provided ineffective assistance. Based, inter alia, on the facts surrounding his entry to the United States as alleged by the respondent, we are not persuaded by his contention that the timing and substance of the motion to reopen filed by his second counsel constitute ineffective assistance. In light of the respondent’s representation that he entered the United States with a United States passport, we conclude that he was not inspected by an immigration officer and thus was not admitted to the United States. See Matter of Pinzon, 26 I&N Dec. 189 (BIA 2013) (an alien who enters the United States by falsely claiming United States citizenship is not deemed to have been inspected by an immigration officer, so the entry is not an admission under section 101(a)(13)(A) of the Act). Because he has not shown that he was inspected and admitted, the respondent has not shown prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status. See section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a) (providing, inter alia, that to be eligible for adjustment of status, an applicant must have been inspected and admitted or paroled into the United States). We therefore conclude that the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by the performance of his second counsel. See Debeatham v. Holder, 602 F.3d 481, 485 (2d Cir. 2010) (an alien claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must show prejudice resulting from counsel’s alleged deficiencies). Thus we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion and will dismiss the appeal.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In a decision dated December 10, 2009, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent voluntary departure until April 9, 2010. When the respondent failed to depart the United States before the expiration of the voluntary departure period, the order of removal became effective and became the final administrative order of removal from which the 90-day deadline is calculated.

2

We are also not persuaded by the respondent’s assertions that filing the initial adjustment application or that admitting removability was unreasonable under the circumstances of this case.

2013 WL 6921648 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CONCEPCION YAMILETH ROSERO
File: AXXX XX5 965 - New York, NY
December 12, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Teodora Arias, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Colombia. The Board’s final administrative decision was issued on February 23, 2011, when the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act and a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act. The respondent’s motion to reopen was received on October 10, 2013. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). As the record reflects that the Board did not receive the motion to reopen until October 10, 2013, the motion was filed out of time. The respondent has not alleged or shown that she is subject to any of the exceptions to the time limitation on motions to reopen under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). As the respondent refers to misrepresentations by former counsel, we will consider whether equitable tolling of the time limit on motions to reopen due to ineffective assistance is warranted.
 
The respondent was represented by counsel before the Immigration Judge, but was considered pro se on appeal. The respondent contends that she was misrepresented in criminal proceedings and that she received ineffective assistance from her former attorney before the Immigration Judge. However, with respect to immigration proceedings, there is no indication that the respondent has filed any complaints against her former attorney or that she provided her with notice of the ineffective assistance claim so that she had an adequate opportunity to respond thereto. We find that the respondent has not met the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Jian Yun Zheng v. United States Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 47 (2d Cir. 2005); Esposito v. INS, 987 F.2d 108, 110-111 (2d Cir. 1993). We also note that as the respondent has not provided any detailed explanation for her delay in filing the motion for any misrepresentation before the Immigration Judge, we find that the respondent has not shown that she exercised due diligence in seeking reopening based on the claimed ineffective assistance. Consequently, equitable tolling is not warranted. See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir.2006) (“[N]o matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled.”); Iavorski v. United States INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134-35 (2d Cir. 2000). We also find no basis for granting reopening sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reopen and the request for a stay of removal will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and the request for a stay of removal are denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDITH TRUCKENBROD PETER TRUCKENBROD
Files: AXXX XX0 810 - Honolulu, HI
AXXX XX0 809
December 11, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
James A. Stanton, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Heith M. Kaneshige
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination; adjustment of status
 
The respondents, natives and citizens of Switzerland, appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 13, 2012, finding the respondents removable as charged and denying their applications for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the determination of credibility. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). Contrary to the respondent’s argument on appeal, the Immigration Judge did not err when she found the respondents removable under section 237(a)(1)(B) as their E-2 visas expired in 1995. The respondents concede that their visas expired in 1995 (Br. at 15). The respondents appear to argue that they are not removable because their former counsel failed to file an extension for their visas. The respondents have not, however, complied with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000). The respondents are removable as charged.
 
Assuming that the Immigration Judge had jurisdiction in this case to consider the respondent’s applications for adjustment of status, we affirm her conclusion that the respondents are not eligible as the applications they submitted are incomplete. In addition, we affirm for the reasons stated in the Immigration Judge’s decision that the respondents do not merit adjustment of status in discretion if they were eligible (I.J. at 5). As noted by the Immigration Judge, the misrepresentation made by the respondents during the bankruptcy proceedings is a strong negative factor, and the respondents have not presented sufficient positive equities that would outweigh this factor. Finally, we reject the respondent’s contention that they are not removable because they have an approved EB-5 visa. The respondents’ applications for adjustment of status on the basis of this visa were denied on February 9, 2009 (Exh. 4). The respondents were informed at that time that they were in violation of their nonimmigrant status. Accordingly, the respondents’ appeal will be dismissed.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 6921655 (BIA)

2013 WL 6921656 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTOR ROSEL A.K.A. VICTOR ROSEL SANTOS
File: AXXX XX4 325 - New York, NY
December 9, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Benjamin L. Simpson, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic who was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident in 2009, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated July 17, 2013, ordering his removal from the United States. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to order the respondent’s removal from the United States. The Immigration Judge properly concluded that the respondent is subject to removal from the United States under the provisions of sections 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), (B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii), (B)(i), as a result of his August 2012 New York state conviction for Criminal Possession of a Controlled Substance (Cocaine) with Intent to Sell (I.J. at 1-3; Exh. 1; Tr. at 13). As recognized by the Immigration Judge, the respondent’s argument that his conviction does not constitute a conviction for a drug trafficking aggravated felony because New York’s definition of “sell” includes offering to give a controlled substance has recently been rejected by the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit (I.J. at 3). Pascual v. Holder, 723 F.3d 156 (2d Cir. 2013). Moreover, despite the respondent’s claims that his criminal defense counsel provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel, it is well-established that an alien may not collaterally attack a conviction in removal proceedings. See, e.g., Higgins v. Holder, 677 F.3d 97, 106-07 (2d Cir. 2012); Varughese v. Holder, 629 F.3d 272, 275 n. 3 (2d Cir. 2010); Lanferman v. BIA, 576 F.3d 84, 88 (2d Cir. 2009); Matter of Cuellar, 25 I&N Dec. 850, 855 (BIA 2012); Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). As such, in the absence of evidence that his conviction has been vacated or modified, his conviction remains a valid predicate upon which he can be removed from this country.
 
*2 As the respondent is subject to removal from the United States, it is his burden to establish eligibility for relief from removal. Section 240(c)(4)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). However, the respondent has not filed an application for any form of relief from removal which can be granted by an Immigration Judge or this Board. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.1(a)(1) (enumerating many of the forms of relief from removal which can be granted by an Immigration Judge).
 
The respondent’s desire to seek post-conviction relief with the New York courts did not amount to “good cause” for a continuance. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 1240.6. The respondent’s request for a continuance was explicitly opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 40). Moreover, even though the respondent had presented evidence that he had filed a motion with the New York courts to vacate his conviction, he did not present any evidence that such motion would be adjudicated in the foreseeable future (I.J. at 3). See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980) (holding that the statements of counsel are not evidence). Additionally, the respondent was granted continuances on May 17, 2013 (Tr. at 7), May 31, 2013 (Tr. at 18), and June 19, 2013 (Tr. at 26).1 Overall, considering these factors, as well as the speculative nature of the outcome of the nature of post-conviction proceedings, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s request for a continuance.
 
The respondent has also not established that he was prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his request for a continuance. Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). Now, more than 4 months after the Immigration Judge entered a removal order on July 17, 2013, the respondent has yet to present evidence that his motion for post-conviction relief has been adjudicated in his favor or that said motion has been scheduled to be adjudicated in the near future. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 403 (BIA 1991) (noting that we are not favorably disposed to the practice of waiting until the conclusion of the administrative appeal process to file a motion that seeks to offer additional evidence regarding the matter previously in issue). Accordingly, we will not disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to order the respondent’s removal from the United States to the Dominican Republic on the basis of the charges set forth in the Notice to Appear. The following order is entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On July 9, 2013, the Immigration Judge continued these proceedings upon her own motion (Tr. at 29-30).
