2013 WL 6921667 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PIOTR CHORAZY
File: AXXX XX4 523 - Kansas City, MO
December 5, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raymond R. Bolourtchi, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jayme Salinardi
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Poland, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 2, 2013. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent requested continuances on the grounds that the conviction, which serves as the basis for removal, and which renders him ineligible for relief from removal, is being challenged before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).2 The respondent says he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction, and he has filed a motion to vacate it. The respondent was granted continuances from January 22, 2013, until July 2, 2013. However, the respondent was not granted any further continuance. On appeal, the respondent indicates that motion remains pending and the conviction has not been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).3 We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

3

In determining whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings, a variety of factors may be considered, including, but not limited to: the Department of Homeland Security’s response to the motion to continue and the reason for the continuance and any other relevant procedural factors. See Matter of Hashmi, supra.

2013 WL 6529290 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEANDRO BETTIOL A.K.A. LEANDREW BETTION
File: AXXX XX8 356 - Boston, MA
December 4, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George C. Maroun, Jr., Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; stay of removal
 
*1 The respondent is a native of Brazil and a citizen of Italy. He has timely appealed from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 24, 2013, denying his motion to reopen. He has also filed an emergency motion to stay removal. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal and deny the motion.
 
The respondent’s removal proceedings concluded when, on May 30, 2013, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent the privilege of voluntarily departing the United States on or before July 15, 2013. See section 240B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c. On July 17, 2013, the respondent filed a motion seeking to reopen his removal proceedings alleging that he did not knowingly and voluntarily request voluntary departure at his master calendar hearing or sign the written pleading submitted to the court. The motion asked that the proceedings be reopened, that the respondent be granted reasonable bond, and that “any further relief that may be just and reasonable” be granted as well.
 
In a decision dated July 24, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen after concluding that the respondent had neither shown prima facie eligibility for any relief from removal nor included with his motion any applications for relief. The Immigration Judge also noted that, even though the respondent was alleging that his prior counsel failed to properly advise him regarding voluntary departure and that he had not knowingly sought such relief, the court had explained to him the nature and consequences of voluntary departure and confirmed that he wished to apply for it. The Immigration Judge further pointed out that, even if the facts were as alleged by the respondent, the respondent had not complied with the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), nor established that he had been prejudiced by his prior counsel’s decision to seek voluntary departure (I.J. at 3).
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge summarily denied his motion because of his failure to indicate whether he might be eligible for any relief and argues that the Immigration Judge erred in not considering his affidavit accompanying the motion. See Respondent’s Notice of Appeal at 2, item no. 6.1
 
A respondent seeking reopening of his or her proceedings bears a “heavy burden” to establish new facts that will be proven at a hearing and the motion must be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material. See section 240(c)(7)(B) of the Act; see also INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988). The motion must also be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief. Unless the Immigration Judge is satisfied that the evidence is material, was not available, and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing, a motion to reopen will not be granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). The respondent must proffer evidence to demonstrate a reasonable likelihood of success on the merits. Matter of A-N- & R-M-N-, 22 I&N Dec. 953, 956 (BIA 1999). However, the Immigration Judge retains the discretion to deny a motion to reopen even if the moving party has established a prima facie case for relief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3).
 
*2 We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. We agree that the respondent, who has yet to submit an application for relief from removal or identify the form of relief he would seek if his motion were granted, has not established prima facie eligibility for any form of relief from removal (I.J. at 3). We also agree that the respondent has not shown compliance with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, nor proved prejudice. See Zeru v. Gonzales, 503 F.3d 59, 72 (1st Cir. 2007) (stating that “to succeed on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, [aliens] must show ‘a reasonable probability of prejudice’ resulting from their former representation”). We are not persuaded by the respondent’s appellate argument that the Immigration Judge erred in not considering his affidavit accompanying the motion. The fact that the decision did not explicitly refer to the respondent’s affidavit does not necessarily mean that the Immigration Judge disregarded it. Moreover, the affidavit only contained general assertions regarding the respondent’s desire to be allowed to remain in the United States and his belief that he received ineffective assistance from his prior counsel. It remained silent as to the issue of what form of relief from removal the respondent might be interested in if his proceedings were reopened.
 
We further find, after examining the totality of the respondent’s general allegations of deficiencies in his underlying removal proceedings, that the respondent has not demonstrated that sua sponte reopening is warranted. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999). As such, we decline to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. Additionally, inasmuch as the respondent has not demonstrated that reopening is warranted, his request for a stay of removal pending adjudication of his motion is moot. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 107-110 (BIA 2009). Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion is denied as moot.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent checked the box provided in his Notice to Appeal to indicate his intention to file a separate written brief or statement in support of his appeal, but he did not do so.

2013 WL 6529272 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MEIBE VILLUMSEN
File: AXXX XX7 483 - Boston, MA
November 29, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Lodged: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Brazil, appeals the Immigration Judge’s July 20, 2012, decision finding that the respondent is removable under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i). We review the Immigration Judge’s factual findings for clear error and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred by finding that the respondent illegally entered the United States. According to the respondent, the road on which she was traveling when she was stopped by a border patrol agent is entirely within Canada. See Respondent’s Brief at 1. The respondent did not raise this argument before the Immigration Judge, however. In fact, the respondent’s attorney argued that the respondent entered the United States at a Vermont port of entry (Tr. at 48-50). Further, the border patrol agent who stopped the respondent testified at the April 27, 2012, hearing (Tr. at 90). Agent Lahan testified that he stopped the respondent on February 7, 2009, when he was patrolling the border between Canada and Vermont (I.J. at 4; Tr. at 91-92). He said that he stopped the respondent’s car approximately 50-100 yards south of the border on a street that does not have a port of entry (I.J. at 4, 5; Tr. at 92-93).
 
The respondent also argues on appeal that Agent Lahan’s memory was poor and that he testified incorrectly about the direction of the road on which the respondent was traveling. See Respondent’s Brief at 4, 5. The respondent did not raise these factual issues before the Immigration Judge, however. See Matter of Jimenez-Santillano, 21 I&N Dec. 567, 570 n.2 (BIA 1996); Matter of Edwards, 20 I&N Dec. 191, 196 n.4 (BIA 1990). Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984) (the Board “is an appellate body whose function is to review, not to create, a record” and it is not appropriate to accept evidence proffered for the first time on appeal). Instead, before the Immigration Judge, the respondent’s attorney argued that the point of entry through which the respondent traveled was undefined and that the respondent lacked intent to enter at a place that was not a designated port of entry (I.J. at 8, 9; Tr. at 117-18).
 
*2 Further, to the extent that the respondent argues that her attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel, we note that the respondent has not satisfied the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639, 640 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (respondent must (1) present an affidavit explaining in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) inform the attorney of the allegations leveled against him and give him an opportunity to respond; and (3) explain whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not, and (4) demonstrate prejudice as a result of the alleged error). See Respondent’s Brief at 7-8.
 
For these reasons, we uphold the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent is removable under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 6529185 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FEDERICO ROJAS-VENCES
File: AXXX XX7 026 - Lumpkin, GA
November 25, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brian Kim, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand; stay of removal
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated July 2, 2013, ordering his removal from the United States. He has also filed a motion to reopen, remand, and stay his removal. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal and deny his motion.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. It is undisputed that the respondent is subject to removal from the United States because he is an alien who is present in this country without being admitted or paroled or who arrived at any time or place other than as designated by the Attorney General (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 71-73). See section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i). As the respondent is subject to removal from the United States, it is his burden to establish eligibility for relief from removal. See section 240(c)(4)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). However, the respondent does not allege that he is eligible for any form of relief from removal which can be granted by an Immigration Judge or this Board and would permit him to remain in this country. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.1(a)(1) (enumerating many of the forms of relief from removal which can be granted by an Immigration Judge). On appeal, the respondent does not pursue a request to voluntarily depart this country.
 
The respondent has not established that, in light of his claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, these proceedings should be remanded to the Immigration Judge. In particular, the respondent has not established that his former counsel’s performance was so deficient that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 371 F.3d 771, 775 (11th Cir. 2004). While the respondent argues that his former counsel should have pursued a claim to immigration benefits under the Department of Homeland Security’s Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (“DACA”) program, neither the Immigration Judge, nor this Board can grant the respondent DACA benefits. See generally Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 349-350 (BIA 1982). Instead, if the respondent wishes to pursue DACA benefits, such a request must be filed with United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”). Moreover, the respondent acknowledges on appeal that aliens who are subject to final orders of removal are eligible to request DACA benefits (Respondent’s Br. at 5). Thus, the respondent has not established that his former counsel has divested him of the ability to pursue DACA benefits.
 
*2 We do not consider the administrative closure of proceedings, which is merely a tool to temporarily remove a case from an Immigration Judge’s active calendar or from the Board’s docket, to be a potential “outcome” of these removal proceedings. Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 480 (BIA 1996). Even if these proceedings were administratively closed, the respondent has not established that, upon reopening, a different outcome of these proceedings would occur. If granted DACA benefits, the respondent would remain subject to removal from the United States under the provisions of section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act. Moreover, while DACA benefits may be permit an alien to temporarily remain in this country, such benefits, in themselves, do not lead to a permanent status.
 
Even assuming that administrative closure of removal proceedings was a potentially different outcome of removal proceedings, the respondent has not established that, in the absence of ineffective assistance of counsel, such measures would have been warranted. In order to fulfill DACA’s educational requirements, the respondent has indicated on page 2 of his Consideration for Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (Form I-821D) that he is enrolled in a GED class at the GED Academy Online. A copy of an e-mail from the GED Academy indicates that the respondent did not enroll in its program until July 30, 2013, approximately 4 weeks after the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States. Considering these circumstances, we conclude the respondent has not shown, in the absence of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, the administrative closure of these proceedings would have been appropriate based upon his yet-to-be commenced involvement in an online GED program. See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688, 696 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that the administrative closure of proceedings is not warranted based on a purely speculative event or action).
 
For the reasons set forth above, we conclude that the respondent has not demonstrating the requisite prejudice which would warrant remanded proceeding on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. As the respondent has not demonstrated that remanded proceedings are warranted, his request for a stay of removal is moot. Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 107-110 (BIA 2009). Accordingly, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to order the respondent’s removal from the United States to Mexico. The following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen, remand, and stay his removal is denied.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 6529185 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NHU AN THI VAN
File: AXXX XX9 488 - Los Angeles, CA
November 21, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Erin Mount, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)] - Conditional resident status terminated
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Vietnam, appeals the Immigration Judge’s April 11, 2012, decision. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s second untimely motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order that was entered on January 23, 2007. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded only (1) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (2) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act, or that the alien was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (ii) of the Act; see also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 723 (BIA 1999). Aliens raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in a motion may have the time limits on motions equitably tolled. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003). However, an alien raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim must establish that he has been diligent in raising the claim and must establish that he was prejudiced by his prior attorney’s alleged failures. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1184-85 (9th Cir. 2001) (requiring exercise of reasonable diligence in pursuing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim);
 
The respondent’s first motion to reopen, based on the ineffective assistance of counsel, was filed January 27, 2009. The motion was filed by her second attorney, Nguyen Hong Nhuan, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel by her first attorney, Neil V.X. Nguyen. The Immigration Judge denied the motion because the respondent (1) did not comply with the requirements of Matter of Compean, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009),1 and (2) provided no explanation for her 1 ½-year delay in filing the motion. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, supra. See February 25, 2009, I.J. at 2-3. The Immigration Judge’s decision was not appealed and is now final. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39.
 
*2 On February 3, 2012, the respondent filed a second untimely motion to reopen based on the ineffective assistance of Nguyen Hong Nhuan. The respondent claims that Nguyen Hong Nhuan provided her with ineffective assistance of counsel because he failed to file a complaint with the State bar against Neil V. X. Nguyen and that he did not file a motion to reopen in her case until July 2009, approximately 8 months after he was retained by the respondent. She stated that she did not discover that her motion to reopen had been denied until approximately September or October 2009.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not demonstrate due diligence in filing her second motion to reopen. In the decision on appeal, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen because she did not exercise due diligence between the discovery of the denial of her first motion to reopen, October 2009, and the filing of her second motion to reopen, February 3, 2012 (April 11, 2012, I.J. at 4-5).2 Before the Immigration Judge and on appeal, the respondent argued that she exercised due diligence and that her motion to reopen occurred within a reasonable time because she required that amount of time to obtain evidence in support of her motion (April 11, 2012, I.J. at 4-5; Respondent’s Br. at 4-5). See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 677 (9th Cir. 2011) (due diligence is determined by assessing when a reasonable person in the alien’s position would suspect fraud or error, what steps, if any, the alien took to investigate, and at what point the alien learns of the harm). As stated by the Immigration Judge, the respondent did not demonstrate why it took over 2 years to gather the evidence, which should have been in her possession, relevant to her ineffective assistance of counsel claim and file state bar complaints (April 11, 2012, I.J. at 4-5). On appeal, the respondent does not provide any further information or explanation regarding her lack of diligence.
 
The respondent does not appeal the Immigration Judge’s denial to reopen these proceedings sua sponte pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), and we decline to disturb his decision. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1134 (BIA 1999); see also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances). Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacated Matter of Compean, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), and reestablished the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Those requirements include filing an affidavit describing the scope of the representation agreement, notifying the prior attorney of the allegations, and lodging a complaint against the attorney with the proper disciplinary authorities. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. Here, the respondent did not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada regarding Neil V. X. Nguyen.

2

Regarding Nguyen Hong Nhuan, the respondent substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (Respondent’s Br., Tabs L, M, N).

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MAURICIO DIAZ
File: AXXX XX2 746 - Baltimore, MD
November 19, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ivan Yacub, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On March 13, 2013, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii) (alien convicted of an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 101(a)(43)(F), to wit: crime of violence), found him ineligible for relief from removal, and ordered his removal to El Salvador. The Immigration Judge also denied the respondent’s motion to continue the proceedings for post-conviction relief. On June 27, 2013, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the motion to continue. On September 25, 2013, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen before the Board. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security has not opposed this motion. The motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
During his removal hearing, the respondent, through former counsel, admitted that he had been convicted of second-degree assault under Md. Code Ann., Crim. Law § 3-203, and conceded that he was removable based on that conviction.
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit has observed that Maryland’s second-degree assault statute reaches both violent and nonviolent touching. Following the Immigration Judge’s decision, the circuit court concluded that convictions under that statute are not categorically crimes of violence. See Karimi v. Holder, 715 F.3d 561, 568 (4th Cir. 2013); U.S. v. Harcum, 587 F.3d 219, 224 (4th Cir. 2009). Also subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s decision, the U.S. Supreme Court addressed the appropriate application of an elements-based examination of a statute of conviction. See Descamps v. United States, 133 S.Ct. 2276 (2013). In light of the recent relevant decisions from the Supreme Court and the Fourth Circuit, the Board will grant the motion to reopen and remand the record to permit the Immigration Judge to reexamine the issue of removability in the first instance, and to enter a new decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (limiting the Board’s fact-finding ability on appeal). The Board, however, expresses no opinion regarding the outcome of proceedings on remand.1
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the decision.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Inasmuch as the proceedings will be remanded for a new decision regarding removability, the Board will not at this time consider the ineffective assistance of counsel claim raised in the instant motion.

2013 WL 6529166 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE SAUL REYES A.K.A. JOSE SAUL REYES-GONZALEZ A.K.A. JOSE REYES-GONZALEZ A.K.A. JOSE REYES
File: AXXX XX6 135 - Dallas, TX
November 15, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fernando Dubove, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margot Merrill-Johnson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 8, 2013. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent requests remand on the grounds that the conviction, which serves as the basis for removal, and which renders him ineligible for relief from removal, is being challenged before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).2 The respondent has filed a writ of habeas corpus to vacate the conviction. The respondent has offered evidence of that with the appeal. However, the conviction has not been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue a post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). A post-conviction motion to vacate a conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMUEL ADJEI
File: AXXX XX9 186 - Charlotte, NC
November 14, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kwadwo A. Opoku, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Colleen E. Taylor
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening and remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Ghana, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s April 2, 2012, decision denying his motion for a continuance and ordering him removed from the United States. The Department of Homeland Security has filed a brief in response to the respondent’s appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent states that he does not challenge the Immigration Judge’s decision denying a continuance and ordering him removed from the United States. Rather, he is requesting that the Board reopen the proceedings and remand the record to the Immigration Judge because he received ineffective assistance from his prior attorney. While he has not formally filed a motion to reopen, he is requesting the reopening of his case. A request for reopening and remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in the Board’s decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d sub nom. Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Matter of Lozada, supra, we held that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must (1) be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) provide evidence that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned has been informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and (3) indicate whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. In addition to these requirements, an alien alleging ineffective assistance of counsel must show that he or she was prejudiced by the actions or inactions of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
In the instant case, the respondent has not complied with any of the above-mentioned requirements. He has not even provided an affidavit outlining the agreement he had with his prior counsel or what representations his former attorney made to him. Nor has he shown that he has notified former counsel of the allegations against him or indicated whether he has filed a complaint with appropriate disciplinary authorities. Moreover, he has not alleged how the proceeding below was so fundamentally unfair that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case. As such, we do not find that the respondent has met his burden of proof to warrant reopening and remanding the proceedings to the Immigration Court.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 6628287 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HILIARIO CISNEROS-SANCHEZ
File: AXXX XX1 113 - San Diego, CA
November 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David W. Williams, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ted Y. Yamada
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 18, 2012, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded.
 
The respondent failed to timely file applications for relief by May 2, 2012, the deadline set by the Immigration Judge on October 19, 2011. In a decision dated May 9, 2012, the Immigration Judge deemed the applications abandoned and granted voluntary departure to the respondent. On June 8, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, which included a sworn statement from the respondent’s counsel, David Williams. In that statement, Mr. Williams averred that his employer retired from practice in February 2012, resulting in Mr. Williams taking over the employer’s caseload. Mr. Williams then admitted that due to administrative changes, he failed to properly calendar the May 2, 2012, filing deadline. By order dated July 18, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding that the respondent had failed to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The U.S. Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case lies, has held that strict compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, is not required when the ineffectiveness of counsel was plain on its face. Tamang v. Holder, 598 F.3d 1083, 1090 (9th Cir. 2010); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (ineffectiveness was plain on its face where petitioner’s former counsel misrepresented to petitioner that his application had been filed and petitioner later filed a declaration complying with Lozada); Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934, 938 (9th Cir. 2003) (ineffectiveness was plain on its face where former counsel mis-calendared the removal hearing, petitioner failed to appear at the hearing, and declarations were filed that complied with the first two prongs of Lozada); Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 824-25 (9th Cir. 2003) (ineffectiveness was plain on its face where former counsel forgot to file the brief because he was transitioning to a new job and taking over a former colleague’s caseload, and declarations were filed satisfying the first prong of Lozada).
 
*2 Here, Mr. Williams has admitted that the failure to timely file the applications was due to his error, not the respondent’s. Consequently, we find that ineffectiveness of counsel is plain on the face of the record. See Lin v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 1014, 1027 (9th Cir. 2004) (holding that the court “““must consider the underlying merits of the case to come to a tentative conclusion as to whether [a petitioner’s] claim, if properly presented, would be viable,” notwithstanding the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s decisions of July 18, 2012, and May 9, 2012, are vacated and the proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: XINGCHEN YE
File: AXXX XX4 496 - San Francisco, CA
November 8, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Zachary Nightingale, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James B. Gildea
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings; remand
 
*1 The respondent has appealed an Immigration Judge’s decision of August 7, 2012, finding that the respondent had abandoned his lawful permanent resident (“LPR”) status. While the appeal was pending, the respondent filed a motion to remand, which the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has opposed. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Where, as here, the respondent has a colorable claim of LPR status, the burden is on the DHS to demonstrate by clear, unequivocal, and convincing evidence that the respondent abandoned that status. Khoshfahm v. Holder, 655 F.3d 1147 (9th Cir. 2011). In order to qualify as a returning resident, an alien “must be returning to an unrelinquished lawful permanent residence after a temporary visit abroad.” See Singh v. Reno, 113 F.3d 1512, 1514 (9th Cir. 1997). A trip is a “temporary visit abroad” if (a) it is for a “relatively short” period, fixed by some early event; or (b) the trip will terminate upon the occurrence of an event that has a reasonable possibility of occurring within a relatively short period of time. See Chavez-Ramirez v. INS, 792 F.2d 932, 937 (9th Cir. 1986). If the alien’s trip abroad is not “relatively short,” it is a “temporary visit abroad” only if the alien has “a continuous, uninterrupted intention to return to the United States during the entirety of her visit.” See Khodagholian v. Ashcroft, 335 F.3d 1003 (9th Cir. 2003). Factors to be considered in evaluating the respondent’s intent to return to the United States include: “the alien’s family ties, property holdings, and business affiliations within the United States, and the alien’s family, property, and business ties in the foreign country.” Singh v. Reno, supra, at 1514-15.
 
The respondent was granted LPR status in 2004 and was placed in removal proceedings in April 2010. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had abandoned his status, citing to the fact that between 2004 and 2010, the respondent spent a minimal number of days in the United States, and he got married, had children, and started his own business in China.
 
*2 The Immigration Judge specifically noted the dearth of documentary evidence of the respondent’s ties to the United States (I.J. at 10), the lack of affidavits from family or friends (I.J. at 12-13), the lack of proof that, he maintained any rental units or had any property ownership in the United States (I.J. at 10, 12).
 
With his motion, the respondent has submitted evidence of his United States business ties (Motion, Exhs. B1-C6), affidavits from his United States citizen family members (Motion, Exh. A, 2-4), documentation of the bank account he maintained in the United States (Exh. A12), as well as evidence he says indicates that, during the years in question, he paid rent to maintain a residence here (Motion, Exh. A2) and owned, registered, and paid taxes on the car which he kept in the United States (Motion, Exh. A, 5-8). The respondent has also submitted medical evidence regarding his father’s illness and death which he said caused him to remain in China in 2005, as well as evidence of his own medical problem that he said delayed his return in 2010 (Motion, Exhs. D and E).
 
Although the evidence proffered by the respondent was previously available, he asserts a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his former attorney for failing to advise the respondent to produce such evidence (Motion, at 14-20). He also claims that his attorney failed to advise him of the consequences of a removal order, namely the 10-year bar from returning to the United States, and did not give him the option of withdrawing or renouncing his LPR status (Motion, at 23; Tr. at 100-08).
 
The respondent has complied with the requirements set forth in in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for ineffective assistance of counsel claims (Motion, Exh. H, 2-5). We will remand for the Immigration Judge to evaluate the ineffective assistance claim and to determine whether the respondent has established the requisite prejudice. See Dent v. Holder, 627 F.3d 365, 373 (9th Cir. 2010) (prejudice “means that the outcome of the proceeding may have been affected by the alleged violation”).1 Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

1

Given our disposition of the motion, we need not adjudicate the respondent’s appeal at this time.

IN RE: HONG ZHENG

November 4, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Margaret Wong, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated April 20, 2012, denying her motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against her on February 22, 2012. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including any determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge properly ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States based upon her prior admission to the factual allegations contained in the Notice to Appear and concession of removability and her failure to appear for the hearing. Section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent timely filed a motion to reopen in which she alleged “exceptional circumstances” for her failure to appear and alternatively requested sua sponte reopening of the proceedings. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and 240(e)(1) of the Act; Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion, and this appeal followed.
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s April 20, 2012, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings and rescind the removal order against her. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). Section 240(e)(1) of the Act states that the “term ‘exceptional circumstances’ refers to exceptional circumstances (such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.” The respondent had been on notice of the hearing date for more than 4 months (I.J. at 3; Exh. 7). Neither her attorney’s representation that he would seek administrative closure or a continuance of the proceedings, nor the fact that the Immigration Judge could not adjudicate the respondent’s adjustment application because the visa petition was still pending, excused the respondent’s absence at the hearing based on her assumption that the hearing would be rescheduled. See Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996) (the filing of a motion for a continuance does not excuse an alien from failing to appear). The respondent has indicated that she is not making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (Respondent’s brief at 10). The Immigration Judge correctly noted that the respondent had not complied with the requirements for such a claim and that any claimed ineffectiveness of the prior attorney is not plain on the face of the record where the respondent was notified of the hearing date.
 
*2 The respondent received proper notice of the hearing and did not establish exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear. Additionally, sua sponte authority for reopening proceedings is invoked sparingly and only for truly exceptional situations. The respondent has not demonstrated that the application of such an extraordinary remedy is warranted in this case. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the removal order. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: ZHE LIU A.K.A. LIU ZHE
File: AXXX XX7 062 - Saipan, MP
October 31, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mun Su Park, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Chandu Latey
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening; stay of removal
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on July 24, 2013, when we issued an order upholding an Immigration Judge’s removal order and denied a motion to remand this case for further proceedings. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reconsider that decision. She also seeks reopening of the removal proceedings and a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief in opposition to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent seeks reconsideration and/or reopening of these proceedings due to the alleged ineffective assistance of her former attorney, Stephen Woodruff, who represented her before the Immigration Judge. The respondent contends that Woodruff erred in failing to advise her of her apparent eligibility for pre-conclusion or post-conclusion voluntary departure.
 
To the extent that the respondent seeks reconsideration, she has failed to identify any error of fact or law in our prior decision that would warrant its reconsideration. See section 240(c)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). The respondent’s newly raised argument concerning Woodruffs’ alleged ineffective assistance can and should have been raised previously and, as such, is not a proper basis for reconsideration of our prior decision. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (stating that “[a] motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied,” and that “[a]rguments for consideration on appeal should all be submitted at one time, rather than in piecemeal fashion.” (citation omitted)).
 
We also decline to reopen these proceedings. As a preliminary matter, the instant motion is unaccompanied by any new, material evidence that would establish the respondent’s prima facie eligibility for the relief that she seeks. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992).
 
Further, the respondent’s motion does not comply with any of the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for raising an ineffective-assistance-of-counsel claim, and there is no clear and obvious ineffective assistance on this record with respect to the issue of voluntary departure. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (substantial fulfillment of the procedural requirements of Lozada, supra, is generally necessary to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel unless the record reflects a clear and obvious case of such). We also discern no prejudice where, as here, the respondent has not shown that Woodruff’s alleged failure to inform her of apparent eligibility for pre-conclusion or post-conclusion voluntary departure may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855 (9th Cir. 2004) (a claim of ineffective representation must show that the allegedly deficient performance of former counsel may have affected the outcome in the case). The respondent has not meaningfully shown that she is even eligible for voluntary departure or that she would, in fact, have sought voluntary departure if Woodruff had advised her of the availability of such relief.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion for reconsideration is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 5872179 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JIMMY RAMIREZ PICHARDO A.K.A. CLAUDIO RAMIREZ A.K.A. JIMMY RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX8 624 - Boston, MA
October 21, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Rubin, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic who was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident in 1990, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated July 10, 2013, denying his request for a continuance and ordering his removal from the United States. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. On appeal, the respondent does not meaningfully dispute that, at the present time, he is subject to removal from the United States under the provisions of sections 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), (B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), (B)(i), as a result of his December 2007 Massachusetts conviction for Distribution of a Class A Controlled Substance (heroin) (I.J. at 1-2; Exhs. 1, 2, 3). The respondent does not allege that, in light of his conviction, he has established eligibility for any form of relief from removal which can be granted to him during the course of these proceedings. See section 240(c)(4)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1240.1(a)(1) (enumerating many of the forms of relief from removal which can be granted by an Immigration Judge).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s request for a continuance, which was opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 21). The respondent’s desire to seek post-conviction relief with the Massachusetts courts did not amount to “good cause” for a further continuance of these proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. The respondent does not claim that, at the time of his ultimate removal hearing, he had actually filed for post-conviction relief.1 Cf. Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009) (recognizing that a continuance may be warranted where an alien has presented actual evidence that he is the beneficiary of a pending immigrant visa petition). Moreover, the respondent did not articulate sufficient facts to hold that there was a likelihood that his yet-to-be filed post-conviction relief claims would be successful. See Matter of Sanchez Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 815 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that an alien seeking U nonimmigrant status should not be granted a continuance “as a dilatory tactic to forestall the conclusion of removal proceedings” where it is unlikely that his application will be granted). While the respondent does refer to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010), in his appeal brief, he has not articulated sufficient facts to hold that his conviction would be amenable to modification or vacatur under such decision.2 Considering these circumstances, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s request for a continuance and order his removal from the United States to the Dominican Republic. The following order is entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD

1

On appeal, the respondent, through counsel, claims that he filed a Motion for New Trial with the Worchester, Massachusetts Superior Court on August 19, 2013. However, such claim is not supported by any evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980) (recognizing that the statements of counsel are not evidence). Moreover, the respondent has not filed a motion to remand to consider such alleged new developments in his criminal case. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984) (“The Board is an appellate body whose function is to review, not to create, a record.”).

We acknowledge that, during the pendency of this appeal, the Supreme Judicial Court of Massachusetts held that, in certain circumstances, Padilla can be applied retroactively. Com. v. Sylvain, 466 Mass. 422 (Mass. 2013). Nonetheless, the respondent has not articulated sufficient facts to demonstrate that the Massachusetts courts would likely hold that he was provided ineffective assistance of counsel during the course of his criminal proceedings.

2013 WL 5872185 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZHU XIA LI
File: AXXX XX7 741 - Los Angeles, CA
October 18, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Keith R. Ayers, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of China, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s June 18, 2013, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings, in which the respondent was ordered removed in absentia under section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5). The respondent has also submitted evidence, which we will construe as a motion to remand the proceedings to the Immigration Judge. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion denied.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the Immigration Judge’s determination of credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision that the respondent did not establish exceptional circumstances for not appearing at her hearing. The respondent claims, inter alia, that she suffered ineffective assistance of counsel, where a prior attorney - Walter Burrier, Esquire - allegedly advised her that appearing at the hearing “was not worth it” and she would be returned to China immediately if she appeared without counsel. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, dated May 23, 2013, tab 1 at 6. But the Immigration Judge provided the respondent with oral and written notice of the date, time, and location of her removal August 4, 2005, hearing, as well as the consequences for failing to appear (I.J. at 4). See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, dated May 23, 2013, tab 1 at 6. Thus, the respondent knew that failing to appear at her hearing could result in the Immigration Judge issuing an in absentia removal order. The respondent received proper notice of her removal hearing and was on notice of the consequences of not appearing. Her alleged fear of being immediately removed to China does not establish exceptional circumstances for failing to appear.
 
Nor are we persuaded that the respondent has established ineffective assistance of prior counsel. As the Immigration Judge found, the respondent did not sufficiently comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) in order to raise ineffective assistance of prior counsel. The respondent has not submitted sufficient evidence that she - or her current counsel - informed counsel-Burrier or counsel-Cindy Chang of their alleged deficient performance, and she did not file disciplinary complaints against either attorney. Although the respondent’s counsel has submitted a vague affidavit indicating that he has communicated with counsel-Burrier and counsel-Chang, he has not identified the circumstances of the alleged discussions or when they occurred. See Respondent’s Appeal Brief, tab 4. Counsel’s vague affidavit is insufficient to meet the Lozada requirements. And given the nature of the respondent’s allegations against counsel-Burrier, compliance with Lozada is particularly important here, as counsel should have the opportunity to address the respondent’s assertion that he essentially advised her to ignore an Immigration Court order to attend her hearing.
 
*2 We also agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not diligently raise ineffective assistance, where she waited over 7 years to present the claims against her prior attorneys. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003). The respondent has not adequately shown that she was unaware of the in absentia removal order at the time it was issued. Nor has she demonstrated that she maintained regular communication with prior counsel in order to stay abreast of the status of the case. Further, given that the Immigration Judge advised the respondent of the consequences of not appearing at her hearing, she should have been aware that the Immigration Judge would issue an in absentia removal order when she did not attend her August 2005 hearing. Thus, the respondent has not shown the requisite due diligence to support her ineffective assistance of counsel claims.
 
In conjunction with the appeal, the respondent has submitted evidence, which we will treat as a motion to remand. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4). The motion will be denied, as the only evidence attached to the respondent’s appeal brief that does not predate the respondent’s May 2013 motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, consists of purported Internet-based searches of the current status of her former attorneys, and current counsel’s affidavit discussed above. The respondent has not established that this evidence was not reasonably available and could not have been presented with her motion; therefore, it does not comply with the regulatory requirements for a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Alternatively, even if we considered the documents, they would not alter our conclusion that the respondent has not met her burden of demonstrating an exceptional circumstance for not appearing at her hearing below.
 
ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 5872185 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HANS CHARLES ZIZI A.K.A. CHARLES ZIZI A.K.A. HANS CHARLES FRANCOIS ZIZI A.K.A. HANS CHARLES F. ZIZI
File: AXXX XX2 128 - New York, NY
October 18, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Shari L. Astalos, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s June 14, 2013, decision denying his timely-filed motion to reopen these removal proceedings. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i).
 
As an initial matter, it appears that the respondent departed from the United States while his appeal was pending, thus resulting in the withdrawal of his appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.4 (providing that “[d]eparture from the United States of a person who is the subject of . . . removal proceedings . . . subsequent to the taking of an appeal, but prior to a decision thereon, shall constitute a withdrawal of the appeal, and the initial decision in the case shall be final to the same extent as though no appeal had been taken). In particular, counsel for the respondent initially requested a withdrawal of the appeal, but then submitted a request to reinstate the appeal, stating that withdrawal was mistakenly requested due to difficulties communicating with the respondent because he was in Haiti. Since the respondent has departed the United States and is in Haiti, such departure would ordinarily be considered as a withdrawal of the appeal.
 
However, because the circumstances of his asserted departure are unclear, and because the decision of the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit in Luna v. Holder, 637 F.3d 85 (2d Cir. 2011), may require the Board to reach the merits of the appeal of the denial of the respondent’s timely motion to reopen, regardless of his departure, we alternatively consider the arguments raised on appeal. We are not persuaded of any error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not meet his burden of demonstrating that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent does not dispute that, because of his criminal convictions, he had been found to be subject to mandatory detention. Nor does he dispute the Immigration Judge’s statement that he advised the respondent of his possible eligibility for relief from removal (I.J. at 4). As the Immigration Judge found, the recording of the hearing confirms that the respondent declined to pursue relief and waived appeal of the final order of removal because he did not wish to be detained for the time that would be required to adjudicate any application for relief. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
David L Neal
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 5872196 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DENEISE BENJAMIN-STUBBS
File: AXXX XX5 916 - Miami, FL
October 17, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Merrick J. Dammar, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 30, 2012. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under a clearly erroneous standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
As reflected in the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent was ordered removed in absentia on August 5, 1998, when she failed to appear for a master calendar hearing. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent received notice of the August 5, 1998, hearing because it was personally served on the respondent’s attorney of record, Chandler R. Finley, Esquire, on June 10, 1998, who in turn mailed such notice to the respondent’s last known address. On appeal, the respondent argues that there was no constructive service because the Immigration Judge granted Mr. Findley’s motion to withdraw as counsel prior to the August 5, 1998, hearing. The respondent separately argues that her failure to appear should be excused due to ineffective assistance of counsel because a representative at a free legal clinic advised her not to go to Immigration Court as he planned to obtain a continuance or adjournment of the proceedings.
 
The Immigration Judge correctly found that the respondent had proper notice of the August 5, 1998, hearing. Although Mr. Findley’s motion to withdraw as counsel was based in part on the representation that he was never requested or retained by the respondent to represent her in these proceedings, we take administrative notice that the record shows that Mr. Findley had filed a Notice of Entry of Appear (Form EOIR-28) with the Immigration Court, and therefore was counsel of record when served with notice of the August 5, 1998, hearing. Therefore, we agree that the respondent received constructive notice of the August 5, 1998, hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2). Moreover, the respondent has indicated that she received actual notice, inasmuch as she admits that she was aware of and discussed whether to attend the hearing with a free legal clinic representative.
 
The Immigration Judge correctly found that the respondent cannot seek rescission of the in absentia order based on ineffective assistance of counsel as an exceptional circumstance because her motion to reopen was filed more than 180 days after the in absentia order of removal was entered. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). It is also undisputed that she has not complied with the procedural requirements outlined in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for filing a claim of effective assistance. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996). Moreover, even assuming that the respondent’s failure to attend the August 5, 1998, hearing was due to erroneous legal advice, we find that she failed to act with due diligence in this matter, such that equitable tolling of the 180 day filing deadline should be considered. See generally Avila-Santoyo v. United States Atty. Gen., 713 F.3d 1357, 1358 (11th 2013) (equitable tolling generally requires litigant to show (1) that she has been pursuing her rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in her way).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 5872196 (BIA)

2013 WL 5872200 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WALTER ALEXANDER AGUILAR-RIVERA
File: AXXX XX9 519 - San Antonio, TX
October 15, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Juan Carlos Penaflor, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Eric Bales
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated June 13, 2013, denying his motion to reopen. The respondent’s appeal, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), will be dismissed.1
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
On August 17, 2006, the Immigration Court mailed the respondent’s former attorney a Notice of Hearing in Removal Proceedings which informed the respondent that a removal hearing had been scheduled in this matter on October 3, 2006 (I.J. at 2; Exh. 2). As the respondent did not appear at his removal hearing, he was ordered removed from the United States to El Salvador in absentia (I.J. at 2). See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent filed his motion to reopen with the Immigration Court on March 6, 2013 (I.J. at 2).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely under the general rule requiring aliens to file a motion to reopen within 90 days of the entry of a final administrative order of removal. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act.
 
The respondent has not demonstrated that these removal proceedings should be reopened on account of lack of notice (I.J. at 2-3). See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Here, the Immigration Court fulfilled its duty of notifying the respondent of the place, time, and date of his hearing by mailing the aforementioned Notice of Hearing in Removal Proceedings to the respondent’s former counsel. Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985). While the respondent argues that his former counsel did not represent him after he was released from detention, the record is devoid of evidence that his former counsel moved to withdraw from this matter. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.17(b) (stating that an Immigration Judge may permit an attorney to withdraw “only upon oral or written motion”); Matter of Rosales, 19 I&N Dec. 655 (BIA 1988). The respondent has not established that the Postal Service did not deliver the notice to his former counsel. See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008). The mere allegation that the respondent’s former attorney did not advise the respondent of his hearing does not establish that he did not receive notice of his hearing in accordance with the applicable statutes and regulations. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.26(c)(2), 1292.5(a). Accordingly, we conclude that, by mailing the Notice of Hearing in Removal Proceedings to the respondent’s former counsel, the respondent was placed on notice of his removal hearing and reopening is not warranted on account of lack of notice.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent’s appellate arguments (i.e., a claim that his former counsel did not notify him of the time, date and place of his aforementioned removal hearing or did not file a motion to withdraw) can be construed as allegation that ineffective assistance of counsel amounted to an “““exceptional circumstance” causing his failure to appear at his hearing, such claims are untimely under section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act and not in compliance with the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
As the respondent has not otherwise established that these proceedings should be reopened sua sponte, we will not disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

On appeal, the respondent has presented numerous documents. The Board does not consider evidence first provided on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv); Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984). Additionally, a request for a remand for the Immigration Judge to consider such evidence is number-barred under section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Act. See Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000).

2013 WL 5872208 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TOMASI VOSAICAKE NAKAUTOGA
File: AXXX XX4 379 - San Antonio, TX
October 10, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nadia Farah, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Clay Martin
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On July 20, 2004, an in absentia order of removal was entered against the respondent, a native and citizen of Fiji. On March 18, 2013, a motion to reopen these proceedings was filed by the respondent. In a decision dated April 11, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied reopening. This appeal now follows.
 
The Board reviews findings of fact by an Immigration Judge only to determine whether they are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 464-65 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent reiterates that he never received notice of his hearing and that the Immigration Judge ignored and improperly discredited his evidence, establishing nonreceipt and his efforts to seek legal representation as soon as he learned of the removal order (Br. at 5-6). We find that the respondent did not meet his burden of proving nonreceipt of his hearing notice. See Matter of C-R-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 677 (BIA 2008) (the respondent overcame the presumption of delivery where he submitted affidavits indicating that he did not receive notice of the hearing, had previously filed an asylum application and appeared for his first removal hearing, and exercised due diligence in promptly obtaining counsel and requesting reopening of the proceedings); see Matter of Chicas, 19 I&N Dec. 114 (BIA 1984) (regarding the submission of evidence to prove nonreceipt).
 
The evidence proves that the in absentia order of removal was proper. In this regard, the respondent does not dispute that notice of his July 20, 2004, hearing was sent to his last-known address (430 Sellers Street, Apt. 28, Glendora, CA 91741) where he lived with his brother, Jamie (Br. at 2). He concedes that in March of 2004, he moved again. At that time, the Notice of Hearing had not yet been sent. The respondent below and on appeal does not argue that when he moved from his brother’s house, he did not file a change of address form, EOIR-33, as required by the court. The record fails to contain a change of address form, as well. Rather, it appears to have been the respondent’s assumption that his brother would receive and deliver any court notices to his new address (Br. at 2-3). This assumption, however, is inconsistent with the respondent’s duty to the court. We therefore agree with the Immigration Judge that his claim of nonreceipt is without merit and to the extent that he pleads that he had ineffective assistance of counsel, the facts he relies on, arose after he failed to attend his hearing.
 
*2 Finally, we decline to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen. The respondent has failed to present exceptional circumstances which would support the exercise of our limited discretion to reopen sua sponte. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999). Sua sponte authority may not be used as “a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, when enforcing them might result in hardship.” See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Here, it is significant that the respondent only diligently pursued reopening after finding out in 2007 that he could not adjust his status based on the outstanding order of removal from 2004. Evidently, at no time prior to his marriage on June 25, 2005, or before his wife filed an I-130 (Petition for Alien Relative) did the respondent seek to ascertain what his status was after the Notice to Appear issued. For the foregoing reasons, the denial of reopening is affirmed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
