Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO JAVIER RIVERA
File: AXXX XX5 498 - Dallas, TX1
October 10, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fernando Dubove, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico whose status was adjusted to that of a lawful permanent resident on December 14, 2000, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge dated May 29, 2013. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged under sections 237(a)(2)(A)(i) and 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(i), 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii). As the respondent did not apply for any form of relief, the Immigration Judge ordered his removal from this country. On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in denying him a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying him a continuance to collaterally attack the criminal convictions that form the basis for his removability. However, at no time did the respondent request a continuance before the Immigration Judge. At the final hearing on May 22, 2013, the respondent’s counsel stated that the respondent’s family had hired a defense attorney to file a motion to withdraw the plea on the criminal convictions (Tr. at 160). However, counsel conceded that the respondent was presently ineligible for any relief from removal, and did not request a continuance (Tr. at 160-61).
 
We decline to remand these removal proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider the evidence that the respondent has presented on appeal. Even though the respondent has presented evidence that, subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s decision in this matter, he filed a writ of habeas corpus with the Texas courts, his convictions remain final and effective for immigration purposes.2 See Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992) (“The possibility of a decision on any post-conviction motion that has been filed does not affect our finding that the respondent is deportable.”). Moreover, the respondent’s desire to seek post-conviction relief does not amount to “good cause” for a continuance. See Cabral v. Holder, 632 F.3d 886, 890 (5th Cir. 2011). Even though the respondent apparently believes that, in light of Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010), his criminal defense attorney provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel in 2002, the Supreme Court and the Texas Court of Criminal Appeals have concluded that Padilla is not entitled to retroactive application.3 See Chaidez v. United States, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013); Ex parte De Los Reyes, 392 S.W.3d 675 (Tex.Crim.App. 2013). “Padilla also does not suggest that immigration proceedings must be stayed while a Padilla-type claim is being pursued in post[-]conviction proceedings.” Milton v. Holder, 425 F.App’x. 407, 409 (5th Cir. 2011). Moreover, the likelihood of success on the respondent’s motion for post-conviction relief is speculative. Thus, inasmuch as the evidence offered on appeal does not establish that, if proceedings were remanded, the outcome of this case would likely be different, we conclude that the respondent has not established that a remand is warranted. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removal proceedings before the Immigration Judge in this matter were completed at the Rolling Plains Detention Center in Haskell, Texas (see OPPM No. 04-06). The Immigration Judge conducted the hearings there remotely from Dallas, Texas, via video teleconference pursuant to section 240(b)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act.

2

Insofar as the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in issuing a ruling because his convictions were not “final,” the fact that an alien may be pursuing post-conviction relief in the form of a collateral attack on a conviction in state criminal court does not affect its finality for federal immigration purposes. Furthermore, the respondent’s motion for post-conviction relief was apparently filed on June 25, 2013, after the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Resp. Brief, Tab C.

3

We acknowledge that there may be an independent and adequate ground under Texas state law upon which the respondent may be able to pursue his post-conviction claims. See Michigan v. Long, 463 U.S. 1032, 1040-1042 (1983). However, such grounds have not been articulated in the respondent’s appeal brief. See Claudio v. Holder, 601 F.3d 316, 319 (5th Cir. 2010) (holding that an alien’s appeal brief is the operative document through which any issues he wishes to have considered must be raised).

2013 WL 5872211 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE TENEZACA
File: AXXX XX4 184 - New York, NY
October 8, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrew Friedman, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated May 15, 2013, ordering his removal from the United States. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. Inasmuch as the respondent, through counsel, conceded that he is subject to removal from the United States because he is an alien who is present in this country without being admitted or paroled or who arrived at any time or place other than as designated by the Attorney General, the respondent’s removability has been established by clear and convincing evidence (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 18; Exh. 1). See section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.10(c), 1240.8(c). Moreover, the respondent does not raise any meaningful argument on appeal which would cause this Board to disturb the Immigration Judge’s holding that he is also subject to removal from the United States as a result of his May 2012 New York state conviction for Strangulation in the Second Degree (I.J. at 4-6; Exhs. 1, 2; Tr. at 18). See section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act.
 
As the respondent is subject to removal from the United States, it is his burden to establish eligibility for relief from removal. Section 240(c)(4)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). However, the respondent has not filed an application for any form of relief from removal which can be granted by an Immigration Judge or this Board. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.1(a)(1) (enumerating many of the forms of relief from removal which can be granted by an Immigration Judge). The respondent, through counsel, expressly declined the opportunity to file an Application for Asylum and for Withholding of Removal (Form I-589) because “[h]e doesn’t have any fear of returning to his country” (Tr. at 27; I.J. at 4).
 
To the extent that the respondent appears to be arguing that his counsel provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel by not filing a Form I-589, he has not complied with any of the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), nor established that he was prejudiced by his counsel’s decision not to file a Form I-589 in these proceedings.
 
*2 The respondent’s desire to seek post-conviction relief with the New York courts did not amount to “good cause” for a continuance (I.J. at 4-6). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 1240.6. The respondent has not presented evidence that he has actually filed for post-conviction relief, nor evidence that such claims will likely be successful. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980) (holding that the statements of counsel are not evidence); see also Heredia v. Holder, 516 F.App’x. 63 (2d Cir. 2013) (holding that an alien could not demonstrate that an Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his request for a continuance constituted an abuse of discretion under any standard so established because he failed to submit evidence of a pending motion to vacate); cf. Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009) (recognizing that a continuance may be warranted where an alien has presented evidence that he is the beneficiary of a pending immigrant visa petition and established a likelihood of success on an application for adjustment of status). Overall, considering the lack of evidence contained in the record to corroborate the claims of the respondent’s counsel, the speculative nature of post-conviction proceedings, and the amount of time that the respondent was provided to present his claims, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s request for a continuance.
 
The respondent has also not established that he was prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his request for a continuance. Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). Even in the absence of his aforementioned conviction, the respondent would still be subject to removal from the United States because he is an alien who is present in this country without being admitted or paroled or who arrived at any time or place other than as designated by the Attorney General. While the respondent apparently seeks cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1), he has not articulated any basis to hold that, in the absence of his disqualifying conviction, he would be able to establish that his removal from the United States would result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to a qualifying relative as required by section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act. Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 56, 65 (BIA 2001).
 
For the reasons set forth above, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to order the respondent removed from the United States to Ecuador. The following order is entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 5872215 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE IVAN MERAN-ALMONTE A.K.A. JOSE I. MERAN
File: AXXX XX8 879 - Newark, NJ
October 7, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anayancy R. Housman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joseph Silver
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s May 23, 2013, decision denying his motion to reconsider1 the Immigration Judge’s underlying April 23, 2013, decision denying his motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) urges affirmance of both of the Immigration Judge’s decisions. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that in a decision dated June 23, 2010, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed following a hearing conducted on an in absentia basis. It is undisputed that although the respondent’s former attorney (Cynthia N. Grob) appeared at that hearing, the respondent did not. With the assistance of new counsel (Robert Cygan) the respondent filed a motion to reopen on March 13, 2013 (which was subsequently supplemented by additional documentation filed April 4, 2013). The respondent sought reopening to rescind his in absentia order in light of his claim that he did not receive notice of his June 23, 2010, hearing. In addition, the respondent asserted that “““exceptional circumstances” accounted for his failure to appear at that proceeding due to the ineffective assistance provided by attorney Grob, who allegedly never notified him of his June 23, 2010, court date. Finally, the respondent sought reopening for adjustment of status consideration based on his contention that he was married to a United States citizen (USC), with whom he has two USC children.
 
In a decision dated April 23, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s March 13, 2013, motion to reopen, as supplemented. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had received actual notice of the hearing in question, as he was present at his February 25, 2010, hearing at which his next scheduled hearing for June 23, 2010, was announced, and for which notice was provided in writing. See April 23, 2013, I.J. dec. at 2-3; Notice of Hearing dated February 25, 2010.
 
As to the respondent’s claim of exceptional circumstances accounting for his failure to appear due to alleged ineffective assistance provided by attorney Grob, the Immigration Judge concluded that the evidence submitted was insufficient to satisfy the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Further, the Immigration Judge noted that even if the Lozada requirements were otherwise satisfied (given a previously-submitted affidavit by attorney Grob), the respondent’s motion to reopen to rescind based on his exceptional circumstances claim was filed years after his in absentia hearing had been completed. The Immigration Judge went on to conclude that equitable tolling of the time limit for filing the reopening request based on such claims did not apply, as the respondent did not exercise due diligence regarding these claims. See April 23, 2013, I.J. dec. at 3-4.
 
*2 In addition, the Immigration Judge did not sua sponte reopen the respondent’s proceedings for adjustment of status consideration. Ibid. at 4-5. The Immigration Judge noted that a prima facie showing of eligibility for such relief had not been made, as there was no indication that a visa petition filed in the respondent’s behalf had been approved. Also noted was the absence of any evidence confirming that the respondent was married, that his wife was a USC, and that he and “Olivo Almonte” (the named beneficiary of the visa petition that was filed) are one and the same person. The Immigration Judge did, however, specifically state “the Court may reconsider its position if additional evidence were provided.” Ibid. at 5.
 
In response, the respondent, with the assistance of attorney Cygan, filed a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Court (to which additional documentation was appended) in an effort to cure the noted evidentiary deficiencies of his previously-filed motion insofar as he sought reopening for adjustment of status consideration. See Motion filed May 3, 2013. On May 23, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied this motion for lack of a showing that the respondent is the beneficiary of an approved visa petition, and because the evidence submitted did not demonstrate that he and “Olivo J. Almonte” (as listed in the submitted evidence) are the same person.
 
We now turn to the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of both his March 13, 2013, motion to reopen (as supplemented), and his May 3, 2013, motion to reconsider (with additionally-submitted documentation). The respondent challenges these decisions by reiterating his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Grob (regarding whether he received notice his June 23, 2010, hearing), and asserting for the first time that he received ineffective assistance from attorney Cygan2 in connection with his motions to reopen and reconsider filed in Immigration Court.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
Regarding the denial of the respondent’s March 13, 2013, motion to reopen, as supplemented, we first observe that this filing is effectively three motions (i.e., a motion to reopen to rescind his in absentia order based on a claim of lack of notice, a motion to reopen to rescind his in absentia order based on a claim of exceptional circumstances/ineffective assistance by attorney Grob, and a motion for sua sponte reopening to apply for adjustment of status). Each of these motions is subject to different statutory and regulatory requirements.
 
As to the denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen to rescind his in absentia order based on a claim of lack of notice (for which there is no filing deadline), the respondent does not dispute (and has not addressed) on appeal the Immigration Judge’s findings that he received actual notice of his June 23, 2010, hearing, and that such notice was given orally and in writing at his February 25, 2010, hearing that both he and attorney Grob attended. See April 23, 2013, I.J. dec. at 2-3. Moreover, the respondent makes no claim that these findings are “clearly erroneous.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). Under the circumstances presented, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen to rescind the respondent’s in absentia order based on a lack of notice claim.
 
*3 Concerning the denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen seeking rescission of his in absentia order based on exceptional circumstances owing to alleged ineffective assistance by attorney Grob after his February 25, 2010, hearing, this motion (filed March 13, 2013, as supplemented) was untimely by more than 2-1/2 years. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (providing that such motions must be filed within 180 days of the order of removal).
 
Further, there was no showing below, and the respondent has not persuasively argued on appeal, that this 180-day motion to reopen filing deadline is equitably tolled, even assuming, without deciding, that such tolling is available under prevailing circuit law in cases such as this. The respondent admits that on June 24, 2010, he was advised by Immigration Court personnel that he had been ordered removed in absentia the previous day. He now reiterates that he “immediately consulted another attorney” after his in absentia order was entered. However, he has not explained what action he took to actively pursue his claims of ineffective assistance against attorney Grob in the years following that consultation.3
 
The fact remains that no motion to reopen seeking rescission of the respondent’s in absentia order was ever filed until April 23, 2013 (when attorney Cygan did so in Immigration Court), based on a claim of exceptional circumstances/ineffective assistance by attorney Grob. On this record, the respondent has not shown that he acted with due diligence in pursuing and presenting his claims of ineffective assistance by attorney Grob. Accordingly, his motion to reopen to rescind his in absentia order based on his claim of exceptional circumstances (accounting for his failure to appear at his June 23, 2010, hearing) was correctly denied by the Immigration Judge on this basis alone. As such, we need not address the remaining arguments concerning the alleged ineffective assistance provided by attorney Grob, and the alleged ineffective assistance by attorney Cygan insofar as the motion he filed rested on such claims.4
 
As to the respondent’s denial of sua sponte reopening for adjustment of status consideration,5 and notwithstanding the respondent’s arguments on appeal, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s resolution of this issue, as addressed in both his April 23, 2013, and May 23, 2013, decisions, based on the evidence then before him.
 
On appeal, the respondent challenges the Immigration Judge’s decisions in this regard based on claims of ineffective assistance by attorney Cygan. The respondent contends that attorney Cygan rendered ineffective assistance in filing his motions to reopen and to reconsider by not including certain documentation and evidence in support of his ultimate request for adjustment of status. See Brief at 7. He now provides supporting documentation of the type he claims should have been submitted by attorney Cygan (e.g., his personal attestation that his true and correct name is Olivo Jose Almonte, and other evidence and documentation confirming this point, and to demonstrate that he is the same person listed in previously-submitted documentation, including regarding the visa petition filed by his USC wife). Ibid. at 10, and Tabs A-F.
 
*4 Even if this newly-provided evidence had been submitted by attorney Cygan with the respondent’s motion to reopen and/or motion to reconsider filed in Immigration Court, this evidence still would have been insufficient to establish his prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status at that time as he did not, and indeed could not, demonstrate that the visa petition filed in his behalf by his USC wife had been approved. See April 23, 2010, I.J. dec. at 4. Thus, even assuming, without deciding, that the respondent’s current submissions demonstrate compliance with the procedural requirements for presenting an ineffective assistance claim against attorney Cygan based on the aforementioned contentions, he has not also established that “there exists a reasonable probability that the Immigration Judge may have granted [his] motion to reopen had it been adequately prepared.” Brief at 7.
 
We also decline to sua sponte reopen the respondent’s proceedings for adjustment of status consideration, as there has been no showing of exceptional circumstances warranting such action. 8 U.S.C. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). We also observe that the respondent has yet to establish that the immediate relative spousal visa petition filed by his USC wife has been approved. Therefore, he currently is unable to demonstrate prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status. We also observe that the visa petition in his behalf was not filed by his wife until March 22, 2013, more than 2-1/2 years after his final order of removal was entered on June 23, 2010.
 
Based on the foregoing considerations, the appeal is dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

He also entered an order (at 3) denying the respondent’s “motion to reopen of April 23, 2013.” See May 23, 2013, I.J. dec. at 3. It is readily apparent, however, that in so ordering the Immigration Judge mistakenly identified the date of the respondent’s motion to reopen. The record clearly reflects that the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed March 13, 2013, and was supplemented on April 4, 2013. The record also clearly reflects that the Immigration Judge denied this motion to reopen on April 23, 2013. The parties have not raised this matter on appeal. We view this Immigration Judge mistake to be harmless error.

2

On appeal, the respondent questions whether Mr. Cygan is an attorney. However, the record reflects that Mr. Cygan swore under penalty of perjury that he was an attorney and a member in good standing of the New Jersey bar, as evidenced by his Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before Immigration Court (Form EOIR-28), filed March 13, 2013, in Immigration Court. In any event, on this record there has been no showing that Mr. Cygan was not an attorney during the time he represented the respondent, or that he is not currently an attorney.

2013 WL 5872127 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SONIA KARINA PINEDA-ZEPEDA
File: AXXX XX0 138 - San Francisco, CA
September 27, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Monica N. Ganjoo, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on April 29, 2004, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen her proceedings and to rescind an in absentia order of removal. On July 18, 2013, over 9 years after the Board’s order, the respondent filed the current motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the Board’s decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). It is also number-barred, as it is the second motion to reopen filed in this case. Section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent argues that her proceedings should be reopened and the in absentia order should be rescinded because she did not receive her notice of March 11, 2003, hearing. There is no time limitation to a motion to reopen removal proceedings and rescind an in absentia order based on the lack of notice. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) and (c)(7)(C)(iii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3), 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). However, such motions are still subject to the number limitation. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (noting that an “alien may file only one motion” to reopen and rescind an in absentia order entered in removal proceedings). As noted above, the respondent’s motion is the second motion to reopen filed in her case, therefore it is number barred and will be denied.
 
Even if we were to consider the respondent’s argument that she did not receive the notice of hearing, we would find little merit in her claim. The record in this case indicates that the notice of the March 11, 2003, hearing was sent to the respondent by mail to her San Francisco, CA, address (Exh. 6), and there is no indication that this notice was returned undelivered. In addition, the record indicates that the Immigration Judge’s decision was also mailed to the same address at a separate time, and this document was not returned undelivered. The respondent admits in her motion and affidavit that this was her correct address and that she received the in absentia removal order from the Immigration Court at this address, but she now claims that she never received the Notice of Hearing (“NOH”) from the Immigration Court at this address (Motion at 2, 5; 13-15). However, we observe that the respondent did not claim that she did not receive the NOH from the Immigration Court in her prior motion to reopen or affidavit attached to that motion or on appeal, and only claimed in this prior motion and affidavit that her failure to appear at the hearing was due to her inability to understand English. Under these circumstances, while we acknowledge the respondent’s argument that she had an incentive to appear at her hearing to pursue an asylum claim, we would not find that the respondent has shown that the presumption of delivery has been rebutted, even if her motion were not number-barred. Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674-76 (BIA 2008); Matter of C-R-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 677 (BIA 2008).
 
*2 In the alternative, the respondent urges that the numerical limitations on motions to reopen and rescind an in absentia order should be equitably tolled because she is the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and 240(c)(7)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i) & (c)(7)(A) (setting forth time and number limits on motions to reopen and rescind in absentia orders of removal); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3) & 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (same); see also Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that equitable tolling can apply to the numerical limitations on motions to reopen).
 
First, the respondent has not substantially complied with the Lozada1 procedural requirements. See Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893 (9th Cir. 2008). While she asserts that she apprised prior counsel of the allegations lodged against him, the only evidence submitted in support of this assertion is a photocopy of a vague email purportedly to her prior counsel’s email address. We do not find this sufficient notice to her prior counsel. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 598-99 (9th Cir. 2004) (finding that alien did not comply with Lozada where he did not submit an affidavit and did not provide former counsel with an adequate opportunity to respond to allegation). Second, the respondent has not demonstrated that she acted with due diligence in pursuing her claim prior to filing the instant motion on July 18, 2013, over 9 years after the Board’s prior decision. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra, at 897. Third, the respondent has not demonstrated that she was prejudiced by her prior counsel’s actions or inactions. Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 153 (9th Cir. 1999) (finding that prejudice must be shown for a due process challenge and that prejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected outcome); see also Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004) (an alien can show prejudice where counsel’s performance “may have affected the outcome of the proceedings”). Here, the respondent seeks to apply for asylum and withholding of removal. However, she has not articulated a basis for her claim nor or any evidence to establish her prima facie eligibility for relief. Thus, the motion to reopen is untimely, number-barred, and the respondent has not shown due diligence or prejudice to warrant equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*3 Finally, although not explicitly requested, the respondent has not shown that an “exceptional situation” exists that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte, particularly given her lengthy criminal history. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that “““as a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Matter of Lozada, 19 I & N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), requires a successful ineffective assistance of counsel claim be accompanied by: “(1) an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement with former counsel concerning what action would be taken and what counsel did or did not represent in this regard; (2) proof that the alien notified former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance and allowed counsel an opportunity to respond; and (3) if a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities is claimed, a statement as to whether the alien filed a complaint with any disciplinary authority regarding counsel’s conduct and, if a complaint was not filed, an explanation for not doing so.”).
